JpORGETFUL of the lessons of history,
ignoring the teachings of science, bent
on gratifying their selfish desires and on
pursuing social advancement, they are
seeking to follow the dictates of a narrow
reasoning, and blindly imagine that, in
raising the standard of their own physical
comfort, they are smoothing the path of
life for themselves and for posterity, while
leaving to others the creation of that
posterity for which they profess to be so
concerned. —DR. JOHNSON’S GHOST, by the
mouth of N.S.W. Birthrate Commission, 1904.
THE RED PAGE.
" Follow My Leader."
(AS A GAME FOR ILLUSTRATORS. )
LOOKING through recently, with two or
three fellow-students, a fairly complete
collection of the published work of several
celebrated illustrators, we came to the
conclusion that they—in their comparatively early
work,at all events—had very completely borne out
a statement made by Kipling in a letter, part of
which was published in The Bulletin a few years
baok.
“We all steal from one another,” he wrote,
“it’s part of our business.” Now, although many
famous writers have freely confessed how much
they owe to brother authors, leading illustrators
apparently have been less open about that sort of
thing. After all, though, we must remember that,
in the case of the writers, they speak for them-
selves, whereas of the illustrators we are thinking
of—or two of them at least —those who inter-
viewed them speak for them.
FIG. I.— From Regnault's Animal Studies.
FIG VI.— By Du Maurier, in Punch, March
11, 1865.
There is a well-known passage by R.L.S. in
which he mentions his literary debts; and, by the
way, in Harper’s Magazine (June, 1888) there is
this sentiment from one of R.L.S.’s lettersNo-
body had ever such pains to learn a trade as I
had, but I slogged at it day in and day out, and I
frankly believe (thanks to itty dire industry) I
have done more with smaller gifts than almost
any man of letters in the world.” This same “dire
industry ” is characteristic of the four great illus-
trators we are especially discussing—Chas. Keene,
Linley Sambourne, Phil May, and Raven Hill.
And one takes it that a sentence in an article
about Mozart which appeared in the Sat. Review
lately is exactly applicable to them also: “Mozart
was the most completely original of all the com-
posers, and like all highly original men, he owed
much to his predecessors and contemporaries.”
And again : “ He mastered the technique of Bach,
of Haydn, of Gluck ; and in his glorious passionate
youth set to work to use for his own ends all that
he had learnt.” But why, we-wonder, are illus-
trators, who are as'completely original in their line
as Mozart was in his, so chary about acknowledging
how they have helped themselves by not only
looking at but also closely copying bits in the
wofk of t h e i r predecessors and contemporaries ?
*
Less reticence in the matter might be helpful to
younger workers “ Per-hkps—p’r’aps not ”
it is a difficult question that. Anyway, the
fact remains that Phil May used to talk—accord-
ing to more than one interviewer—of having
studied, but not copied, Caran d’Ache; but a
Sydney barrister, very keen in art matters, has
something to say on this head. His admiration
for May’s work is intense—he watched for it
before the brilliant „ artist’s Bulletin period—
and owns some pencil studies made for
various Bulletin drawings, which are simply
marvellous for their delicacy ; but he is not
blind to the fact that May’s memory was
playing him false when he told Arthur Lawrence
(Idler, Dec., ’96): “ I never, at any time in my
life, sat down—as I am afraid a good many young
fellows do now—to deliberately copy another
man’s work.” On the contrary, this collector’s
natural love of getting at evidence in questions of
this sort has resulted in his being able to point to
a case where May copied Caran d’Ache almost
line for line through a whole series of sketches.
This in 1886.
Six years later we find that splendid pen
draughtsman, Schlittgen—whose work in Flie-
gende Blatter is really a feature of that delightful
FIG. II.— By Phil May, in Pick
ME-UP.
FIG. VII. —By Linley Sambourte, in Punch, March 7, 1868.
little gaper—copying May just as closely. That
is, almost line for line. And we can hardly
suppose that “the bookshelves of Caran d’Ache
heave beneath the weight of English and
American unbound comic papers ” for the sake
of the work in said papers being merely looked at
now and again,
jg
. ■ ■ , . , _
, Brother instance of close copying of French

Marys little lamb used—-according to the Scotch
reciter—to follow her about most perseestently,
and it is in that same manner Raven Hill and the
other illustrators in question go to Nature ; but it
would appear they ate at the same time willing to
take help in so doing.
It. reminds one of what W. M. Hunt so insists
on in that splendid little book Talks About Art—
for, with all his constant advice about going to
Nature, we find sentences like this: “Copy
Albert Diirer and Hans Holbein for accuracy and
form. Then draw them from memory, and then
make them apart of yourself.” ... “I’ve told
you over and over again whose works to draw
—Michael Angelo, Raphael, Albert Diirer, Hans
Holbein, Mantegna. Get hold of something of
theirs ; hang it up in your room ; trace it, copy it,
draw it from memory over and over, until you
own it as you own ‘Casabianca’ and ‘Mary had
a little lamb.’ ”
But returning. In an interview (Idler, Sept.
’95) with the late Gleeson White, Raven Hill is
made to say much the same, as to Keene and him-
self, that May affirmed about Caran d’Ache and
himself. On second thoughts, it would probably
have been a case—re copying—of “What, never?
—well, hardly ever,” as warbled by the cap-
tain of H.M.S. Pinafore. And having thus got
round from Art, Music and Literature to
the Drama, perhaps we may add that it

isn’t altogether strange that to some collectors

MEMORANDA
Notes and Queries remarks that every word or dog has
its day, and this day of fiscal (in England). People
who a year since hardly knew of it now roll it on
their tongues like a confection. “ Not long ago the
female keeper of a registry office informed a customer
that the fiscal conditions of domestic service had en-
tirely changed.” Long, long ago a notorious Australian
embalmed the word in a concise election forecast—
“ More Popery than fiscal.” But he could n’t spell fiscal.
The tendenc3' of Lond. journalism is marked by an (un-
authoritative) announcement that The Daily Chronicle
will presently reduce its price to \d.
Some reproductions of etchings by Chas. Keene, in
Feb. Scribner's, are worth looking at. Spielmann as
critic seems to have uncommon flair, and no measure in
his judgment: his swans are swans, but he insists that
they are phoenixes, and all the time. In The Smart Set
mediocrity is nickel-plated...not heavily enough to de-
ceive. These minor Americans are perfect ladies and
elegant gentleman with a sawdust stuffing ; and even
the writers with a red corpuscle (King Charles has rested
quite a while) like Theodosia Garrison seem tamed to hide
it. “Felix Carmen” (F. D. Sherman) is seen squaring
the rhymer’s circle—
To Ver 1 sing—that is, to Spring
As Latin poets write it —
She comes with mirth and song to earth,
And scatters flowers to light it. . .
So, in these times of vernal rhymes,
I toss this verse to thrill Ver,
With, joy to see how nicely she
Supplies a rhyme for silver !
A large and interesting water-colour, by A. H. Fullwood,
“The Selector’s Home,” is shown in Beard, Watson and
Company’s window, George-street, Sydney. A typical
hut mellows in purple coastal atmosphere, and verisimili-
tude is carried as high as a decoy parrot on a sapling-top.
The result is quietly charming. The picture is being
subscribed for in shares—the winner to be decided bv
lot.
William Watson proses in an Amer. of “ The
State Discouragement of Literature ’’—although he re-
ceives a Govt, pension of 100 L “in consideration of his
merit as a poet.” A bit of his heart on his sleeve :
“ Thoughtful rfeaders, the true lovers of books .. . still
prefer a dignified style [like mine], a style that has breed-
ing'and a pedigree [from Matt. Arnold] ; they are still
content with the great decorum of a long-descended
[from Wordsworth] art ; they have not yet wearied of the
best. .. In regard to poetry especially, they have
the gbod sense to wave away the critic.. . with so little
love of the great old writers that he cannot bear a modern
[like me] who reminds him of them. It is not those
readers who slight the ritual and ceremonial of the muse
[and object to me embracing her in a dress suit and
starched collar]. It is not those who applaud a paraded
contempt for form and accomplishment." In other
words, since the difference between Wordsworth and
myself is rather in my favour, why will people persist in
praising one W. W. and appraising the other?
Some .years ago. an Eng. maga. printed a picture of
N.S.W. Govt/. House stables as Govt. House : and N.S.W.
tamely endured the affront. But in January last, when
The London Argus by mistake printed a picture purport-
ing to represent the Bath Hotel at Felixstowe, and’reall.v
representing somebody’s stables, the proprietor of the
hotel sued for libel and recovered damages.
Herbert Spencer’s will directed that, after cremation,
his ashes should be buried “ without any species of reli-
gious ceremony ” “in the unconsecrated part of the
Highgate cemetery,” where he had purchased a grave—a
proper and consistent protest against funeral mumblings
and mummeries and the prehistoric habit of the patter-
cove.
A.G.S.

JAMISON TOWN, N.S.W.
Dear Sirs, —There is nothing that gives
me greater pleasure than showing or
speaking of the great benefit I have re-
ceived from wearing one of your Belts.
For seventeen years I was a cripple, almost
a burden to my people, and a perfect
misery to myself, in fact I was what you
might call a useless man. I tried a good
many doctors, but got no benefit from
their treatment. My boys subscribed £5
for me and got me to Sydney, where I soon
got the Belt. I wore the Belt pretty regu-
larly for from five to seven hours each day
for about three months. My neighbors, like myself, were more than
surprised at the great good I was receiving from the use of the Belt.
I thank God my footsteps were directed to your Institute, for I am
positively a new man altogether. I remain,
Yours faithfully, CHARLES SHEPHERD.
WE GAN
GIVE YOU
The same Benefits these men and
thousands of others have received.
12 Lofts Avenue,
KEW, VIO.
Dear Sirs, —I must say that your wonder-
ful Belt is my best friend, for you know
when I came to you first I was looking
miserable and worn, but I can now say that
I have worn your Belt for three months
and it has improved me wonderfully. I
eat, sleep, and work better, and I find my
life a pleasure. I find your little book
speaks the truth, as your Belt has cured
me, and I am doing my best to let every-
one of my friends know of my great cure.
As for leaving it off, as you were saying
m
some people do, I have not left my Belt off for one night since I got
it, and Ido not mean to for some time yet. I hope every person
with a Lame Back as I had will take my advice and get one of your
Belts, for they speak for themselves more than anybody can.
Yours etc., A. E. PAGRAM.

Various Verses,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sirona.
There is a story told
About a giant
Who in a tower old
Lived care-defiant.
He had no child or wife
To love with passion,
And yet he lived his life
In joyous fashion.
Th ,i y say that he was blind,
With one gift only—
The gift to hear the wind
Singing songs lonely.
He had the finer ear
That feels dawn glowrng
Through darkness, and can hear
The green grass growing.
Ere rosy-footed Spring
Brought golden hours,
He heard the murmuring
• Of coming flowers.
He lived afar from man
The tale supposes;
Up his grey tower ran
A rhyme of roses.
And, twining through and through
Porches and arches,
Nasturtium-trumpets blew
Bold battle-marches.
Once I was, for high cheer,
On sight reliant,
Now songs august I hear,
Like that blind giant.
You brought the Golden Key,
And have displayed a
New World of Dreams to me—
Enchanting Ada !
N.S.W,
VICTOR J. DALEY.
The Victim.
What was there to love? The strong brown arm,
And a certain smile in the eager eyes.
Does a butterfly wantoning deem it harm ?
If we charm not when we have power to charm,
The gods are lenient to lovers’ lies.
But I thought I only should make believe,
I would pass his time as I passed my own,
And if one of the twain were left to grieve,
It would not be I, for no day should leave
Regret to me that its hours had flown !
I said in my heart: “ ’Tis pleasant weather,
The wattle is fair on the topmost range.
If we find it sweeter to stand together,
Shall remorse outweigh by a single feather
The wish I harbor for love—and change?”
But now that the arms no more are tender,
And the hoof-beats dull into distance grey,
Shall I be for my sin my own defender ?
A woman must wait for what Fate shall send her,
And —how you smiled as you rode away !
You smiled, and I had no answering laughter.
Were you the victim, or I the toy?
I had played that there be no losing after,
Yet I won tears and you won laughter.
Ah ! how you smiled as you rode a way !
Q-
M. FORREST.
Par Away.
Far away, oh, far away !
Where the golden morning breaketh
Over purple summer seas,
Rippling, sparkling, in the bay,
Splashing softly on the strand—
On the shining, yellow sand;
When the reign of Summer waketh
In that distant summer land
Far away.
Far away, oh, far away !
Where the dusty road winds down,
Past where oak and willow trees
Dream throughout the drowsy day.
Where the snowy orchards lie,
Bridal-robed ’neath azure sky,
Winding on towards the town
Where the swift-winged sea-birds fly
Far away.
Far away, oh, far away !
0 ! the dainty garden bowers
With the great, sweet, yellow roses,
Sweeter than the scent of May.
O, the little sunny town !
O, the little white roofed town,
Sleeping through the quiet hours
By the hillside green and brown,
Far away.
Faraway, oh, faraway !
Setting sun behind the hill,
Flinging shadows dark and vast
On the waters of the bay,
While the wavelets kiss the shore,
Coming, going ever more;
Where the dusk is cool and still,
And the stars light heaven’s floor,
Far away.
I shall never see it more.
I have left it all behind
On the weary round of time;
Never see the shining shore.
Nor the road that windeth down
From the hillside to the town ;
Nor the scented evening wind
Swing the roses as of yore,
Never more.
VIC.
KATHLEEN WALKEY.

Tfie Finger Post to Health
This is an Interesting Story
for Liver Sufferers.
The case of
Mr. G. HEATHCOTE.
(by a special reporter.)
Sihce enlightened humanity requires that
the trials incidental to sickness should be
minimised wherever possible, and since by
kindliness and consideration much can be
done in that direction, a reporter called
upon Mr. George Heathcote, of Surry-
street, Waterloo, who willingly assented
to answer any questions.
“ Were you once a resident of Bunda-
berg, Queensland ? ” asked the pressman.
“Yes,” answered Mr. Heathcote. “I
was living there when it was necessary to
cross the bridge by boat instead of walking
over it, or, to be more explicit, during the
time of the great flood, and it was during
that exciting and disastrous period that my
blood was so impoverished through exces-
sively hard work for long hours at a stretch
that sores broke out on my hands and arms,
and my legs swelled terribly. Previous to
this I had suffered from fever and ague,
which left me very weak and shaky, and
for some weeks I noticed that my appetite
was falling off ; but I put that down to
fatigue more than anything else, until other
symptoms cropped up to show me it was
not so, but my liver and kidneys which
were responsible.”
“ What reasons were there to enable you
to arrive at that conclusion ? ”
“ Any number of them, one of the most
unmistakable being a nasty pain which
caught me here (Mr. Heathcote indicated
the part affected by placing his hand on the
small of his back). My back ached so in-
tensely that my wife made flannel belts for
me to wear, thinking that they would afford
me some relief. If I was doing anything
which caused me to bend down, the pain on
straightening myself was something terrible,
while walking also provoked great distress
and inconvenience. Other proof that my
kidneys were in a bad way was also in
evidence, and as for the rest of my ail-
ments—well, I hardly know where to begin
first. ”
“ Tell me a few of the most important—
those that are uppermost in your mind ? ”
“ Oh ! I have not forgotten any of them,”
was the quick response, “ especially the
heavy feelings in my chest, which made me
short-winded. After eating, a weighty sen-
sation appeared in that quarter, and before
I knew where I was my stomach began
swelling out with wind, which made me
very uncomfortable always, and at times
gave rise to sharp, cutting pains. The
sour gases gradually worked their way up,
and caused ine to belch, after which, and
until I took a little more food, I felt
slightly easier, but the same unpleasantness
followed each meal, however trifling. When
making my morning preparations for work
I used to feel as if I would like to go back
to bed again, as I was entirely devoid of
energy, and had no desire to attempt any-
thing. This languidness was due, no doubt,
to the restlessness every nig it. I could go
to bed feeling so drowsy that my eyes
seemed swollen for the want of rest, yet I
found myself awake the greater part of the
night, and when I got up my limbs were
all aches. Nearly every day I was dis-
tracted with intense headaches-, which ap-
peared to bring a mist before tny eyes, as
if my sight was failing me, and without any
warning my head became so giddy that I
had to take a rest till the sensation passed
away. Every month I was getting weaker
and more wretched, being continually be-
set w ith pains under my shoulder-blades,
which travelled right through to the heart,
and caused me considerable anxiety. The
white coating on my tongue explained the
unhealthy condition of my stomach, too,
and what I wanted was a real good medicine
to clear away all disorders and impurities ;
but in those days I did not know as much
as I do now, or I would have been out of
my troubles very quickly. I went from
bad to worse, and had arrived at that
melancholy and desperate stage when the
least and most simple thing excites a feeling
of irritation, and I am sure you will admit
that such a life of misery is not worth en-
during. Had I gone on a little longer in
that sorry plight I question whether I
would have been as sound a man as I am
to-day, for I was fairly in the grip of my
complaint, when, owing to the recollection
of some conversations between a few of my
friends, 1 hit upon the idea of taking some
Clements Tonic. I took immediate action,
as I firmly believed that further delay
would have brought irretrievable disaster
to my constitution.”
“ Your good fortune came at a very ac-
ceptable time, I should imagine ? ”
“ Yes ; and a very critical time, too, and
I regretted that I had not bought a few
bottles of Clements Tonic at the outset;
but we all have to buy our experience, you
know, although I intend doing all I can to
help other sufferers by letting them know
what Clements Tonic can do. Personally,
I can truthfully say that it generally re-
vived me from my lethargic state when I
had drank but a small quantity, and I found
myself rapidly improving. Clements Tonic
had a magical effect on my kidneys and
greatly relieved the pains about my back
and shoulders. This relief, coupled wi' h
the abatement of my headaches, had the
effect of making me feel more restful at
nights, and the sound sleep I got through
using Clements Tonic did me an immense
amount of good. My slumber was not in-
terfered with by dreams, and when I awoke
at the usual time for getting up I arose with
alacrity and hastened to my morning meal,
after which I was ready for work. It was
a marvellous change to be full of energy
and cheerfulness again, but the invigorating
properties of Clements Tonic were so re-
markable that I could not have restrained
my feelings of joy had I wanted to. Wind
never formed in my stomach after Clements
Tonic got to work, neither did my chest
trouble me, while in the matter of palpita-
tion the same medicine was equal to the
prevailing requirements. There is no
denying the fact that Clements Tonic
was wonderfully effective as a blood
purifier, for the sores were gradually
effaced from my hands and arms, and the
cause of my legs swelling—whatever that
cause might have been —was entirely re-
moved. My illness had considerably
affected my nerves, which became shaky
whenever I was disturbed, but to my heart-
felt thankfulness I must say that Clements
Tonic was as effective on my nervous
system as it was on my other deranged
organs.”
“ What was your appetite like then ? ”
“As it was soon after commencing on
Clements Tonic—very good, indeed, and it
has remained so ever since. But the
strengthening qualities of my newly-found
medicine surprised me, for although my
work was very heavy I always got through
it without feeling any ill effects from the
time Clements Tonic cured me, and I have
not once had the faintest return of any of
my old symptoms, in acknowledgment of
which I give you permission to publish my
story as it pleases you.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Georob Heathcotb, of Surry-street, Water-
loo, in the State of New South Wales,
Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that I have carefully read
the annexed document, consisting of five
folios, and consecutively numbered from one
to five, and that it contains and is a true and faithful
account of my illness and cure by Clements Tonio,
and also contains my full permission to publish in any
way my statements—which I give voluntarily,
without receiving any payment; and I make this
solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of
the Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Waterloo, mis nine-
teenth day of April, one thousand nine hundred and
two, before me, *
B. G. CAMPBELL, J.P.

Love Lyrics.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Gods and the Girls.
I There’s a toast that has waited proposing
Since the first wine was pressed from the grape ;
I ’Tis a toast better far than the prosing
I Of the King and the Crown and the Crape !
I There’s a pledge fit for men waits a drinking—
I ’Tis a toast set with bright eyes and curls;
I Set the hearts and the glass-rims a-clinking—
Fill them up I— To the Gods and the Girls /
’Tis a toast for a man’s heart to cherish,
’Tis a pledge for a true soul to sing ;
It’s a toast good to drink till we perish
And the arms cease to clasp and to cling 1
’Tis a toast for the strong and the loyal—
Who refuse it are outcasts and churls;
’Tis a hail to the ones truly royal—
So we drink to the Gods and the Girls.
Set a foot on the chair and the table,
Bring the best spidered wine in the bins ;
Now a cheer that shall half lift the gable—
Thank the Gods one and all for our sins !
For we owe them to Bacchus and Cupid—
They invented our vices, our pearls ;
Man without them is muddy and stupid—
Ho ! we drink to the Gods and the Girls !
’Tis a toast to the twin founts of pleasure—
A libation to Jove and to Love;
’Tis a toast for the vine’s purplest measure,
Poured out to the good Gods above.
He who drinks not is base and a varlet,
So we drain to Sweet Life as it whirls ;
Fill them now with a liquor of scarlet—
Long life to the Gods —and the Girls !

GRANT HERVEY.
White Clover.
White clover, white clover,
In sunshine a-growing ;
White olover, white clover,
My tears are fast flowing. . .
Dear God, is all over ?
(Oh tell me, white clover)
Through green grasses showing.
White clover, white clover,
Sweet fragrance a-flinging;
White clover, white clover,
Such memories bringing 1
** Oh, Love is a rover—
Love dieth in clover ! ”
Those wild birds are singing. . .
White clover, white clover,
With glad bees a-humming ;
White clover, white clover,
Just breathe—ls he coming ?
My dear and my lover
(Oh, tell me, white clover) —
What silence!—benumbing.
vio.
DOROTHY McCRAE.
Blue-Eyes.
Do you remember long ago,
Blue-Eyes ?
We watched the Cook Strait rollers go
Into the East, where, all aglow,
The red moon swung so proud and slow
Into the rich, blue skies.
And we spoke softly, love—so low —
Do you remember long ago ?
Do you forget, Blue-Eyes ?
Do you remember how I swore,
Blue Eyes,
Tiiat I would love you evermore, *?
While there were stars on ocean’s floor—
While there were breakers on the shore.
To rush and roar and rise ?
Did you remember all I swore
And wonder why I promised, or
Did you forget, Blue-Eyes ?
I can remember nothing else,
Blue-Eyes,
But those sweet days by foam-flecked swells,
When we went gathering fluted shells,
Or dreamed *mid flowers in sunny dells
And heard the sea-gulls’ cries.
And, deep within me, something tells
That you, remembering nothing else,
Hare not forgot, Blue-Eyes.
We watched the seas together, Ah 1
Blue-Eyes ?
We saw the ships go driving far
Out where the restless currents are,
Yet something seemed my joy to mar—
I felt it throb and rise
In my hot heart; and then a star
Beckoned, and I went sea-ward ; Ah I
Do you forget, Blue-Eyes ?
Would you forgive me if I came,
Blue-Eyes,
And called you by that old, old name ?
Could I rekindle Love’s red flame ?
Would you be just the very same—
So gay and yet so wise ?
If I took all the bitter blame
Would you forgive me if I came ?
Have you forgot, Blue-Eyes ?
Do you remember ? Whisper low,
Blue-Eyes.
It seems so very long ago,
Dear, since we watched the rollers go
And heard the angry breakers throw
Their gray plumes to the skies . . .
It is so sweet, dear love, to know
That you remember long ago—
So long ago, Blue-Eyes.
AI.L.
WILL LAWSON.

THE NEW POLITICS.

THURSDAY, MARCH 17, 1904.
The Defence Forces.
The Australian “defence” system takes the
opportunity of an outburst of war not far from
the back door of the Commonwealth to advertise
ts absurdity and proclaim its impotence. The
review held, the other day, at Hobart, when
there was a gaudy general, a gorgeous staff, and
some purple language, but no soldiery, was in one
sense an accident. But, nevertheless, it was an
epitomised description of the actual state of the
Australian military organisation. There is a
finely-clothed and eloquent general, a staff,
almost as beautiful as regards its clothes if
not its language; beyond them, nothing worth
speaking of—few men, no guns, a pinched
supply of rifles, mostly Britain’s obsolete
and cast-off weapons, and no adequate means
of manufacturing either rifles or cartridges. If
an enemy were to throw an invading force into
Australia, practically the only means of warding
off the attack would be to mobilise General
Hutton and launch him, armed with adjectives,
against the foe. If he failed to drive them back,
it is difficult to say what resource would be left to
the Commonwealth. In all probability, too, he
would fail, if for no other reason than the painful
ignorance of the average foreigner as to the
beauties of the English language.
* * * *
Yet the booming of guns in the China Sea
must awaken any Australian with a mind that can
climb above the cricket level to an appreciation of
the fact that a sense of security which goes so far
as to lead to neglect of the simplest precautions
against attack is no sense at all. By an expensive
naval tribute arrangement with Britain Australia
pays a share of tbecostof defending “the Empire, ”
but makes no provision for defending its own coun-
try. Byanexpensivemilitarysystemrunon Empire
lines under the advice of an Imperial officer, the
Commonwealth keeps in comfortable positions
certain imported and home-grown personages,
whose uniforms lend a brightness to social
functions, but is left without a real army of any
sort. There are men banded together in a Per-
manent Force, in “ Partially Paid ” and Volunteer
regiments, but they aro practically without arms,
and their chief occupation seems to be grumbling
at the personages before mentioned, or being
grumbled at by them. In one respect, and in one
respect alone, is the Australian military system
complete, and that is in regulations. There are
enough regulations in existence to run half-a-
dozen Napoleonic campaigns, and whenever there
is a complaint as to the efficiency of the military
machine a fresh regulation is drafted, by which
the D.A.M.F. is to report to the A.G.C.L.F.
through the D.A.G., instead of through the
C.O.D.F. as before.
• • • •
Seriously, Australia is for all practical purposes
defenceless. And, defenceless as it is, it has
national aspirations and enacts exclusive legisla-
tion which may at any time bring it into conflict
with an armed enemy. Yet, supposing the present
naval and military muddle to continue until a war-
crisis came, it is difficult to see what attitude
Australia could take but a Chinese and prostrate
one. There would be no use in rushing against
modern rifles with road metal. The days are
dead vvhen a bill-hook or a stave with a stout
heart urging It on was a military weapon to be
seriously reckoned with. Supposing Australia
to possess a modern small-arms factory and
enough powder mills, it would have some
hope in a fight. With the fleet that it pays
for fighting someone else’s battles away in a
far sea, with its great cities in ruins for want of
protecting squadrons and batteries, it could still
fall back on its fields and hold them against the
wave of invasion. But even for that desperate
game there are no cards on the table. Under
present conditions Australia could not even
undertake a guerilla defence.
* * * *
It is a scandalous reflection on the national
Parliament that such should be the state of affairs;
no task could better claim the immediate atten-
tion of an Australian Ministry than the founding
of the elements of a defence system in the Com-
monwealth. To effect that there are necessary
three steps :
1. The establishment of a small arms factory, an
arsenal capable of turning out large guns, and ammunition
factories. (This is put as the first step because the main
essential is to get guns. Even a mob with guns would be
of some use ; an army, however nicely drilled, beauti-
fully dressed and vilely hair-oiled, without guns, would
b» of no use at all.)
2. A law to make every healthy male adult undergo
(without pay) the rudiments of military training, and to
encourage (with pay) a certain proportion of citizens to
train for the more technical tasks of a modern army.
3. The extension of the Undesirable Immigrants Act to
all military instructors from abroad, especially British
officers.
The programme contains somi contentious
matter, but it is the minimum needed for effective
reform. An army must have guns, and it must
make its guns in its own country, since it will not
be able to import them Yvhen they are most
needed. An army must have men, and in the
case of a small people inhabiting a large land,
every man must be enrolled to do his share in
preparing for the national defence. An army
must have a sensible, effective and economical
system of administration, and therefore must
keep at the farthest possible distance all British
instructors, for Britain, of all countries on earth,
muddles its army administration most abomin-
ably. The officer who is trained under system
which costs so much in clothes, polo, afternoon
tea, private theatricals, horse-racing, &c., that
only a rich man can be an officer, is of no use to a
country like this. Indeed it might almost
be hazarded that if the practice of import-
ing military commanders and instructors from
England wore abolished, all the other neces-
sary reforms would follow in due course.
What Australia does not want is an army
modelled on that of Great Britain. The latter
country maintains military forces chiefly for the
entertainment of lordlings, snobs and rich fools
who, without an army to play the fool with, would
be lonesome and would be always crying for their
mothers. As the British titled matron must go
into society, the army has been organised to keep
her cubs amused. Since the British people are
rich, and have a gigantic reputation as fighters in
the past to fall back upon, an army of the sort
that country indulges in is not immediately fatal.
Like Freetrade, it can be endured for a while.
But Australia is not rich, and has no great
fighting reputation to point to when a foe
threatens trouble. Consequently Australia wants
an army which shall be cheap, effective, and in
its organisation democratic. All these things are
utterly opposed to the typical British officer’s
notions, and therefore the British officer must be
kept as far away from the Australian army as
possible, if the necessary Whole Hog reform is
to be carried out.
* * * *
Whole Hog the reform would need to be to
change the present Australian muddle-and-cuss
into an army. The greater portion of the very
large sum now devoted to military purposes is
absorbed by “ staff officers.” Most of these are
something worse than unnecessary. A few mili-
tary instructors are required ; a few engineering
and electrical experts; beyond them, the
“staff officer” is a nuisance. An Australian
officer against whom allegations of misconduct
were understood to have been made wished to
ask for an inquiry to clear himself. He was an
officer of years standing, and a lawyer to boot,
but, after devoting some weeks to the task, he was
unable to find out how it was possible for him to
acquaint the proper people in the proper way
with his very proper desire. It was not the right
thing for him to write directly to the Minister for
Defence, to the Federal Commandant, to the
N.S.W. Commandant, or to the officer command-
ing his regiment. (It is practically never the
right thing to write directly to anyone under the
British army system.) The aggrieved individual
made several attempts to let the military people
know that he Yvished for an inquiry, but
all bis letters were sent back to him—
because of some informality. He asked how
he should proceed, and was told to “proceed
in proper form,” but he mever did manage to
learn the proper form. Another military man
wished to draw attention to the conduct of an
officer who was suspected of malversation. He
consulted every possible book on the subject and
several staff officers, and then found that his com-
munication was informal. And the British mili-
tary personage who reads a letter which has not
reached him quite formally is incapable of taking
any notice thereof. There is a tradition that an
English officer in India once received a telegram
from the regimental surgeon telling him that his
wife “ on the hills” had smallpox, and asking him
to send up a special doctor and nurse. But, as
the communication had not been sent through the
D.A.S.M.G. or some such person, the recipient
simply couldn’t take any notice of it. When,
later, he heard of his wife’s death from lack of
proper care, the message came through a non-
military source, and he was able, therefore, to
shoot himself promptly in non-official remorse.
The Indian story may not be true ; the Australian
instances are. That’s the work for which “staff
officers ’ are kept—that and a mean sort of spying
called confidential reporting, and general back-
biting and intrigue. The whole system of circum-
locution and of spying needs to be swept away,
partly because it is useless and expensive, partly
because it is mischievous.
• • * *
On citizen lines, without gold lace, feathers or
other flummery, with a general conscription
limited as to its time demand, with no snobbery,
no circumlocution and no British commanding
officer, an Australian army would not be
particularly costly, and it might be made
highly effective. There is excellent fighting
material in Australia—especially in the country
districts—and in many cases a very small amount
of instruction would convert the citizen into a
fighting man. The man who is a good shot and
a good horseman has gone most of the way to be-
come a good soldier. A great deal of the fight-
ing material is not available now because an Aus-
tralian of the right type hesitates to put himself
under the discipline of the present contemptible
military organisation; and what material is ottered
is made the worst possible use of. But a citizen
soldiery, sternly disciplined, but governed without
class snobbery, organised on the lines adopted by
the French Republic after the Revolution, would
attract the bush worker, the miner, and, to
some extent, the city mechanic and laborer, "’here
the present system seems to mainly draiv the snob
and the city loafer anxious to parade the bars in a
gaudy uniform. If in the course of events the Aus-
tralasian Government is re-organised on Radical
lines, The Bulletin hopes to see it grapple with
this defence question seriously. The Federal
Labor party showed that it bad the right grasp
of the position during the life of the last Parlia-
ment, and to any Government in which it shares
control might be Yvell entrusted the task of reform
—a reform running on three main lines : Aus-
tralian manufacture of Australian arms, universal
liability to service, and the training of the army
on Australian lines by Australian officers.
Certain Tory Doctors, Some Facts,
and a Lie.
“ To make the people breed you must sweat the
workers.” That is the one sociological conclusion
to which the N.S.W. Birth-rate Commission
triumphantly comes. On matters of medicine, on
statistical subjects, its conclusions and suggestions
are sound enough. It is when it strays from the
domain of exact fact to seek by induction the
ultimate cause of the prevention evil that it comes
to the curious decision that trades-unionism is
responsible. Passing over the truths that it might
have noticed, it flaunts that huge fallacy as
the result of its observation and reasoning.
A blind and stupid Toryism seems to have
dominated the majority of the Commissioners
where the inquiry touched upon the “ political ”
side, and to have prevented them from coming to
an accurate judgment of causes and remedies.
Not only in what it omits is the report faulty,
but in the remarkable falsehood which it parades
as a scientific conclusion. An astonishing thing
is that such a report should be put before the
public as a unanimous one. From gentlemen
such as Messrs. M’Laurin, Mackellar, Knox,
Littlejohn, Fosbery and Foreman a reasonable
judgment on a subject which is partly economic
and partly social was not to be expected. Some
of them are provided with a capacity to judge,
others palpably not, but all are so mentally
warped by stupid and unreasoning Tory prejudice
that their opinion on any sociological issue is
valueless. It is not, therefore, altogether sur-
prising to find those gentlemen committing them-
selves to such a statement as that trades-unionism
is responsible for a dwindling birth-rate in N.S.W.
But there were others on the Commission.
• * * *
Dr. Nash has, in the N.S.W. Upper House,
shown himself a Liberal, almost a Radical. Mr.
Holman, M.P., is a member of the Labor party.
Mr. T. A. Coghlan, as a State servant, can have
no public opinions on politics, but is too intelli-
gent and Yvell-informed a man to be a Tory.
From Mr. Hughes, ex-Mayor of Sydney, and
Dr. Paton, Government Medical Officer, some-
thing better was to be expected than an acqui-
escence in. the pitiably silly fiction that trades-
unionism is one of the origins of the social
evil which the dwindling birth-rate proves.
Why is there no minority report from these
gentlemen—no repudiation of an allegation
which is exactly contrary to whatever evidence
can be obtained on the point ? In the absence of
such a minority report they must take the re-
sponsibility of approving what is made public
under their names. It, is a heavy burden of
ignorance and prejudice to stagger under.
* * * *
The sentence in which the Commission ex-
presses its sentiments on the connection between
democracy and the birth-rate is not very distinctly
phrased ; if what has leaked out as to the pro-
ceedings of the commission is correct, it represents,
in a form modified from its primal savagery, the
doctrine of the prime old Tories (one gentleman
boasts that he would “ have rubbed it in much
more strongly ” if he had been allowed his own
way); but its meaning is clear enough :
Legislative regulation of the conditions of employment
has not, as yet, as far as can be traced by statistics, had
any tangible influence upon the birth-rate; but we
cannot overlook the fact that restrictive regulations of
trade, designed to abolish competition, effectually inter-
fere with the continuity of employment in many walks of
life, so that the income of those employed is more or less
precarious, and this cannot fail to indirectly discourage
the existence of large families, and the natural increase
of population which would ensue therefrom.
There ignorance and class prejudice speak,
and speak most clumsily. The accusation that
trades-unionism (“ restrictive regulations of trade
designed to interfere with the continuity,” etc.)
is responsible for the decline of the birth-rate is
put spitefully, and phrased so as to include a lie
within a lie ; for that trades-unionism “effectually
interferes with the continuity of employment ” is
just as much a falsehood as that the decline of
the N.S.W. birth-rate can be traced to any
interruption of the continuity of employment.
Not satisfied with that, the Tories seek to make
a vicious inference with the statement that as yet
such legislation as the Arbitration Act and the
Factories Act has not had any detrimental effect
on the birth-rate, as if it were practically certain
that the detrimental effect would arrive in the
near future.
* * * *
If the Commission had been capable of coming
to an honest conclusion on the facts before it, its
verdict as to the social result of the efforts of the
workers to improve their lot by combination would
have been exactly otherwise. There is no doubt
that trades-unionism, by shortening hours, by
raising wages, by insisting on sanitary comfort in
workshops, by discouraging the employment of
immature children at work which would stunt
their growth, adds materially to the productive
capacity of the population—makes the large and
healthy family more possible. It may be that
when trades-unionism has done that much, other
causes then fail, and the couple who through
trades-unionism have been protected from stunt-
ing in their youth, and are now' secured a
greater degree of comfort than would be
otherwise possible to them, do not add to the
population as they might. But that implies
nothing against trades-unionism, which has at
least taken them the long step of making a family
decently possible. Something of that truth peeps
out even in this Tory report when it is admitted
that the regulation of the hours and conditions of
female factory labor has bad a beneficial effect.
And when the members of the commission pro-
ceeded to suggest medical remedies for infant
mortality the Tory doctors, in the extent of their
medical knowledge, forgot their political ignorance
and actually recommended further State socialism
—in the direction of State education of mothers by
posters and pamphlets, State education of girl
children in the care of infants, State control of the
sale of infant foods, municipal socialism in the
provision of milk depots, and extension of State
hospitals and foundling asylums, and the State
system of boarding-out poor infants. I* v,out
when it wandered away from medical points a do
which it was well-informed, to probe into
problems of social philosophy, about which t
majority of its members were either ignorant,
prejudiced, or both, that the commission blu -
dered. The Tory majority would probably ha\
blamed trades-unionism and Labor legislation 1
it had been asked for an opinion on the causes ?
the bubonic plague. A scapegoat was thought to
be necessary, and, to a certain class, the Labor
party is scapegoat-in-ordinary. Some lories
blame the Labor party when their aged finders
fumble o\ er-long at their collar-stud in the morning.
» * » *
A truthful impeachment, if it is sought to
probe into the causes of the prevention
habit, would have to be directed, not against the
masses of the people, but the classes, the well-to-
do, going-into-society folk—the folk of the class
to which the majority of the Birth-rate Commis-
sion belonged. It is they who have set the fashion
in prevention, without having available as an
excuse that narrowness of means makes large
families inconvenient. It is against them that an
accusation of promoting race-suicide should be
launched. This fact is shown clearly enough by
the statistics embodied in the report. Paragraph
64 reads:—
The statistics collected from the information obtained
from the registrations of deaths during the quinquennial
period 1898-1902 afford the following comparison of the
respective average issue of marriages to the
occupations of the husbands who died within that
period:
In the domestic occupations (grooms, married
couples, etc.) .. 4"32
~ professional 4*59
~ commercial .. .. .. .. .. 4*70
~ industrial b'27
~ agricultural .. 6'28
These figures represent the average number of children
born to women, the great majority of whom were married
before there was any marked decline in the birth-rate ;
and, therefore, they do not indicate the expected average
issue of present-day marriages. Nevertheless, the Com-
mission have reason to believe that the proportionate
differences which the figures disclose will be maintained.
These differences in the average issue of the various
classes enumerated may be accounted f®r to a slight
extent b}’ difference in age at marriage.
The class which is least prolific is that engaged
in domestic occupations i.e., grooms, cooks,
station married couples, butlers, and so on. This
class is made up almost exclusively of the victims
of the well-to-do, who are forced to be sterile so
that their brats may not come between the wind
and the nobility of the rich. The average squatter
bans the baby on his run ; the married couples
that he employs must be “ without encum-
brances.” The fashionable rich in the cities
refuse all possibility of family life to their
servants, who must also be “without encum-
brances.” Next to this class on the list
of those parsimonious with children came the
professional people—the Maclaurins and Mac-
kellars and Foremans and the commer-
cial people. These are far less prolific than
the workers—the trades-union class to which the
Maclaurins and the Mackellars
charge the offence of which their own class is
guilty. The artisan and the agricultural worker
are, in respect of parentage, the most patriotic and
moral of all. Had the N.S.W. Birth-rate Com-
mission, after ascertaining the facts, progressed
from sound medical conclusions to honest socio-
logical inductions its report would have had much
influence for good. As it is, the blind Tory
stupidity shown in some portions, will probably
invalidate in the popular mind what there is of it
that is well-reasoned and truthful.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Abolishing 1 the Upper House.
Premier Seddon, of Maoriland, who has done
nothing remarkable for some little time, announces
his intention to get rid of the Upper House of his
State Legislature. Seddon may not be quite the
Democrat he was, but there is still the material of
a very sturdy and progressive Democrat about
Richard of Kumara, and his resolve to simplify the
needlessly bloated and cumbersome processes of
legislation is most commendable. Anything that
simplifies the processes of legislation, and abolishes
useless relics, excrescences, warts, frills, survivals,
and silly remnants, represents progress. Upper
Houses have long been recognised by all intelli-
gent persons as the superfluous lingering of an old
disused remains or misfortune. They exist in
many outlying parts of the British Empire because
Britain has an Upper House ; also there are
Upper Houses in many foreign countries solely in
imitation of the “Mother of Parliaments” at
Westminster. Yet Britain never deliberately
created its Upper House, and never went in
deliberate search for one. That body exists just
as a wart does—because it happened. England
has had a Parliament of one House, and also one
of four Houses ; it was the most casual kind of a
toss-up that prevented it finally settling down
with a Parliament of three Houses. Its bi-
cameral Legislature and its hideously incompre-
hensible table of weights and measures are alike
things of accident. The House of Lords does not
exist because it is supposed to do any good in the
way of legislation, or to be of any benefit
whatsoever to the people, but simply because an
hereditary nobleman is supposed to have an
hereditary and inherent right to legislate,
even though he may not be able to legislate
worth a copper. If Britain had no hereditary
nobility it seems reasonably safe to say that it
would have no Second Chamber. In Australasia,
this country having no dukes with an alleged
inborn right to legislate, we try to persuade our-
selves that there is an inborn right of legislation
in old worn-out tailors and unsuccessful cheese-
mongers, who are sent to die Upper House for
life and told to look as like dukes as they can.
Britain tolerates the peers of Argyll and Nor-
folk as law-makers because they are at least rem-
nants of history. There is a haze of old legend
about their names. The first of the Argyll race
that we clearly remember pursued Robert Bri ck
with kilts and bloodhounds and an unfriendly
expression, and the first Norfolk who looms up
very definitely in the ordinary man’s memory
found insulting and larrikin expressions pinned
on N to his tent at Boswortb telling him
that Dickon, his master, was bought and
sold. And because of these things N.S. W.
sends James Blanksby, who was something in a
building society, and Hepher, the tailor, to its
House of Lords to legislate for life, and tells
Blanksby to try to look like John of Lorn, and
Hepher to be as similar to Jockey of Norfolk as
his naturally unwarlike countenance will permit.
It is a pitiable and insulting joke —a sillv process
of guying an aged and infirm relative. The
nigger Emperor of Hayti didn’t “poke borak
quite so savagely at a foolish old British in-
stitution when he started his House of Lords
by creating a big buck African “ Duke of
Marmalade.” In Maoriland, as in N.S.W.,
the nominee Upper House has long been a
thing of pity. It consists mostly of cast-off


politicians who can no longer get any one to elect
tnem to the Lower House. When it dissents
rorn the Assembly it is suppressed ; when it says
Cll i°i t^ie Assembly it is useless —a cockatoo
could say as much as that. Premier Seddon has
wisely resolved to get rid of this silly and
disrespectful parody. And in doing so he at
least has precedent behind him. There are a
multitude of legislative bodies in the Bi’itish
Empire, and a full half of them, if not more, are
run on the one-House principle.
lioftus, Dailey, and the Soudan
Contingent.
** J-T.D.” writes : When the Soudan Contingent
returned to Sydney in June, 1885, “Hof’’im-
proved the occasion by giving us in The Bulletin
the well-remembered caricature of Lady Butler’s
picture, “ The Roll Call.” Dalley was on horse-
back as the general of the ragged remains of the
band of heroes. Augustus Loftus, just deceased,
who was Governor of New South Wales at the
time, was represented as an officer calling the
names. Loftus had a fowl between his feet.
This was a poetic touch, which reminds us that
the old gentleman made a bit on the quiet by
dealing in eggs and “fancy poultry.” Sydney
Telegraph in its obituary of Augustus Loftus
remarked: ‘ ‘ While Governor of New South Wales
he delivered at the departure of the Soudan
Contingent a fine patriotic speech, which wasexten-
sively quoted in England.” The speech referred
to was read by the feeble Augustus at Circular
Quay on March 3, 1885. It had been written
for the occasion by William Bede Dalley, who
was then aeting-Premier. Dalley stood at the old
Governor’s elbow and joined in the applause.
When the speech was printed in the papers,
everyone knew it was Dalley’s. No man knew
better how to blend playful humor with mock
heroics. Here is some Dalley as delivered by
Loftus
Countless as have been the occasions when the blood
and treasure of England have been poured out freely to
protect the people, to shield the defenceless, and to
maintain the right, there never has been one in which
humanity is more deeply interested in the triumph of the
arms of England than the cause which you are heroically
resolved to uphold by your valor. You will be greeted
in Egypt by the hearty welcome of thousands of
chivalrous soldiers who have never yet looked upon
such an action as yours. The eyes of your
gracious Queen will be bent upon your exertions,
and in every part -of the world where our
flag floats, men and women and children will
eagerly read of your exploits and pray for your suc-
cess. Soldiers, you carry in your keeping the honor of
this great colony which has made such splendid sacrifices
to send you to the front with an equipment of which
nations most practised in war might have been proud ;
and you will have the glorious privilege of helping to
maintain the honor of the Empire. In your ranks are
numbers who are voluntarily leaving the paths of for-
tune, worldly advantages, the comforts of home, and the
sweetness of domestic life for heroic service in a bloody
war in which already many brave men have been stricken
down. Soldiers, you leave us amidst the acclamations of
your fellow citizens whose hearts will be with you in your
camps and your conflicts —amidst an enthusiasm of
aflmiration and sacrifice unexampled—with the sym-
pathies of every true citizen of the Empire—
with our earnest hope that it may be your glorious
privilege to share in the triumph as in the service, and
to come back to us crowned with England’s gratitude,
as you are now encompassed with her sympathies.
Soldiers, on the part of your fellow-citizens I now say to
you—Farewell ! And may God have you in His holy
keeping.
The N.S.W. contingent did not strike a single
blow for the empire. It did not take part in any
engagement. Leaving Sydney on March 3, 1885,
the “heroes” were back at Circular Quay on
June 24. Six heroes had died of fever, over-
feeding and alcohol. Governor Loftus got off
another speech (written by Dalley) at the Victoria
Barracks. Said the Governor: —
It has been not the least gratifying circumstance in the
history of our enterprise that you have not been called
upon to exercise in any bloody engagement the courage
and devotion which prompted you to volunteer for the
defence of the empire. While this has been a source of
disappointment to you, it is a subject for our gratitude to
Heaven. All the duties that were laicLppon you you
have discharged with the instant obedience of good
soldiers and with the alacrity of gallant men.
A third speech written by Dalley was fired off
by Loftus at the citizens’ banquet to the returned
contingent at the Exhibition Building on July 28,
1885. Dalley spoke at the banquet, and compli-
mented the Governor upon his “eloquent and
elevating address.” Lord Loftus returned to
England in 1885. He was threatened with arrest
for debt on the mailboat. A couple of days before
the departure of the mail-steamer Dalley raised
the money among a few political and private
friends ; so there was no scene and no scandal.

A Deputation.
A pathetic deputation waited, a few days ago,
on Acting-Premier Wise, of N.S.W. It repre-
sented the great mass of men who have been
thrown out of work through the loan money having
practically given out, and it wanted the Govern-
ment to find more loan money somewhere, and
invent more public works, in order to avert a
calamity. The deputation led to no definite
result. It held that the Government was bound
to find work for the people, and the Acting-Premier
forcibly disagreed, and there was a deadlock. The
whole proceeding was a sorrowful comment on the
insane policy of trying to keep a country pros-
perous by imports and loans, but both parties,
unfortunately, adhered closely to the present
effect, and nobody took any stock of the more or
less remote cause. The situation in N.S.W., and
to some extent, also, in" other States, is that
there is a very large section of the population
which depends wholly on loans for its existence.
If at any time Bull refuses to lend, Heaven only
knows, just at present, what is to become of this
section. While Bull will lend, it is understood
to be the duty of the Government to invent jobs
for this section of the community, and spend the
loan money on these jobs. The jobs don’t pay,
and the amount of interest on the State’s public
debt which is not covered by the revenue from
public works, and which has to be made good by
taxation, is now about £1,200,000 a year. If
Bull keeps lending, and N.S.W. keeps building
public works that don’t pay interest in order
to supply employment, the amount of interest
which has to be raised by taxation will
increase indefinitely, and the final result will be
insolvency. In any ordinary, well-governed com-
munity 1,400,000 consumers are able to supply
employment for 1,400,000 producers, but N.S.W.
.(and Australia in general) has acquired a curious
disease which seems to make it unlike any other
country. N.S.W. has suffered for years from the
idea that it is better to import things and
deprive its people of employment than to make
tilings and employ its own people; and having
thus carefully deprived the people of employment
it has spent many millions of loan money on hope-
lessly unpayable works to find employment for
the people. Acting-Premier Wise has frequently
voted for this policy. The people who are
now unemployed in Sydney have, to a great
extent, voted for it. Sydney Telegraph is
even now publishing wild and whirling articles
to the effect that if, say, £250,000 worth of
looomotives can be manufactured a little more
cheaply in England than here, it is a positive duty
to send the work and the money to England—and
borrow £250,000 to keep the local people employed
at something else, and pay £IO,OOO a year interest
on the loan, and find the same old unemployed
problem waiting when the loan is spent. The
Freetrade theory is that we shouldn’t produce any
manufactured goods unless we can produce them
as cheaply as our cheapest competitor. If the
theory was carried to its logical conclusion, then
we should grow no agricultural produce unless we
could grow it as cheaply as everybody else, and
borrow no money unless we could borrow it
as cheaply as everybody else, and build no
railways unless we could build them _as
cheaply as the cheapest of foreign countries.
But the Freetrade theory has struck against a
snag. It lasted while loans lasted. If they have
really given out, either the unemployed must
starve or remunerative work must be found for
them at making something. One way of finding
work is by letting the unemployed make a few
million pounds’ worth of the things we now im-
port ; even if they don’t make them quite so
cheaply at first as the foreigner can do, still it is
better to pay £1,100,000 a year for goods that
would otherwise cost £1,000,000, and lose £IOO,OOO
a year for a time, than to borrow £1,000,000 a year
to keep the unemployed going at unprofitable
jobs—and lose £1,000,000 a year indefinitely.
Sydney persistently returns men like Pulsford,
Neild and Gray to the Australian Parliament, to
holler for lots of imports and no local industries.
Have they anything to suggest out of the resources
of the importing gospel, as a means of finding
work for 5000 or 6000 unemployed ? If these men
shouldn’t make locomotives, what should they
make 1
Secret Commissions.
The old sore of secret commissions has been un-
covered again in Melbourne. A butter and
produce shipping co., having lost money lately,
proposed to reconstruct; and one of its creditor
firms objected. The co. hit back in an affidavit, in
which it alleged that the principal creditor firm
(in much the same line of business) had got on
better than other people because it had accepted
from the shipping co.’s a secret commission of 3
percent, on all freights paid through it as shipping
agent for actual producers. Subsequently, tne co.
which was in difficulties said, having found
out what the other firm had been getting,
it had itself demanded the same concession from
the shipping co., and had collected £lOOO. Now,
probably the co. in the difficulties doesn’t know
for certain what happened as between the
shipping co. and the other firm, but it ought to
know all about the £lOOO which it got itself ; and
the fact that it demanded the money because
somebody else had been paid, and got it, is very
strong presumptive evidence that somebody else
actually was paid too —the shipping co. would
hardly have handed out the £lOOO otherwise. On
top of that came action by the actual producers ;
they sent round for signature among the ship-
ping agents— their shipping agents, the people
whom were paying to get the best possible
freights for them—an affidavit, setting out that
the signatories had not accepted any such second
commissions. And six refused to sign it, or, any-
way, didn’t sign it. In all the circumstances it
may be regarded as practically proved, then, that
secret commissions have been taken ; whatever
firms haven’t taken them, it is pretty clear that
some have. And this has happened at a time
when the agricultural and dairying industry
has been in an unusually bad way, and when
every ounce of help that could be given it
was needed—when there has been a cry for
concessions from an already embarrassed State for
this very industry, and when Kyabramapootra
has crowed aloud for economy, and has in particu-
lar denounced “socialistic legislation” as the
cause of the State’s troubles. If there was one
lot of ’Pootras who crowed louder than others it
was the city middlemen flock —a flock which
always can be relied upon to be very severe con-
cerning “socialistic legislation” and the obstacles
placed in the way of Private Enterprise. And
this is the flock which is now practically
convicted of bleeding the farmer over
whose woes it lately wept ! While the
middleman has yelled for economy, he, or
part of him, has put into his own pocket a 3
per cent, commission, which, morally, if not also
legally, belonged to the very men whose cause he
was hypocritically championing, and for whose
hardships he was blaming everybody but himself,
and every system but the one under which he was
feathering his own nest. For as to the immorality
of second commissions there can be no doubt. It
is doubtful, in fact, where there can be a second
commission ; there is no doubt, though, that there
can be one commission and one bribe. A man
pays an agent to get the best terms he can
for him; if the party from whom the agent
is supposed to screw these terms also pays
the agent, it is proof that the principal isn’t
getting the best terms possible. If the shipping
co.’s could afford to pay the agent 3 per cent.,
they could afford to charge the shippers 3 per
cent. less. In. England the law in the matter lias
been pretty clearly defined ; in Australia, unfor-
tunately, it has not yet been. Possibly these dis-
closures may help matters on. In any case, they
ought to open the eyes of the Victorian farmer to
a suspicion of the real character of some of those
“ friends ” who ran him into his recent outbreak
of Kyabramapootrism.

A Commonwealth Exhibition.
The Australian Prime Minister, in reply to a sug-
gestion that population should be attracted by
holding “a Great Australian Commonwealth
Exhibition,” has been, very properly, non-com-
mittal and snubby. There is in public affairs
nothing more pathetic than the notion that pros-
perity is a plant that can be cultivated without
hard labor, that there is some short and easy
path by which a nation can become great
without grafting for its greatness. Australia has
this notion pretty badly. It was mainly responsible
for the borrowing policy, that robust folly which
had its foundation in the belief that the path to
the pawnshop is the path to progress, and that
Cohen of London in a benevolent person with a
nose, anxious only to help us “todevelop our magni-
ficent natural resources.” It, too, is responsible
for the present notion that the difficulties, to which
borrowing and land monopoly and the lack of
adequate Protection have brought Australia, are
to be solved by holding an “ exhibition ” —a sort
of agricultural show on a large scale, where the
foreigner will display the machinery which we don’t
n.ake and the cloth which we don’t weave; and
the Australian will show his wool and pumpkins;
and the confidence man will bring a rich and rare
collection of Fiji uncles, match-boxes and Brum-
magem diamonds to gather the harvest which is
ripened for him wherever thousands gather to-
gether. An exhibition, it is claimed, would
“advertise Australia.” But Australia has so
little to advertise that any stock-taking of its
industries must lead to unfavorable comparisons.
Canada, not much older in civilisation, exports to
Australia pianos, bicycles, various sorts of
machinery, cheese, furniture, and even poultry.
Australia should at least climb up to the Canadian
level of production before it blows a trumpet to
invite the nations to come and behold its indus-
tries. There is no such easy road as a “great
international exhibition ” out of the bad situation
which is shown by the smallness of the Australian
immigration figures. The causes of existing evils
must be faced—the locking up of the fertile coast
lands and the lack of manufactures —and cured.
Holding an exhibition under the circumstances is
like hiring a brass band to cure a case of typhoid.

“Private Enterprise” in a New Role.
Dear Bulletin. —What looks like an impudent
attempt to defy the law and to flout the “con-
tract ” provisions of the Immigration Restriction
Act is, to all appearance, being worked just now
by the locking-out bosses of the Outtrim (Gipps-
land) coal mines. A brief paragraph from its
local correspondent notified in the Argus of 2nd
inst. that “the six imported Scotch mine-bosses
had refused to fulfil their engagements, having
been urged thereto by the leaders of the
V.C.M.A.; but that there were signs that some
of them were weakening.” Next day the com-
pany’s legal manager denied that the men were
imported under contract they were merely
ordinary immigrants looking for work, he ex-
plained—and thereafter the Argus, perceiving it
had carelessly trodden on a very hot brick, shut
down on the whole circumstance and maintained
a loud silence. Now the facts in the case are
these : The Victorian union leaders got informa-
tion from Scotland, months ago, that Scottish
miners were being induced to go out to
Victoria to work in the Outtrim mines—from
which the Victorian union miners were locked
out at the end of ’O2, and which have been practi-
cally idle for over a year. The Scotchmen didn’t
say the emigrants were under contract, but that
six of them were coming out by the Orontes—the
famous six hatters, it will be remembered, came
by the same boat—and their names were given.
When these six men reached Melbourne, certain
Labor members and union leaders went on board
and interviewed them. They admitted they were
miners—not bosses—and that they came from
Scotland, but denied that their passages were
paid for them, that they were under contract, or
that they had any intention of applying at the
Outtrim-Howitt mine for employment at all.
That was 25th February. Five days later these
six miners, who all came out by the same boat,
and from the same place, had, with surprising
unanimity, found their way to the same small
township—Outtrim—and the Argus’ local corres-
pondent reported that they had declined to fulfil
their contract. The Argus man may have
made a mistake, but the surprising detail
about the affair—the thing that calls aloud for
explanation—is how six perfect strangers who, on
Thursday, had no intention of going to Outtrim
nor any knowledge of the place—on the following
Tuesday should have all arrived there, have im-
mediately been engaged to work, and have caused
such a stir by their final refusal to work. Of
course, it was carefully pointed out to them by
the Labor members in Melbourne that such a con-
tract as they were supposed to have signed was
utterly null and void, and could be broken with-
out fear of penalty, and that employers who con-
tracted such men were liable to a penalty of
£5O or three months’ gaol—but the Union
leaders could do nothing to move the Com-
monwealth Government while the suspected
contract was unprocurable, or if, as is shrewdly
suspected, it was merely a verbal one. The
Outtrim-Howitt Co. offers contract work to
all who will apply—excepting members of the
V.C.M.A. ; and, notwithstanding that it has
scoured the States for blackleg labor ever since it
brought its first train-load of riff-raff through
Melbourne last Eight-Hours Day, its monthly
output of coal for January last was not equal to
its average weekly output before the lock-out.
It has all along persistently refused arbitration,
and, as it must get more miners somehow or some-
where if it is to continue its existence, the direc-
tors were commonly supposed, as a last shift, to
have resorted to the trick of importing contract
laborers, sub rosd. And the Immigration Act
doesn’t make any provision for dealing with
suspected contracts when neither party will own
up to having made them.—F.

The cold-blooded Conroy, misrepresentative of
N.S.W. in the Federal Parliament, rehashed the
old wheeze last week that old-age pensions would
tend to destroy thrift among the people, and on
that very illogical ground he would oppose the
Federal Bill. No doubt Conroy is, at the same
time, virtuously angry that the same class of
people are restricting their increase. Yet lie is
trying to kill a measure that might possibly tend
to encourage working people to rear families.
What possible chance is there for folk with small
means to show an outward visible sign of their
having exercised thrift if they rear a family
of (say) six, give them decent food, and
keep them at school as the State de-
mands ? Even if work is continuous and health
invariably good, the house-father’s two or three
pounds a week cannot possibly stretch into much
of a surplus. And the house-mother must exercise
the keenest kind of thrift to save a little for the
emergencies that are sure to happen. So that the
thriftiest of the workers are only likely to keep
their heads above water in their best days, and in
their worst— God help them ! —the only hope for
them is that at 60 or 65—if they have managed to
escape gaol, and various other regulations have
been lived up to—there is a small sum weekly
available for the very short time still left to them.
Providing always that yapping politicians of the
Conroy variety cannot cut the amount out of the
Estimates some dull season as a matter of Reform,
and for the purpose of encouraging thrift,

The ratepayers of Melbourne city seem to have
done the right thing in electing one John
Gardiner (an old municipal identity) to be coun-
cillor in preference to a rival candidate, Curtis.
Having been duly declared non-elected, the irate
Curtis said he was now satisfied that nobody can
get into Melbourne City Council “unless he is a
Labor man to the backbone and a Roman Catholic
to the backbone,” after which he fired off the
satisfactory announcement that he would never
again apply for election. The shriek of Curtis
is an extraordinary fiction, considering that the
Council, which Curtis alleges to be Roman
Catholic and Democratic to the backbone, is
bossed by Malcolm M'Eacharn and graced by
the presence of Cornelius Job Ham. Curtis,
in fact, would pass muster as a compara-
tively harmless crank if he didn’t sound
like an agent for George Reid. What Curtis
was saying at the Carlton polling-place George
Reid was suggesting in the Federal Parliament,
and the Age next morning caught the public eye
with two cross-headings—“ Defeated Candidate’s
speech. Labor and Sectarian Influence Alleged.”
“Sectarianism in Politics. Mr. Reid and the
Labor Leader.” It was a nasty coincidence that
a pair of discredited spouters should have been
bidding for Yellow sympathy on the same night.

The Sydney Telegraph, last Monday, reported
in its Melbourne correspondence under the
heading “Threat to Public Servants”: —
Melbourne, Sunday.—The State Premier, Mr. Bent,
spoke to a large audience of farmers at the annual
Warracknabeal Hospital gathering to-day. . . . The public
servants, he was sorry to notice, had allied themselves with
the Labor party, and if they became disturbers by
political agitation, the Government would have to six
if something further should not be done with them.
The Victorian public servants were ordered by
the Irvine-Bent Government to elect representa-
tives of their own—therefore, they have to take a
hand in “political agitation.” And Premier
Bent, to all intents and purposes, according to
this report, threatens to corruptly use the power
with which he has been entrusted to punish them
if they agitate in favor of his political opponents,
and don’t vote for men that will support him. In
a less hopeless community this outrageous pro-
ceeding, supposing the matter to be correctly im-
ported, would place Premier Bent fairly within
reach ot the law, as an individual who tries to
influence voters by threatening language. What
it will do in Victoria remains to be seen.

A Plea for Sunday Trading 1 .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[At the Licensed Victuallers’ Picnic in Sydney on March
10th the President (Mr. J. B. Sutton) said it should not
be a crime for a poor man to get a drink on Sunday. Mr.
Owe’Sullivan, Minister for Works, responding to the
toast of “ The Ministry,” said he had always been in favor
of fair play for the liquor interests and he recognised
that men must occasionally have some liquor if they
wished for it.— Extracted from S. M. Herald report.]
I agree with J. B. Sutton
And endorse O’Sullivan !
And I do not care a button
If it angers any man !
Their remarks have brought conviction
To my mind that there’s a flaw ;
An unreasoning restriction
In the present Liquor Law !
It is true we may on Monday
Start to drink at 6 a.m.
As on every day (bar Sunday),
Still the Law I must condemn.
It is shameful, to my thinking,
Most disgraceful every way,
That we cannot keep on drinking
More than seventeen hours per day !
Though the Law may now command it,
Such a Law we should not he&d ;
I don’t know why people stand it—
No ! I really don’t,(indeed !
Hang such laws ! we do not need ’em !
That is why I write this rhyme.
people want is Freedom !
Give them Freedom all the time !
I’ll admit it is but one day
That hotels are told to close,
But suppose you’re dry on Sunday
(Not a hard thing to suppose),
Must you quench your thirst with water,
Or with lemonade, or tea ?
Why not have a beer-and-porter ?
Is this country really free ?
Though I can’t be called a glutton,
Or I hardly think I can,
I agree with Mr. Sutton
And his friend, O’Sullivan,
That this Law of Sunday-closing,
Which we surely ought to ban,
Grievous hardship is imposing
On the Suffering Working Man.
It is true, and I’m not sorry,
That a man on Saturday
Might arrange to hire a lorry,
Or a waggon, or a dray,
Or a motor car, if quicker
(Though a bit more apt to burst),
To take home a stock of liquor
To assuage his Sunday Thirst!
He could take home wine and brandy,
Schnapps and whisky, rum and gin
Any liquor that is handy
With a cart to stack it in !
He could take it by the bottle,
Or in cases, that is clear,
And to help to rinse his throttle
He could take some kegs of beer !
But as I am not omniscient,
I would ask O’Sullivan :
Would a drayload be sufficient
For a Suffering Working Man ?
Would it last him until Monday ?
If O’Sullivan says not,
Let us open pubs, on Sunday,
For the Liquor Law is rot!
Let the barmaids and the barmen,
And the bonifaces meek,
And the brewers and their carmen,
All Work Seven Days a Week !
They’re not worth consideration—
They are merely slaves, in fact,
Who require no Arbitration,
And no Early Closing Act!
n.s.w.
W. T. GOODGE.
