Does anyone remember, in the pauses of
getting his living and paying or evading his debts,
an essay of the late Robert Louis Stevenson in
which the boys of the boy Stevenson’s neighbor-
hood are described as going about after nightfall
with bull’s-eye lanterns strapped to their waists,
inside their coats. “ Have you got your lantern ? ”
was the dark, husky query when two of these em-
bittered conspirators met. “ Yes ! ” and the coats
would be unbuttoned and the lights displayed for
a second. There was no levity in the proceed-
ing, and the lads cultivated each a gloomy
frown and the hostile aspect of a fortress. On
the revival of the “ Sign of the Cross ” at the
Sydney Her Majesty’s, last Saturday, one person
at least recalled the memory and laughed. The
uusanctified mirth occurred at the point where
the two boss Christians, Favius and Titus, meet,
and, after exchanging mysterious Wilson Barrett
glances and hoarse Latin whispers, pull up their
sleeves and exhibit the sign of the cross (in
charcoal) on their arms. One simply can-
not take the “Sign of the Cross” seriously,
chiefly because the early Christian martyr seems
so unpleasant and sorrowful and needlessly husky.
We are quite willing to give him all the honor we
don’t want for ourselves, but we would much
rather have Marcus Superbus or Licinius to
dinner. Mercia isn’t unpleasant, hence she is the
cause of a lot of trouble. The most convincing
tiling in the present revival is Julius Knight’s
love-making. He has a good idea of how “the
brute aroused in the man ” would roar and rend
its prey. He folds his arms high up on the roof
of his chest at times, and the hang of his toga is a
marvel of skill. If the original Marcus wore his
with the same artistic but negligent.droop Mercia
must have indeed been unimpressionable. Maud
Jeffries plays the white-faced spiritual maiden
who goes about Rome bare - headed in so
beautifully passionless a way that one doubts if
the lions got any red blood and satisfaction from
their meals in the days of old. Memories af
Caleb Porter and after him Atbolwood as Nero
handicap actor Warburton for the majority of
the audience. Arthur Wontner as Tigellinus
and Johnston Weir as Licinius are well fitted.
So are Madge Hope as Poppea, Olive Noble as
Dacia, and Rose Pendennis as Berenice. Eardley
Turner is good in everything, and as the drinky
Glabrio he pleased a very crowded house on
Saturday.
** « m
“The Face at the Window,” described as
“a remarkable detective story of real life,” was
revived at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday, in spite
of the State anxiety as to the birth-rate, and The
Face appeared framed in green limelight, and Le
Loup howled through a weird tin kazoo in the
distance, and the galvanised corpse sat up and
accused the villain with all the old success—
indeed, with more, for the gallery which
goes to Le Loup has, nowadays, learned to
imitate his noble howl, and works it
off at frequent intervals. The public thus
gets a good deal more howl for its money
than it did before. As regards the acting,
Robt. Inman gives quite a touch of life to Paul
Gouffet, but Willard’s comic make-up as Le
Loup detracts seriously from the horror of his
performance. His working garb as a murderer is
impressive enough, but his make-up as an Italian
chevalier is borrowed from a circus clown, and a
Horror should be a Horror always—not a
ludicrous object and a Horror in turns. When
Harry Diver used to do this sort of work he gave
it an impressiveness by his very sincerity and
consistency. Miss Guildford does as well as
anyone could in a colorless part, and Miss
Ethel Buckley is a fairly spirited humorous
relief. But Bert Bailey’s comic business
is becoming weary and mechanical. Of
course this class of play is not exactly
encouraging to originality, but actors should
make an effort to avoid falling into a rut—for
their own sakes, if not for the sake of the audience.
“The Face at the Window” will cast its green
and sickly horror over the Lyceum for a while yet.
It has achieved a certain popularity, and may yet
become an Institution —like “East Lynne.”

“ The Royal ” is just now closed awaiting
the arrival of Bland Holt. Last Saturday the
Musical Comedy Co. finished a successful season
with a long and merry performance, wherein the
comic people gagged to their hearts’ content, and
were left gagging somewhere near midnight by
the majority of the audience.
* * *
The Fitzgerald Brothers’ Circus, which is
always a welcome conglomeration of man and
beast, returns to Sydney shortly, and the big tent
will be pitched in Belmore Park on the 26th. In
addition to all the usual and unusual attractions,
and the extra and astonishing attractions, six
wrestlers from Monkey Land have been engaged.
The wrestlers from that country have a world-
wide reputation, and an illustrated poster shows
two fat and fearsome persons struggling with each
other in an extraordinary fashion.
** * #
W. F. Hawtrey will follow “ The Lady of
Ostend ” with “ The Two Mr. Wetherbys.” at the
Sydney Cri. on Saturday night—for the first time
in Australia. Hawtrey himself will be one Mr.
Wetherby, the bad one, who, as it turns out, is
about as good as the good one ; Gregan McMahon
will be the good one, who is no better than the
bad one. Circumstances, however, conspire to
make McMahon appear to live up to a reputation
for owning all the domestic virtues ; chief among
these circumstances is Miss Bronton, who is an
awful aunt this time, instead of a fearful mother-
in-law as hitherto. O. P. Heggie, the Adelaidean,
who did good work formerly in “ The Message
from Mars,” is included in the cast; likewise
Miss Mabel Lane and Miss Austin.
sfr * * *
The three Dumonds will cease their clever
musical buffooning; at the Tivoli this week, and
Clarke and Hamilton will then, as far as is
known at present, be the star attractions until
the Easter Jbill' appears. Manager Rickards,
back from Adelaide, where he experimented with
a new patriotic song, is spreading himself upon a
new and gorgeous “first part,” which threatens
to be really different in some respects from other
“first parts.” The girls are to be taken out of
Japanese clothes, and everybody is to appear as
some sort of a mechanical toy. Meantime, the
mostly musical programme continues to draw the
usual eight good houses a week.

THE FLOOD TIDE.
The wondrous tale of Thompson we will won-
deringly sing—
Denny Thompson, known in Chili as the Dotty
Diamond King.
The story has a moral which is very old and
tough :
If the world believes you wealthy it will furnish
you with stuff:
If you’ve not got Thompson’s riches—well, his
name is good enough.
There’s a steamer bound for London, from the
Valparaiso port.
The passengers are mostly of the silent, super,
sort,
But two are lady-spielers, a Mama and Daughter
pair,
Who talk and dream of Thompson, the eccentric
millionaire.
For a secret, worth the keeping, in their mental
safe is stored —
The fact that Denny Thompson, King of Dia-
monds, is aboard.
Now he travels as M‘Naughton, and he roams the
quarter-deck
In a chronic state of liquor, with his boots slung
round his neck,
Until he ends the chapter of his shocking
jamboree
By jumping off the vessel when she’s ploughing
through the sea.
“Lord help him!” says the captain—“he can
get no aid from me.”
Then the younger lady-spieler, with her mother
at her side,
Assumes the rank and station of the Diamond
Monarch’s bride.
Believing him a dead ’un, they dissemble their
belief,
And speak of poor M'Naughton as the fool who
came to grief.
The pair of cute pretenders state that Thompson’s
coming soon
To their swell hotel at Brighton to complete his
honeymoon ;
They revel in expenses, but the landlord doesn’t
kick :
They order gowns and jewels, horses, carriages—
on tick—
Intending, should their creditors grow dubious
and shy,
To gather up the plunder and politely do a guy.
It happens that a stewardess, to save her Dad
from shame,
Conceives another scheme of working Denny
Thompson’s name.
She knows that all his baggage has been sent to
the hotel,
And thinks her own Young Man might pass for
Thompson very well,
Whilst she plays secretary and provides the
common sense,
He’ll need for raising money by this fraudulent
pretence.
So young man Clipps, and Polly, trip to Brighton
right away,
And find the lady spielers are already on tlie lay ;
And each pair knows the other for the biggest
fraud in life,
But silence suits them better than an internecine
strife
’Twixt a bogus Denny Thompson and his bogus
darling wife.
O! the joy of getting credit for whatever you
desire,
When you seem to walk on ether, though your
feet are in the mire !
The ladies—Heaven bless ’em ! —strike a Hebrew
for a loan,
And the masculine impostor makes ten thousand
on his own
By signing an agreement re the floating of his
mine,
And then he buys a racehorse. This is Polly’s
pet design ;
For her father owns a gee-gee who is entered for
a race,
And can win it (if he loses it means “ruin and
disgrace”)
Provided “Thompson’s” purchase isn’t jigging
for the stake.
So “Thompson,” quite unconscious of the risk he
has to take,
Agrees to scratch the favorite for Polly’s father’s
sake,
And the angry mob assaults him till his inmost
feelings ache.
In the meantime Thompson (proper) reappears
upon the scene.
He was fished from out the ocean ere the water
washed him clean ;
His togs are torn and tattered; there are hay-
seeds in the hair
Of that homeless, cashless, friendless, drunken,
dotty millionaire
When he comes to Polly’s father for assistance in
his plight.
Now, “the King’s” return is awkward, so, to keep
him out of sight,
They bid him seek seclusion by the peaceful Eng-
lish lakes,
With his boots upon his shoulders, just to keep
’em free from snakes.
There a villain tries to drown him (’tis the spieler
mother’s plan);
But this purchased desperado is a disappointing
man,
For his night attack on Thompson proves the
silliest of tries;
Himself is left to perish when the Flood Tide
waters rise,
And the other schemers mostly get rewarded for
their Lies.
When Thompson’s mine is floated for a million
pounds or more,
Which is five times what the property is worth,
He offers half the money to the fraudulent vendor
And reckons him his dearest friend on earth.
Thus the sin of wily Polly makes the fortune of
her boy,
And puts her father straight again, of course ;
For the old man’s bitter sorrow was converted
into joy
The day they worked their swindle with the ’orse.
Nor does the spieler daughter ever suffer from
regrets;
She leaves her wicked mother in the lurch —
And the cranky King from Chili duly liquidates
her debts
When she’s booked as Mrs. Thompson by the
church.
Alas ! poor, wicked mother! Of her daughter
now bereft,
Her world seems dark and desolate and cold ;
The other frauds are happy, she’s the only one
that’s left
To expiate the sin—of growing old.

is carrying Bland Holt’s long season to a success-
ful end. Sometime in the week after next the co.
will shift to Sydney, but for the present it will
continue to impress upon large audiences the im-
portance of being Thompson.

The co. will stay on for another week in Mel-

** * *
At Melbourne Princess’s the threatened
outbreak of excruciating merriment is happening
in moderation. “ A Stranger in a Strange Land ”
disarms criticism to a great extent by calling itself
a farce, or a laughing show, or an Aid to Hysteria,
or anything but a comedy. People who go to
laugh at this candid caricature of the orthodox
farcical comedy must take a magnanimous sense
of humor with them, and give it a liquid stimu-
lant, or a box of chocolates between the acts. The
alleged misunderstandings in “A Stranger” are
not supported by the least evidence of possibility,
therefore the girls who profess to mistake two
different Indians for a third different Indian, and
the men who say ditto to the girls all the time, are
screamingly funny in a serious way of speaking.
The attempt to make the audience laugh is itself
laughable at times when the endeavor doesn’t
score a legitimate success. And there are other
times when Willoughby and co. manage to give an
appearance of personal drollery to the farce, as a
farce.
** * *
Vulcana, likewise Atlas, are filling in a
few farewell nights at Melbourne Opera House.
Jennie Gray is a recent arrival; Mrs. Sims
Reeves, who has moderated her peculiarities, is
somewhat less recent; the other items on the pro-
gramme are pretty well seasoned. Russell, the
ventriloquist, seems to be amassing popularity as
he goes along, and to say this of a professor in his
antique line of business is to crown him with a
wreath of laurels.
#* * #
Albert Friedenthal, the most popular
pianist of the non-sensational kind who ever
visited Australia, is shortly returning for another
tour. Friedenthal was out here just five years
ago—a quiet, fuzzy-haired artist, with a light
touch and a powerful magnetism. His concerts
were recitals unassisted by any other talent than
his own. Barring a ten minutes interval, he sat
at the piano and played, played, played pretty
well all the time, sometimes taking the encore
without leaving the platform. His one-man
entertainment lasted an hour and three-quarters
or thereabouts, and the audience was always loth
to let him go. It is easy to believe that this un-
pretentious pianist has performed with “astonish-
ing success ” in the biggest cities since he left
Australia. This country had heard nothing of
Albert Friedenthal when he first came, so his
success, under the circumstances, was amazing.
** * m
Atlas and Vulcana have left Adelaide
Tivoli, where the two Bells have come in and the
six Empire Girls have begun to give their well-
timed and collective kick. Atlas promoted a
weight-lifting competition among local youths
during his last week. But his speeches to the
audience were somewhat marred when it was
found that any casual reference to Pagel sent him
into a kind of frenzy. As anybody can call out
“Send for Pagel” or similar jests, the spectacle
of an excited Welshman dancing at the footlights
became painfully frequent. At the Royal, the
Majeronis are putting in a fortnight with plays that
are either new or old enough to seem so. The
brothers (one virtuous, one villainous) act easily
and well, and have efficient support from Maie
Nelson (why “Maie”?), Frank Vernon, and others.
The Wild Cat Column.
The N.S.W. Miners’ Accident Relief Fond,
which began operations in 1901:
Carried to Re-
Gross Ex- serves for Un- Re-
Dec., Income. Claims, penses. expiredltisks. serves.
1901 .. £31,906 £6,821 £lOl £24,984 £24,984
1902 .. 39,036 11,096 1546 26,394 51,378
1903 .. 45,559 14,518 1050 29,991 81,369
This fund is the nearest approach to a State Life Depart-
ment in Australia. The premiums are paid by the
miners (50 per cent., or 4£d. per week), the mineowners
(25 per cent.) and the State (25 percent.). The benefits
include funeral allowance, accident benefit, and a small
pension to the widow and young children of a miner who
has been killed by mining accident. The expense-rate
is exceedingly low, amounting to only £2 6s. Id. per
cent, of the total income. Part of the expense (office,
secretary, audit, &c.) is, however, borne by the State
without charge to the fund ; but even with this included
the rate would be extremely low, due to the fact that,
every mine workman being a contributor, noth ng goes
in grubbing up business or in collecting contributions.
The fund is administered by a small central board ; but,
as its members are also chiefly M’s.P., they take no fees.
Local committees, which attend to the distribution of
the benefits, are paid small amounts, totalling last year
£914. The accumulated funds in hand (£80,137) consist
of N.S.W. Stock and Treasury Bills £79,000, cash and
bank balances £ll2l, and £l6 in local committees’ hands.
The balance of reserves (£1232) is, as usual, made up of
interest and contributions due bv the Government.
Interest earnings (£2185) are not what they might be
expected to he ; on mean funds they amounted to only
£3 ss. lOd. per cent. Still, the investments are confined
absolutely to State securities.
The Fund is having the same experience as the
English companies had which lately went in heavily for
accident insurance after the passing of the English Em-
ployers’ Liability Act. At the start the workmen felt a
disinclination to go “on the fund,” and after they had
got on they got off with all speed. Very soon, however,
they found out, either that “ on the fund” wasn’t a bad
place to be, or that their employers did not want stiV! ,
limping men about their works; so both the percentage
of men on the fund and the term of disablement have
gradually stretched out. The N.S.W. experience has
been:—
Non-Fatal Average Amount
Accidents per of Relief per

Disablement.

1902 .. .. 146T8 2 5 9

In view of the rapid growth of funds, it has been suggested
that the benefits might be increased, as the Act permits
in certain cases; but the foregoing figures throw cold
water upon any such scheme. And there are other
figures pointing the same way. There are now 89 widows
pensioners of the fund, and there is no telling when they
will cease to be pensioners ; also there are already 28 ex-
miners who have become permanently disabled during
the three years, and who are also life pensioners. There
is a consequent rapid increase in the relief granted per
1000 contributors

1902 .. 11l 4 2 356 18 7
1903 .. 177 8 7 431 3 5
The benefit in respect of fatal accidents is exclusive of
funeral allowance, which, of course, simply varies from
year to year with the number of fatalities in each year.
It is absolutely certain that it is too early yet to alter
the scale of contributions or benefits. In fact, it is doubt-
ful if a time will ever come when they can be increased.

Notwithstanding that, with three departments
transferred to the Commonwealth, the Owe’See last year
had a revenue of £11,532,133, or over two millions more
than there was five years ago, and notwithstanding that
it also borrowed £3,126,000 apart from what was required
in connection with resumed properties, it still wasn’t
able to spare enough to keep existing works in decent
repair. The Owe’See was so busy providing fidgetty and
hungry constituents with new roads and bridges that the
old ones were allowed to go to rack. Nothing falls to
pieces much faster than a neglected bridge; yet under
this heading, the Owe-See’s officer says in his annual
report—“ No painting was carried out, and many works
(of repail-), which should, for economical reasons, have
been carried out, had to be postponed for want of funds.”
The Government Architect makes the same remark
regarding his branch—a saving had been made “ un-
doubtedly at the expense of a considerable number of
buildings urgently requiring repairs.” How much
revenue would the Owe’See have to get before it would
be able to afford to keep public works in repair ? And
what’s the use of the Government talking of its “very
moderate deficit ” of last year when the “ moderateness”
of it was only achieved by saving the stitch that costs
nine ?
Of the £37,041,785 which the N.S.W. Public
Works Department has spent during the past 15 years,
£21,996,368 has been loan money. Of the £11,906,673
which it has spent during the past three years £9,358,258
has been borrowed. In the 15-year period N.S.W. leaned
up against Bull for 59 per cent, of its public works re-
quirements ; in the past three years for no less than 79
per cent, of them. Even if the money borrowed in con-
nection with the Rocks and other land resumptions is
struck out of the reckoning, 71 per cent, of the expendi-
ture has still come out of loans. And this expenditure,
of course, includes salaries of staff, repairs and upkeep as
well as money spent on new works.

British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering- firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from IS inches to 36im:hes
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supp>
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water-supply.

It is rumored that Mt. Lyell (Tas.) No. 1 nest
of smelters is to be permanently closed, with a c °"® e 9Vf' 9 s
shortening of staff and dismissal of hands. A dividend
is probable within a few weeks. The picking-up in shares
is the barometer. The resumption of work at the South
Tharsis and Royal Tharsis flux mines means that there is
not so much ore in the North Lyell as was estimated. If
so, what a howling farce to amalgamate, except that it
gave the opportunity to the Mt. Lyell people to pay a big
cash dividend before the working of its own mine s low-
grade pyrites had eaten a lot of the co.’s liquid assets.
* * * *
Crown Lyell, after scratching about, intends to
deepen its shaft and make a final effort to pick up some-
thing near the North Lyell boundary. It s odds-on that
it gets nothing, just as it is odds-on that it will make a
loss in sending any ore under 6 per cent, copper to the
Lyell smelters.

After struggling with it for some months the
pneumatic tube between the Sydney Exchange and
the Post Office has been given up as a bad job, and is
no longer in use. But it is still included among the
“permanent and reproductive works” for which the
Owe’See has borrowed money—the Owe’Sullivan’s de-
partment spent £2313 of loan money on it last year alone.
* « • *
N.S.W. Treasury has called for a return giving
the value of the various buildings and properties belong-
ing to the State, and the amount and cost of insurances
thereon. It is under consideration, remarks this note,
whether the Government should not insure its own pro-
perty in its own insurance fund. (It has cost already
some £5OOO to insure the Government steamer Victoria
alone.) The information is being laboriously collected,
but it remains to be seen whether vested interests will be
strong enough to nip in the bud this move towards a
State Insurance Department. Of course, there is but
one opinion as to whether it would pay the Government
or not; on the available evidence the profits would be
very handsome, indeed.
* • » *
N.S.W. gets the money to pay its interest from
three principal sources. First, there are the net profits
from railways and from water and sewerage works. If
these are not sufficient, the balance has to be made up
chiefly from taxation. The amount of borrowed money
spent upon railways is 60‘9 per cent, of the whole bor-
rowings ; on water and sewerage, 14T per cent. —together
75 per cent. If, therefore, these water works and railways
meet even their own interest, there would be left only 25
per cent, to be made up through taxation, etc. The fol-
lowing shows what has actually happened during the past
few years:

Previous to 1899 the deficiency which was payable out o
taxation had reached 37'6 in 1892 ; but from that point
the tendency had t>een gradually downward, and this
improvement continued steadily through 1899,1900, and
1901. Then, however, the effects of the Owe’See’s big
borrowing policy began to show themselves. The
Owe’See alleged that the money was going
into reproductive works; now these figures rise
up and ask the Owe’See for an explanation. In three
years the amount of interest which is not obtained from
the works in which loans are invested, and which conse-
quently has to be made up out of taxation (or borrowings)
has more than doubled. From £547,881 in 1900 it
went to £1,333,875 in 1903. A country that hasn’t any
great natural resources which it can develop at less cost
than that, had better keep quiet about its resources and
try to develop something else that hasn’t so large an
appetite.
* * * *
Queensland local public bodies seem to have
regarded their debts to the Philp Government as just the
same sort of joke as the sugar co.’s did. Eight of them
obtained loans for the construction of tramways; and
only one of them, the Ayr, has kept its interest and
redemption payments going. The others have either not
paid at all or only irregularly. The Douglas shire council
borrowed £31,030 to build a tramway from Port Douglas
to the Mossman River, and now owes £3396 interest and
a lot of instalments on account of principal. Among
them the local bodies owe £12,000 for interest alone ;
and the arrears have latterly been mounting up at an
alarming rate. Several of the lines—the Douglas one,
for instance—serve remarkably rich country ; but, as the
owners won’t tax themselves sufficiently to keep the
accounts square, there has been nothing for it but for the
Morgan-Browne Government to point to the clause which
gives power to strike a special rate to wipe out the debt,
and to notify that that power will be used unless the bill
is paid.
* * * •
On the basis of population S. Australia is now
borrowing more rapidly than any other State except
Westralia, which is, in this matter, an apparently in-
curable lunatic. In the past four months £472,800 of
a per cent. Treasury bills have been successfully hawked
in Adelaide, and authority still exists to hawk £693,300
more. S. Australia has a population of only 367,000, so
in four months it has added £1 6s. per head to its
already crushing debt. For some years S. Australia and
Queensland were running almost neck and neck in the
race for the Biggest Debt on Earth ; but the southern
state now looks like an easy winner. In 1891 Queensland’s
total per head was £73125. 5d.; S.A.’s was then £62 9s. lid.
Ten years later, Queensland, through its increase of
population and only moderate borrowings, meanwhile
had reduced its debt per head by 11s. 3d.—to £73 Is. 2d.
South Australia, on the other hand, put up its debt by
£lO 7s.—to £72 16s. Ud. Its 10-year record was thus
£lO 18s. 3d. worse than Queensland’s. Philp’s heavy and
persistent borrowings in his later days gave Queens-
land an increased lead again ; in 1902 its total
was £76 Bs. 6d. per head, as against South Aus-
tralia’s £75 2s. lOd. As Queensland’s borrowings
have, however, now practically ceased, while South
Australia’s are going ahead with all the energy
that a premier expert in canvassing for small
orders can command ; and as, moreover, the
South Australian population is increasing much less
rapidly than that of Queensland, it is just about
certain that the southern State will own per head at the
end of 1903-4 the Biggest Debt on Earth. Whether Bull
Cohen will view this possession as much of an asset when
the State approaches him for the renewal of £6,250,000,
as it will have to do before the end of 1909, remains to
be seen. A debt of very nearly £l7 per head falling due
within the next five years would make a more responsible
Premier than Jenkins feel mighty uncomfortable

An ingenious wild-oat has lately broken out in
Sydney. It has branded itself “ The 20s. Gift, Ltd., and
is alleged to be doing a great business. The Catfirsti sells
Brown a book of five tickets for ss. Brown sells these
tickets for Is. each. The five people who buy them take
their tickets to Cat, Ltd., and for 4s. each they get also
each a book of tickets. Brown may then call on Oat,
Ltd., and receive 18s.-£l, less the Cat’s commission of
10 per cent. The same process goes on in each case.
When Smith, one of Brown’s customers, sells his five
tickets, and those five ticket-holders buy books for them-
selves at 4s. each, then Smith also can get £1 (less 10 per
cent.) at the Cattery. It seems a ridiculously easy way ot
“ earning ”£1 (less 10 per cent.). All the time, of course,
the Cat, Ltd.’s liability is mounting up. By the time the
sixth round is reached there will be 15,625 tickets out,
and the animal may be called upon to (less 10
per cent.) to the holders of the 3125 tickets of the fifth
round. Also it will already have paid over £7OO to the
holders of tickets in the first, second, third and fourth
rounds. Grave doubts have been expressed as to its
ability to do it. There is, however, no need to worry on
that score. This isn’t a game that will kill the Cat. lhe
Oat doesn’t have to pay any Brown or other individual
his £1 (less 10 per cent.) until his five victims have lodged
4s. each, or £1 in all. If four lodge 4s. each, and the
fifth doesn’t, then Brown doesn’t get any £1 at all; and
the Oat scores. Even if the five come in, the Cat still rakes
in 10 per cent.—it receives £1 and pays 18s. So long as
people can pass their tickets on, the Cat will only earn
10 per cent. ; hut when the end arrives and the holders
can’t get victims, then the Cat will score heavily. When
the 15,625 tickets are out, the Cat won’t have to P a y the
£3125 (less 10 per cent, which reduces it 10s.)
which they represent until it has been rushed for 78,125
tickets. No man gets his £1 until, through him, first hve
and then a further 25 others have joined in the gamble.
When a man buys five tickets for 4s. and can’t sell them,
then the Cat scores 45., and the man who sold this
other man his original ticket gets no £l. The chances of
missing the £1 are, in fact, so tremendous, while the
Oat stands absolutely on velvet all the time, that only a
very venturesome fool will touch the gamble. It is a
pity there isn’t a law to suppress this utterly useless
business, which is simply another method of preying
upon the producers of the community without giving an
atom of return, but for the present there doesn’t seem to
be. „ „
Sydney city treasurer gives a warning to those
person's with money invested in the liquor trade. Dis-
cussing the collection of city rates he writes . the
ratepayers, as a whole, have cheerfully fulfilled their
obligations, especially in business circles, other than
hotelkeepers ; these, as a class, are certainly in a bad
way, but whether it is because of one year s want of
trade or the accumulation of many struggles against
their environment Ido not venture to say. In my
capacity as city treasurer, I consider it part of my
duty to go very deeply into matters which affect tne
city’s bank balance. In this respect there have been
so many publicans applying for extension of tune to
pay their rates, and in very many instances the evi-
dences which have come personally before me of finan-
cial struggle have been so strongly proven, I am
convinced that there is an underlying influence other
than actual want of trade. The question is not for
me to solve. There are nearly 400 hotels in Sydney.
Is the number too great for the accommodation of
the people ? Is this a class of business which is not
fairly distributed, or are there conditions surrounding
a publican’s obligations which are not contained
amongst the liabilities of any other class of business ?
The fact seems to be that there is far more money sunk
in the liquor trade than can earn a decent return.
During the last few years, in order to “tie” houses
more thoroughly, the brewing co.’s have urged publicans
into a building programme of a bloated and Owe bulli-
vanesque character. There is really not the money in the
business to pay interest on these buildings, and on
brewery shares, and to allow the hotel-keeper a decent
living. , , , .
The extraordinary sequel to Col. Templeton’s
lone-drawn-out liquidation of the Melbourne Premier
Permanent Building Society threatens to provoke an
official inquiry into Its why and wherefore. Seems that
the end of the liquidation was only the beginning of legal
proceedings for the recovery of some ancient land boom
debts which Templeton set down as worthless so far as
creditors of the old society were concerned They were
sold at auction for £67 prior to the closing of the books,
the purchaser being one Taylor, who had acted as clerk
to the liquidator during 14 years of winding-up the
estate Now that Taylor is issuing writs against those
old debtors for the recovery of items ranging from about
a quarter of a million sterling to a few paltry thousands,
the question arises as to whether said Taylor can
possibly have bought a gold-mine for £6l. If it is good
enough for him, the ex-assistant liquidator, to squeeze
the old debtors in this way, how came it that Col. Temple-
ton left any blood in their liabilities before putting them
up to auction ? There is a fair case for an Insolvency
Court inquiry into the reason of this belated “ squeeze
Does accountant Taylor know that the nominal £700,000
of debts sold to him at auction are worth a lot more than
£67, and if Taylor knows it why didn’t liquidator Temple-
ton know it, and act accordingly ?

Envy hatred, and malice are raging in the
Stock Exchange of Melbourne over the new Board of
Works loan. One broker started off the mark early and
persuaded Fitzgibbon and his weaklings of the Board to
entrust him with the underwriting of the loan on terms
that set the mouth of every pawnshop owner watering—
a per cent, commission for getting other people to put
up money on practically a4J per cent, issue. Then came
the storm. First it was over a special quarter per cent,
allowance out of this com. This was fought out at a little
meeting of the broking syndicate which acts with the origi-
nator of the underwriting scheme. To his credit he held
on like grim death to the quarter, and, it is whispered,
got his way. Then, in the outside broking circle, a per-
fect hurricane raged over the whole loan. As the market
slumped the underwriter laughed, knowing that each
relapse was an obstacle out of his way, for the weaker the
market the more justified was the minimum of £9B. All
the howling against him, however, meant nothing. The
only way he could be countered was by the forming of a
rival syndicate to offer better terms But he had yarded
in all the big fry, and got the ears of the banks. So he
will score another triumph, and the ratepayers of the
metropolitan area will suffer.
Baker’s Creek, Hillgrove (N.S.W. ), last clean
up for a fortnight’s run was 150 oz. Manager SPGeorge
on this closed the battery till he heard from the directors
in Adelaide. Since then work has been resumed, but
with fewer hands. The manager is now developing the
lower levels ; if results are favorable, work will be re-
sumed as of yore. Tributors are, however, notified that
tributes will be let on the following terms : If stone
averages , sdwt. per ton ; loz., lOdwt., increasing
sdwt. for every ioz. Tributors who know what these
terms mean are keeping aloof, and talk of calling an
indignation meeting. All the same, many will take what
is offered. „

The rich chutes worked in former days by
Baker’9 Creek have gone into the New Hillgrove Pro-
Erietary Co.’s ground. This co., since reconstruction,
as done a lot of developing work by shaft sinking,
tunnelling, winzes, etc. The battery is practically com-
pleted, and will possibly start the middle of this month
and crush stone on hand. While this is being done, a
large air-compressor on hand will be erected. The erection
of the battery has dragged terribly. Scrip is locally held
tightly. At Hill grove, it is believed that a bear
crowd in Sydney doesn’t want the battery to start till it
can get all loose scrip from small holders. One local man
is said to hold 14,000 shares. The crushing should be
good, but the mine can never pay big divs., being over-
capitalised. Everyone in the place is promised a job
when the battery starts; many will be disappointed.
The place is overrun with city cadets seeking “practical
experience.” , * •
Eleanora mine (Hillgrove, N.S.W.) is now a
dilapidated wreck underground. Stopes have been
rooted all ways, and many of the levels are more or
less filled up with mullock. A local attempt made to
cyanide the tailings was a bad failure ; and the Australian
Gold Mining Co. has now the job on hand. The tailings
are averaging nearly 7dwt. to the ton. Last clean-up
from Eleanora vats, 2447 tons, yielded £1165 worth of
gold, and clean-up not complete.
Sunlight (Hillgrove, N.S.W.) is being worked
by tributors, who are making a good thing for the co.
and about wages for themselves.

«' M.C.” : Think there has been a miscalculation
with regard to the unprofitableness of handling B.H.
South Blocks ore (B. 25/2/ 04). Lead ore assaying 25 per
cent, with 4oz. silver per ton could be carted to one ot
the concentrating mills for, say, 2s. per ton. Most of the
Broken Hill co.’s are mining 15 per cent, lead ore with
lOoz. silver at a mining charge, exclusive of stores,
management, &c., of 10s. lOd. and upwards per ton.
The mill saves two-thirds of the lead (or 10 units), and
about half the silver (or about soz) ; intrinsic value (lead
at £lO, silver 2s. 6d.) 325. 6d. On the same basis of re-
covery, South Blocks ore would return 16 units of lead
with 2oz. of silver ; value, 375. Any of the co. s could co
better by buying South Blocks ore for 10s. per ton than
by mining relatively poorer ore. Notwithstanding this,
the South Blocks could not mine and crush its own ore to
advantage, nor could it mine and sell ore to another
co. at a profit. The whole secret of profit is economic
mining, and that the South Blocks is not down to as yet.
One other thing is certain : the South Blocks will not
require a concentrating plant for a long time to come.
The very worst thing that could happen would be an
imitation of the policy of the northern group of mines—
of a wasteful depletion of ore. Prospecting (for it is
nothing else at present) may disclose some ore good
enough to send direct to the smelter ; the shallow levels
have provided some surprises of that nature at Broken
Hill. Oxidised ore, rich in lead and valuable as a flux,
can, as a rule, be cheaply mined. A big find of such ore
would give the co. a lift. There is just the hope, perhaps
not a very big one, that “something may turn up, or
lead “ take a permanent jump.”
“ Sessil ” : Copeland (N.S.W.) mining field has
given a kick or two lately. It originally got a bad name
owing to the wiles of the untamed pussy, while of later
years the trail of the cocky miner has been over it. The
district is full cf lodes of varying promise, on one of the
biggest of which a number of parties have pegged out
above the Bowman River. A fair amount of work has
been done, and the reef, from 1 to sft., affords the most
promising development the field has seen for years. The
Upper Gloucester syndicate has a big body of fair grade
stone, which has averaged from 15dwt. to Joz. The lode is
proved to 250 ft. and is widening. Co. has a mile of tram,
way from paddock to water-wheel, and batter y of 5 head#
Pleasing feature of whole field is the available water
power. Jas. Irwin—who took £30,000 as a shareholder
from the Mountain Maid years ago—is back and about to
tackle the old lady once more. The Homeward Bound is
producing fair stone again; the Hidden Treasure has
crushed l2£ tons for an average of 4ioz. ; Grant and
party in the old Mint have raised several tons of very
rich stone ; the Prince Charlie has struck the old reef
100 ft. below old workings (same reef went from 15
weights upwards in the old days). So Copeland looks
like”reviving. Its great attractions : accessibility, cheap-
ness of provisions and splendid water-supply, and a
dimate. .

Mine-manager Sansom has left the Birthday
Tunnel, Berringa (Vic.). His successor is Hansen, of one
of the Bamganie shows. The two fields are most distinct
in natural features. At Berringa the lode formations are
wide and tremendously faulted, whereas at Bamganie
the lodes are narrow, and have not been proved to live
down. Meanwhile Tunnels find few sellers and fewer

The London-owned Duke United, Maryborough
(Vic ) after struggling for twelve months to make ends
meet and pay a quarterly 6d. dividend, has met better
ground, a patch of rich wash having been struck south of
the shaft and west of Sam Body’s old workings. As the
wash is making awav due west everyone is thinking that
it is a tributary of the Duke lead. Pinder, the manager,
accordingly has pegged out 8000 ft. of ground to the
south-west. The wash in the new ground is so close to
the old workings that it is a wonder it ever was missed.
Said that specks of gold show in it, so it is good dirt.
Pinder says there is 4000 fathoms drained ready for the
puddlers. This is not a big block ; but, if the dirt is rich,
it will sweeten up other ground north. W. A. Goldfields
people in London, who own the mine, sent a little fellow
named Jeffery out to control the Duke United, but he
struck snags everywhere—especially the Baillieu crowd—
and went back to London. Pinder then came out. He
is making a good fist of matters. W.A. Goldfields
(Wetherell and Stoneham) must be offering up prayers of
thankfulness in London atPinder’s success, as the W.A.G.
has had a hard row to hoe for many a day. Divs. from
any of their promotions will come in handy just now.
Bewick, Moreingand Co., too, will be duly appreciative
at the removal of an alluvial lame duck from the London
market just when they are preaching investment in deep-
lead # • •
The Main Leads North (Vic.) affair is not quite
settled. Out of the secret reserve of 3290 z. the directors
have appropriated 200 oz. for the purposes of a dividend.
Just work out the position for their benefit. The reserve
has been in existence while the co. had an overdraft. It
was in existence when shares were down to 6s. and when
people were quitting for fear of calls. What will these
unfortunates say 7—indeed, what is their legal position ?

Turned up lately, in a Melbourne court, a
reminder of the little Jook’s visit to Australia,
some time last century—or was it the year before
that ?—in the shape of a German sausage-maker,
who handed to the Bench a document from
Government House, stating that Hans’ sausages
were as filling and soothing to the stomach as the
British article, and that little York had much
enjoyed them. As the testimonial got Hans out
of a little difficulty about being on a strange
person’s premises, at least one man is grateful for
his Royal Nibs’ visit and his liking for the
mysterious edible that can’t be whistled to with
impunity.

“ Chass: Watched the fire-fighters at a
recent Ballarat fire. The railway men under the
stationmaster’s guidance ran out the railway hose
with lightning speed, and in a brace of shakes a
red-coated fireman was on the roof of a neighbor-
ing house with his squirt pointed at a blazing
window. But he had not calculated on Tommy
Bents economy, neither had the crowd. An
official turned on the pressure water, and in
a twinkling 50 jets were playing on the crowd
instead of on the fire ; while the rush of water
through the biggest rent in the hose knocked a
youth flat on his back. The crowd’s ironical
jeering of the railway hose and Tommy Bent
prepare one for the thud about June next.

The Victorian honorary magistrate is a
wonderful character in his small way. Lately at
Prahran (Melbourne), two factory inspectors
prosecuted a jam-maker on a charge of having a
loathsome and unclean factory. This was some of
the evidence:—
Thomas Powell, inspector of factories, stated that in
company with Miss Mead, inspectress of factories, he, on
19th ult., visited defendants’ business premises. They
were in a very dirty condition. In the canning, pickling
and pulping rooms there was a great deal of slush. Girls
stood in the filth—pulp, dirt and other matter. In the
pulping room four girls were standing in liquid matter an
inch and a half deep. The manager, Mr. Palfryman
shook his head, admitted that it was “ pretty had,'” and
Slid he would have it altered. On the stairs leading to
the dining-room there was filth ; in the dining-room
i'self it was impossible to see the floor on account of the
dirt. There was a bad smell in the pickling room.
That was a pretty good case, but suddenly the
chief J.P. began to give evidence from the bench.
He said that, at the invitation of the factory
manager, he had visited the place and found it
beautifully clean. Which he naturally would do ;
even the manager of the dirtiest factory would’
clean it up before in viting his friend, the J.P., to
come and look at it in order to influence him
in a forthcoming trial. The case was dismissed
by reason of the evidence given by the Bench to
itself when it wasn’t on oath. At latest Chief
Sec. Gillott was intending to carry this matter
further, so something unpleasant may happen to
the J.P. ; but nothing that may occur to him is
likely to be half as sudden as it ought to be. A
J.P. who, apparently, allows himself to be pri-
vately interviewed by a defendant on whose case
he is to adjudicate, ought to be struck off the roll
without further notice, and if a charge of mal-
feasance of justice will lie it ought to be thrown
at him without delay.
* * * *
Woman is gradually emerging from serf-
dom. In the divorce court at Sydney, a few days
ago, a lady obtained a decree nisi on the ground
t lat her husband did not support her, that he
treated her with contumely and tore her clothes,
and that he used to burn her hair-brush in the
middle of the starry night, and wake her by
holding the flaming implement in her face. Tin's
not only terrified her, but also left her without
the means of doing 1 her back-hair in the morning.
On top of his other misdeeds, he demanded that
she should cut his toe-nails, and when she
declined to abase herself he used to kick her with
the uncut nails in bed. The Judge’s decision has
at least one definite, good effect. Woman may
now consider that she is not bound to prostrate
herself before a partially undressed husband and
cut his toe - nails. In Japan woman is still a
crushed being who only feeds with her husband
by his lordly permission, and who walks behind
him, and rushes to open the door for him ; but the
Jap may keep that social system for his own
consumption.

of the deceased. A dress

funeral the driver of the
mourning coach waits until the
mourners have undressed, and
carries back the garb of woe in
the coach.

A minor collision between Sydney ferry
steamers, just investigated by the Marine Court,
resulted in astern reprimand to one of the masters
involved. The skipper had left the wheel while
he collected passengers’ fares. The only available
person to take the wheel was the raw deck hand
and another steamer popping out in front of him!
the boy lost his head and was run down. Now, if
the master does not collect the fares he is sacked ;
the ferry owners say they can’t afford another
system of collecting fares, so the fault is wholly
theirs. Yet nothing was said to them by the
comfc, which rested satisfied with threatening 1 the
luckless master with the loss of his ticket if he
came up again with a similar explanation. It is
a pity that the court doesn’t find itself struck by a
peremptory command to be in two places at a
time under pain of losing its billet if it isn’t there.
1 hen the court might be more reasonable. The
court is so slow that it takes it all it knows to be
in one place at a time. Sometimes it doesn’t
manage that, and often it is no use when it is
there—and not much ornament.
Sometimes, it seems, the Australian is too
modest to become a hero. While at a picnic a
young girl fell out of a boat on the Macquarie
River (N.S.W.), and was allowed to drown in full
view .of a crowd of young men and women.
Questioned afterwards, some of the young fellows
said that they were not good swimmers ; others
said they could swim, but “did not like undressing
in the presence of so many young ladies.”
• » » *
N.S.W. officialdom has mixed itself up
over the Shaftesbury Reformatory for Girls, at
South Head. For economical and other reasons
it was recently deemed fit to close this old estab-
lishment, and it was so closed under the hand of
John See—at least, so John See imagined. The
inmates were removed to other institutions. The
furniture and fixings were handed over to the
departments that could best use them. The tele-
phone was cut off. The staff was appointed else-
where—except the matron, who was left “on
leave of absence,” until something could be found
for her, in charge of the building. Just when
everything was fixed up, a back-blocks magistrate
committed a girl thief to Shaftesbury. He had
heard nothing of the closing of the place.
Neither had the constable, who duly handed the
girl over to the matron, and she, though Shaftes-
bury was legally non-existent, took her in. She
could hardly do anything else. Here was a
situation ! The girl was not in Shaftesbury Re-
formatory, as that institution was wiped out. But
the law had committed her there, and, as a matter
of fact, there she was. The Crown Law office
people were appealed to in vain. So John See,
after proving to the satisfaction of himself that
the youngster was not really in Shaftesbury,
solved the difficulty by getting an order-in-Counci I
to take her out of it. Just when everyone had
sighed with relief at settling the question, word
was received that another girl, committed by
another up-country magistrate, had arrived safe
and sound at South Head. Then John See fled
to Maoriland, and left his deputy to struggle
with the question.

HIM: “ Oh, I say, you have changed since last

Concerning that 40-year-old letter refer-
ring to the discovery of gold between the Roper
and Limmenbight Rivers (N.T.) found in an old
safe in Adelaide Crown Lands office a couple
of weeks _ ago, it lias just transpired that
another important document was enclosed
with it. This is the printer’s MS. of John
M'Douall Stuart’s diary of his journey from
Chambers’ Creek to the North-west in 1800. In
face of these discoveries it would be worth while
to send an explorer through the various Govern-
ment offices. Even the bones of Leichhardt may
be hidden in some dusty cupboard, or a long-
absent surplus may be dropped upon.

It seems that Melbourne in general had
been looking at certain Mutoscope pictures with
an undiscerning eye. As far as the public could
judge from penny peeps, the Mutoscopes failed to
fulfil their promise of impropriety. “Why
Marie Blew the Light Out” was a bitter disap-
pointment; “The Temptation of St. Anthony”
didn’t go near to realising the worst hopes of a
sinful city. Melbourne, in fact, considered the
Mutoscope Co. rather guilty of obtaining pennies
under false pretences until a local detective seized
Marie the light-blower, and Tony the tempted,
and proved their obscenity to the satisfaction of
Panton, P.M., and other magistrates at the City
Court. One ordinary-minded “beak” couldn’t see
where the obscenity came in, but his opinion was
over-ruled by the Pantonic purists. A fine of £lO
was imposed upon the occupier of the premises
where Marie blew the light out, and Marie now
remains in the hands of the police pending an
appeal against Panton’s decision. Should that
decision be upset on appeal, Melbourne will
resume its original grievance against the Muto-
scope, but if the verdict of Panton is confirmed,
Public Morality will merely bewail the fact that
it was shockingly outraged without knowing it.
* * * *
Kronheimer, the Hebrew gentleman who
offered £5OOO for the establishment of a consump-
tive wing at any of the Victorian hospitals, still
continues to get a good deal of fame out of “his
most generous donation.” At present the Kron-
heimer placard is hung on a picturesque dispute
between the committee of the Austin Hospital
and Tommy Bent. The Austin Hospital wants to
get the £5OOO and build a new wing for the treat-
ment of consumptives. Before he will give his
consent, Bent wants a guarantee that when the
wing is erected the Austin Hospital won’t come
at the Government for additional maintenance
grants. The wrangle has been going on for six
months, and it is getting on to Bent’s nerves—or,
at any rate, on to his meat. In an outburst of
anger he told the committee that Kronheimer’s
gift was more bother than it was worth, and that
Kronheimer had only given the money in order
to .obtain a sort of permanent advertisement.
VhiE aroused the Argus to indignation, and a
leader appeared taking Bent to task severely for
the gruffness of his language. The writer heaved
a bit of Shakspeare at Tommy, who replied in
kind, asking, “Shall quips and sentences, and
these paper bullets of the brain awe a man from
the humor of his career?” Bent quoting Shaks-
peare is like a pork butcher making sweet music
in the slaughter-yard.

Judging by the frenzied appeals that Zion
is making for money, it seems as if Dowie might
get back to his muddy Paradise in Illinois to find
his church in liquidation. The cry which the
“Overseer-in-Charge” has issued to the Zionists
is to the general effect that the Cause is almost up
a tree and can’t pay its bills, and that unless the
Zionists hand over 20 per cent, of their earnings
to keep Dowie’s bloated speculation going, it is
impossible to say what may happen. The Bulle-
tin hopes it will be present to hear Dowie orate
the night Zion bursts. The language of that
short, thick man is terrible on very ordinary
occasions, but his shriek in presence of a really
large calamity should be an effort calculated to
turn a bullock-driver green with envy.

An unusual tale of prison repentance comes
from Westralia. Some months ago a thief, one
Abbott, got three years for stealing £2OO, which
he hid before capture and which remained undis-
covered. Recently the prosecutor died and left
his family in poverty. On the facts being made
known to the prisoner he volunteered to give up
the money, and led the police to King’s Park,
where the coin was unearthed from the foot of
the Soldiers’ Memorial. The usual prison repen-
tance is mostly a cheap one, but this seems
genuine. N.B.—Note that the stolen money was
naturally planted in the congenial company of the
Soldiers’ Memorial.

A Melbourne pickpocket who tried to loot
a gorgeous Chow of his trinkets fell in badly. Ah
Sam was parading Little Bourke-street with a
noble walking-stick, a diamond-set scarf pin, a
huge gold dog-chain and other jewels, when a man
pounced upon him, to find that the wily Chinee
had his jewellery sewn on to his clothes and
protected by an array of fish-hooks. The would-
be thief had his hand cut and his head broken
with the stick; and that was all his gain.
* * * *
Methodist Church in N.S.W. at last
Conference showed a hankering after “en-
richment of service.” It wants to put on
ruffles and lace and knee-breeches, and
ritual, and have trills in its church music.
So progresses the evolution of all religions.
A religion starts with a preacher of wild,
hairy legs and fanatical zeal, who foams at
the mouth as he talks and sees visions as he
sleeps. Such a preacher tells of the joys of
the next world and inveighs against the
luxuries and the sins of this, and many
people follow him, either because they
haven’t a fair share of the luxuries and sins
and hope to even up things in the Hereafter,
or because they have had too much luxury
and sin and, their stomachs having gone
back on them, austerity promises a wel-
come change. That is the beginning of a
church. But by-and-by the novelty of the
thing wears off, and the original prophet is
succeeded by men who foam in a perfunctory
way, and the live frenzy of religion is suc-
ceeded by the cold, dead woodenness of
theology. Then the church drifts back to
the worldly path, and reckons that, after
all, it is best to make the most of this
world. And, to provide some substitute
for the old zeal, and the vanished hairy
legs, and the foam that has disappeared
from the smooth countenance of the plump
preacher, the church ventures on ritual arid
vestments and glittering music. Consider
the Scotchbyterianism of the wild moor
men of Scotland in the 17th century and
the Presbyterianism of Dill Macky’s ele-
gant knee breeches and lace ruffles 1 Con-
sider even—to take a creed with a much
shorter career—the Salvationism of 20 or
30 years ago as compared with the glossy
bell-topper of Booth to-day 1
H.M.S. Rapid is being paid off, having
been at last condemned as not class enough for
even an Australian warship; and so has been
lying in Farm Cove during the week flying the
“Paying-off whip.” This piece of Naval tom-
foolery consists of an immensely long narrow
white ribbon, which flies from the main topmast
far over the vessel’s stern and finally dabbles in the
water. While some distinctive signal was, no
doubt, thought necessary at Portsmouth and else-
where, in days gone by, when a crew was getting
its arrears of pay, a vital matter to many on shore,
the only excuse for clinging to the whip, to-day,
is the conservative nature of the navy, wherever
it is able to hang on to its foolishnesses.

The other day a Townsville (Q.) hospital
took in a Chinaman suffering from two wounds in-
flicted by balls from a pea-rifle. It was alleged

said to another—“lt’s a dull day; let’s go and
shoot a Chinaman !’’ John himself averred that
the enterprising pair had demanded pineapples or
blood, and had shot at him promptly on his
, refusal to part with the fruit. The contiguity of
Asiatic aliens breeds a strange moral tone in
surrounding Caucasians.

Matrimonial diary of an English woman
who obtained a divorce from a naval lieutenant
the other day :
Wedding Day.—Bridegroom arrived home at 3 a.m.
Second Day.—Bridegroom took a sleeping draught.
Third Day.—Oame home “ three parts drunk.”
Fourth Day. —Went to China for three years.
That was all the bliss that was got into the
wedding contract, and the court had no hesitation
in granting a rule nisi.

The advent of the somewhat democratic
Morgan-Browne Ministry has had at least one
satisfactory result up
North. Under the Con-
tinuous Ministry every
black-and-tan alien who
could borrow or steal a £lO
note got a hawker’s license
without demur. Now Ab-
dul Hooshta or Ram Bosh
has to be naturalised by
the Australian Govern-
ment before the local
C.P.S. will look at his
greasy tenner, and the pro-
cess is a slow one. As
a result, the white hawker
gets a fair chance, and
the new regime is cursed
in Siamese and Cochin
China, and Brahmapootra
and Mahunuddy, and all
manner of tea-chest lingo.

• * * *
Bent’s railways (strictly so-called) possess
some oddities with great possibilities about them.
From Essendon to Broad meadows runs a short
train drawn by a loco, which looks like a pony.
One man stokes, drives, and looks after the road,
and is captain and crew all in one. From Colac
to Beeac a twelve-mile run is made by a “ Shang-
hai ” engine—no guard, the driver in sole charge ;
no lights provided in the carriage, and all book-
ing done at Colac. On the N.E. line a goods train
with a passenger ear leaves Essendon on Sunday
nights. The passenger car was built in England,
and imported by the Hobson’s Bay railway com-
pany. Bent dug this curiosity up, dusted it,
chipped off the rust, and it keeps upright.

N.S.W. Arbitration Court has decided
that it will not enforce the payment of levies
imposed for the purpose of carrying on strikes,
but may for lock-outs. The point was determined
in connection with the coal-mining trouble in
Victoria; the N.S.W. unions are helping the
men who are locked out.
* * # *
John Davidson in Macmillan’s Magazine
on the Canadian prohibition town, Fredericton :
Whisky is sold as openly as tea. There is regular, and
apparently adequate provision for domestic drinking.
There are fifteen or sixteen places where drink is usually
sold and it can be obtained without any formalities. The
regular dealers, who are well known, are fifteen or sixteen
in number. They include all the druggists, most of the
hotels and billiard saloons, and some places that make
hardly any pretence of doing any other business. It is
all conducted in a perfectly straightforward way. The
worst effect is that the system has given rise to a large
amount of drinking among young boys of sixteen and
eighteen. No one can question what a boy is doing in a
druggist’s at the soda-water fountain. A lad of that
age would not dare to enter a saloon, except by stealth ;
but he can boldly drink in the most public way at the
soda fountain.
Prohibition thus doesn’t prohibit, though it checks
disorderly drunkenness; but, as a set-off to that,
It encourages juvenile drinking.
A letter from Longstaff shows what a
hollow fraud King-painting is. Longstaff, it
turns out, didn’t have a single sitting from his
subject until after he had painted the picture for
which ex-Gov. Beauchamp gave him a com-
mission, and which is intended for Sydney Art
Gallery. Even then he didn’t have a real sitting.
He took the painted and framed picture to
Buckingham Palace, the King rushed in, looked
at it, and said that the orders on the breast were
not quite right:—
I explained to him that I had not seen them, so conse-
quently could hardly be expected to paint them correctly.
The King* then turned to his equerry and instructed him
to “ send Mr. to me at once.” This latter individual
came immediately, bowing low. The King then asked
me what I would like. I told him that I wanted the gold
chain, the four orders worn on the breast, the sash, also
an elaborate gold chain worn round the neck, with an
enamel Maltese cross. The King, addressing the official,
said : “Get those for Mr. Longstaff at once.” Turning
to me, he said, “ Where would you like them ? ” to which
I replied, “At my studio.” The King said, “ Oh, very
well; be careful of them, and let me have them back
safely by Saturday.” He also gave orders for me to take
his military boots and spurs, and field-marshal’s hat.
So a picture of a king consists of an accurate
representation of gold chains, sashes, orders,
enamel crosses, military boots, spurs, and hat;
the man inside all this furniture doesn’t count.
The artist may draw upon his imagination and his
“ loyalty ” for that trifling detail. There are no
warts on the trimmings ; there may be on the one
that wears them. And kings, unlike Cromwells,
are not painted with warts.

Trial Bay (N.S.W.) prison buildings, now
deserted, might surely be traded off to some
religious sect—some prophet or other who
could take his flock there and form a colony.
The prison cells are specially suited for religious
hermits; there is no township near to push its
lusts and vanities under the noses of the saints;
and the prison authorities might throw in some
rusty leg-irons, so that, any refractory adherent
could be chained up in a dark cellar or a tank.
As a working plant for a new religion Trial Bay
building has many good points.

RESURRECTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VVTien wake the dead on Resurrection morn,
And stir at sound of Gabriel’s golden horn,
When its clear note, imperative and loud,
Summons each sleeper wrapped within the shroud,
Still in my arms, dream that it is not dawn.
Dream on, nor rise and leave me all forlorn,
For whom no life in heaven will then be born ;
Your fair white face by mine lie still and proud,
When the dead wake.
So shall we lie beneath some poppied lawn
And, smiling in our sleep, hold heaven in scorn.
We, who for all eternity are vowed,
Will stay behind the skyward-speeding crowd
And rouse but just enough to kiss and yawn
When the dead wake
H. OLDCLARE.

I do not yield myself to sorrow ;
There cannot be, whate’er befall,
A better day until t o-morrow.

RAINCOATS.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 15, 1904.
My Dear Arini,—
An interesting marriage-to-come announcement
is that of Staff-surgeon Chaworth Louis Nolan,
R.N., son of the late Dr. Andrew Nolan, of
Wicklow, Ireland, and Miss Helen Darley, of
Enniskerry, in the same county—-a niece of Chief
Justice Darley of N.S. Wales. Surgeon Nolan is
a nephew of William Saurin Lyster, of Australian
operatic fame. The latter was a son of Captain
Chaworth Lyster, and a godson of William Saurin,
a famous Attorney-General for Ireland. The
bride’s grandfather was also a lawyer, holding a
high office in the Court of Chancery in Ireland.
Lady Rawson’s galah parrot has not been re-
covered, in spite of the £5 reward. Mr. Share,
p.-s., and Mr. Robertson Clarke have got so used
to looking upward into tree-tops for the fowl that
they have acquired quite a devotional cast of eye.
They have, it is rumored on the worst authority,
climbed every tree in Rose Bay, spoiled £l5
worth of clothes, used £5 worth of language, and
finally, in desperation, think of asking the
Attorney-General for a search-warrant against
every person who has a tree on his premises where
a galah might be secreted. The bird did not talk,
I’m told, and it could not sing, and galahs are not
pretty, so why did Lady Rawson love it ? Perhaps
it was good, and had a sweet disposition. I saw
a galah, the other day, in Dutchy’s Monkey Shop,
at the Circular Quay end of George-street, which
I believe was the missing fowl. It had a down-
ward expression, evidently acquired through long
consideration of searching aides-de-camps from
tall trees, and it looked down at the same angle
at which the aides now habitually look up, which
is conclusive.
The Japanese Consul in Sydney is being
consulted by manager Vincent in the rehearsals
of the Jap play “Darling of the Gods,” to be
produced before Easter. It is a very difficult
play for European actors to accomplish. The
English woman walks erect with her toes turned
out, the Jap lady Has to progress with a stoop,
and inturned toes, and her elbows close to her
sides, and arms hanging so as to make the shoul-
ders as slim as possible. Then the sitting down
has to be learnt. Japs sit on their heels,
and if you want to find out how stiff and muscle-
weary this pose makes you, try it for ten minutes.
In drinking tea, women drink from the side of
the cup just over the handle ; men hold their arms
in such a position as to drink from the side of the
cup furthest from the handle. Then the sexes
kneel and bow differently. All these customs
bring into play, when Europeans practise them,
entirely unexpected sets of muscles. In Eng-
land, after half-a-dozen rehearsals, Beerbohm
Tree’s co. used to complain of being so stiff that it
could hardly crawl. And yet people who work
under Tree, and keep in that manager’s good
graces, are said to have solid crawling experience
In some English women’s papers I see
picture faces wearing the new three-cornered hat
—the hat like the headgear of Napoleon, Nelson,
and Dick Turpin and ,r Jnote that the
cunning artist has put a hint of the Napole-
onic type, or the kiss-me,-Hardy expression, or a
highwayrnannish look into the faces. They are
not the empty, young, pussy-cat faces that usually
go under the latest thing in millinery, and it is
wonderful how the slight, old-acquaintance mien
of hat and face prejudices one in favor of the hat.
Then one tries on the “tricorne,” and—well,
some of us are disillusioned. Talking of hats
reminds me that the young widow of a Sydney
barrister has set up as a milliner, with a
lovely lot of Paris models, in George-street,
under the style of Madame Louise. Soon after
her husband’s death the lady went in for a regular
millinery training at Sydney’s two principal
shops. , .
The “tricorne” is new enough but not so
day-after-to-morrow as the “Torpedo.” InLondon
and Paris some decadents are wearing hats trimmed
with snakes. Eve’s friend is made of tiny, shaded
feathers, in bronze green, which look like real scales.
Owls’ heads are seen on some turbans to be worn
with tailor-mades. Among the new millinery
now on view are some jaunty French felts turned
up all round with cream silk braid, and bird-
quilled front or side. A toque which might illus-
trate “Night and Morning” had silver roses
embroidered on cloudy black, with trails of
chenille and a black and silver osprey. Red hats,
with a crown as big as a harvest moon, and two
inches of brim if seen from above, and as wide as
some suburban pavements underneath, are evi-
dently going to have a great vogue. Emerald
green and navy blue are used together on the new
millinery ; and the new chiffon velvet—an ethereal
fabric —has got an artistic hold on a lot of toques.
Beaver cloth is another strokeable thing of which
hats are wholly fashioned, and its effect is wonder-
fully softening to hard faces. Some faces, you
know, are hard. Not every woman has a face to
fit the Swinburnism “made out of a rose.”
Women buying hats are interesting studies. An
American humorist tells of the daughter of a man
who “got on ” that she ceased wearing hats and
took to ‘confections.’ “Shebought them languidly,
three at a time.” A local “confectioner” says
that Mrs. Kelly (the American Madame Butter-
fly) is the easiest woman to hat she ever met,
because she knows at ten paces and without trying
on what will suit her. A few days ago she bought
three hats in five minutes. Most women would
make a whole forenoon job of it.
Curious how the “Sign of the Cross ” (put on
at Her Majesty’s on Saturday) is gushed over by
women (well, some women) and disliked by men.
The scene in “S. of the C.” that men select for
special sneers is one which appeals to women
(well, some women) —in which Marcus Superbus
chivvies the Christian maiden around his premises,
until a thunder peal gives him pause, and reminds
him of the brevity of his garments and the sinful-
ness of his intentions. Women (again, some
women) like their romance with broad effects.
In this play they get them broad and strong
and florid. A girl confided to me that
“ she hated Mercia for being such a snow-
ball, and that if she had been in her place
the after tragedy would never have occurred.”
Also, another young briar-rose confided to me
that she “liked Julius Knight’s playing of the
scene so much better than Wilson Barrett’s,
because the former catches Mercia occasionally
which Barrett never seemed to do.” Unsym-
pathetic missie ! She didn’t make allowances for
the fact that Wilson B. is an ancient gentleman
who hobbles on to the stage in elevators and
can’t be expected to catch any girl who is at
all active. After that, whilst taking standing
exercise at the back of the circle, a man friend
sized up the play as “claptrap and thunderclap.’’
Which was, I suppose, the truth. But the lives
of the majority of the women of Suburbia (it is
Suburbia which goes to the “Sign of the Cross”)
are so destitute of romance—the narrow things of
the house, and the teething, measles, whooping
cough and the rest pall so much—that they wel-
come the fierce, garish romance of plays like the
“ Sign of the Cross.”
It is a glittery play, and women, “like moths,
are ever caught by the glare” (at least, Byron
says they are), and the shining Roman soldiers,
and Roman eagles, the fasces, ourule chairs and
laurel chaplets revive one’s childhood’s dreams of
the grandeur that was Rome.
Not many well-known faces in the auditorium
at “ Sign of the Cross ” on Saturday. The best-
looking people seemed to be in the stalls. Two
very fair-headed girls down there had what looked
like something new in the way of theatre coats—
in chiffon velvet, ivory white, with ruffle on
ruffle of black and white chiffon and perpen-
dicular strappings of narrow, pastel-blue ribbon
velvet. Miss von Arnheim and a young Fairfax
wife—the former in white, with a half-crown of
green leaves, the latter in gauzy black —looked a
Howard Chandler Christy pair. Mr. E. W.
O’Sullivan looked on from a box as if he
were Laying Ancient Rome, or thinking
what a splendid “resumption” could be got
out of the grove by the Cestian Bridge—the
place where the insanitary-looking Christians
met to sing “yims.” Two girls in front row of
stalls exhibited the new hairdressing. If it gets
to be popular it will prove as unpopular with men
as is the matinee hat. These girls had built up
their beautiful hair almost a foot above their
enterprising faces. It gave them a hair-brained
loolu
Mrs. Burdekin and a fine-feather group were
also boxers. It was this lady’s last public
appearance in Sydney for a year to come. She
and her one unmarried daughter and two sons are
now on the Orontes, en route to London, where
the boys will go to Oxford and Eton respec-
tively.
If you ha /e seen Miss Lilian Foulis’ portrait,
especially that one on the post-card “English Cele-
brities” series, you will be struck by the likeness
to Mrs. Maesmore Morris—the Mrs. M. M. of some
years ago. But the violinist’s face has strength
and character, and her large, heavy-lidded grey
eyes seem ever to be looking beyond, at some-
thing back of the morning star —beyond platform,
audience and all, far into the dim Where-may-it-
be where the box office receipts are being counted.
It was a look that Melba and almost every
visiting musical genius had. Bhe is a tall, dark-
haired lassie, Australian, though born in Scot-
land (her father was a Sydney doctor), and made
her debut at ten years. To-day, in her early
twenties, she is mentioned in company with
Kubelik and Madame Halle. Her great grand-
father, Isaac Nathan, was a composer of note,
who came to Sydney when it was a young city,
and they used to go to Government house in a
bullock dray and count their wealth by their
hogsheads of rum.
Miss Foulis comes here well-lettered to Aus-
tralian Governors. This week her cousin, Mr.
Charles Nathan, and Chancelloress MacLaurin
(who was a Nathan) are giving her and her
mother, who accompanies her, a primrose time.

No one would ever guess from its unpretentious
front, of the splendid interior of Baumann’s new
premises in Pitt-street, opposite Hordern’s. There
is a ballroom floor laid on springs, with supper
and cloak rooms upstairs, and the banquetting-
hall, restaurant, private rooms and lounge will,
when they are in working order, help to make
Sydney life better worth living. The bagasse
ceilings, stained windows, and carved woodwork
and marble halls and walls (the marble, in various
kinds, has been quarried in our own country) have
all been wrought by cunning artificers. Mr. W.
Baumann went through Europe lately, and
brought back some wonderful things in the way
of stoves, and I am not sure that you don’t get
your potato cooked by radium.

Moore at the first opportunity.
Kathleen, third daughter of late brewer Edward

name would stand a lot of uniform. Miss Fitz-
gerald was in Sydney some five years ago with
her sister, Mrs. Prendergast. Like the latter,
i'ie is fair and good-figured. Since her widow-
hood Mrs. Prendergast has lived with her sister,
Mrs. Gordon, in Vic., where Colonel Maria
