An unaccountable cablegram from London is
to the effect that the Geelong Fire Brigade has
presented its bronze medal to the Hon. Charlotte
Knollys, for waking up the Queen when her
Sandringham house was recently on fire. This
statement is unaccountable in various ways.
What has Geelong to do with the matter, to
begin with ? Then why should Geelong, which
is usually a very slow place, and seldom
arrives till the middle of next week, be
so lost in admiration of a lady who rushed
(Geelong never rushes) and woke somebody up
(waking up is contrary to all Geelong traditions) ?
Again, is it so unusual a thing to wake up a
burning monarch that the proceeding has to be
encouraged with a medal containing at least six-
penn’orth of metal? And seeing that the business
of a fire brigade is to pour water over burning
objects, why should it go off its beat to interfere
with a different department of the business?
There are other mysteries, but these strike me as
extra mysterious.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“An Englishwoman, long resident in Australia,
has just returned from a jaunt abroad, and relates
her impressions of the Old World, revisited. The
Australians, she says, are the cleanest people she
knows—because everywhere else the water-tax
seems to be a bogy. In London she paid a
shilling a bath, in America a dollar. She asked
the Americans staying in the hotels, ‘ How ever
do you afford one dollar every day for a bath ?
Why that’s 28s. per week?’ They said, ‘We do
without baths.’ In certain French towns all the
water is carried from the public pump, and the
boardinghouse-keeper would go mad if boarders
used it for anything but drinking. In
England the returned wanderer found the
friends of her youth ‘Church-mad.’ Eng-
land, to-day, gets up in the middle of the
night for‘vigils.’ Fasting is fashionable, and, at
church, the curate grovels on the floor, so that
hearers can’t think of the service because of study-
ing the soles of his boots. The meanest person
met abroad was a French boardinghouse-keeper,
who enlivened every meal by talk of the gluttony
of English people. This lady provided for break-
fast a loaf of bread the size of a cart-wheel and a pat
of butter as big as a wedding-ring, and you had to
make the two disproportionate elements coincide
in a meal as best you could. The far-famed
‘ French coffee ’ proved a pale, straw-colored dis-
appointment. Madame provided no matches.
Boarders were expected to ignite their candles
from a light kept burning downstairs.
“ This newly-returned wanderer tells that she
took stock of Queen Alexandra at an Ascot race-
meeting, where the royal personage spent most of
her day bowing and smiling, puppet-like, in a
glass pen, erected by the Ascot management for
the display of show potentates. That day, the
aspect of the ‘Viking’s daughter’ ( vide Tennyson)
was that of an exquisite wax figure, magnificent
in long pelisse of ermine, and toque to match, a
tall osprey towering at the side. But observers,
knowing the pink-cheeked, blue-eyed, golden-
haired poseuse to be no marionette, but a flesh -
and-blood grandmother of various sorrows, saw
something weird in her belated youthfulness,
something pathetic in her mechanical smile. John
Bull and his womenkind rather decry Alexandra’s
insipid, if decorative, amiability. ‘ She’s a pretty
nonentity—not a patch on the plucky old Queen,’
say they, forgetting that, for 30 years, masterful
Victoria’s understudy was permitted to be nothing
but ‘pretty,’ and to do little, beyond reading
French novels and planning Parisian bonnets.
Even her children were less her children than
Royal heirs, born to suit the succession and sacri-
ficed to its exigencies. A story, widely circulated
in England, has it that Alexandra, then Princess
of Wales, wearing deep mourning for her lost
boy, went down on her knees in vain prayers to
her mother in-law when the latter decreed that
the suppliant’s surviving son must take up all his
dead brother’s responsibilities, private as well as
public.
“ The present Princess of Wales is not exactly
■popular in England, but she’s approved of. ‘May
is hard —like the old Queen,’ says current tradi-
tion. Her husband has little hold on the nation,
but, so long as his popular papa is to the fore, it’s
a matter of only minor importance that the heir-
apparent has inherited neither his mother’s looks,
nor his father’s spirits.
“Commandant Hutton’s fair dame flourished
the greatest dress of the Melbourne Club hop. It
was a diaphanous affair of soft silk veiled in fleecy
white chiffon, on which blazed out large opulent
flowers, their gold being matched by a sovereign-
toned bolero. The pale mint color of the hair
made for more cash effect, while the Hutton dia-
monds perched proudly on the golden apex. Two
other matrons. Mrs. Pursey Chirnside and Mrs.
Everard Browne made display of gems of the first
water, but they were all beaten by the glare of
the great bandeau of single stones that blazed
from Lady Northcote’s head-tresses. For my own
part, I am not so struck with the diamonds of
great size as are most of my sex. It seems to me
that a diamond should wink, prettily and con-
fidentially, at the on-looker, not glare at him (or
her) like the head-light of a tram-car.
“The casual yachting girl of these parts was
out tracking round after the Bona and La
Carabine in Hobson’s Bay on Saturday. A race
was supposed to be afloat, but after watching Mr.
Wallace awhile at the wheel of the graceful Bona,
with a business and a nautical expression in his
weather eye, and looking on the brothers Clarke
at the head of a great crew carelessly finger-
ing the steering gear of sporty La Cara-
bine, the yachting girl concluded that the
race was off. So far the sea-charmer of Hob*
son’s Bay has no special rigging of her own.
She is addicted to picture hats and open-air yokes,
to say nothing of deck-trailing skirts and fal-
lals. Not one of the second sex, either on the
cruisers or the steam-tug, was fitted up with proper
sailing-gear.

“Miss Lilian Foulis, who fiddles divinely (so
advance notices state) on a genuine Stradivarius,
is given the best credentials for amiability and
good manners by her fellow-passengers. She
practised four hours at a stretch, without a break
for the frequent ship snack, in her cabin, and then
brought fiddle and bow to the saloon whenever
requested.
“All the talk anent physical culture of the
Sandow type, and Melbourne’s open - eyed
wonder at it, annoys long-established dancing
teachers, whose practice it is to get the beginner’s
muscles hardened and limbs supple before
initiating the toes into the showy pirouette or
the festive barn-hop. ‘Why, all dancing is, in
effect, physical culture,’ says one dancing teacher,
‘and only the grossly ignorant can hail the
Sandow cult for women as a novelty.’
“The Northcotes are springing new frills of
office upon the populace. The latest surprise
is a pennant flying ahead of the driving Y.R.
procession. An outrider canters in front, bearing
the Union Jack in full sail before the Excellencies.
Down at Footscray, where the violent odors dwell,
when they saw this, the cottagers reckoned a circus
was coming; and Footscray was, on the whole,
not far out.

“Surgeon T. N. Fitzgerald has left his private
hospital to take his sick self to Colombo. For
weeks he has been endeavoring to drop his lancets
and give his health a badly-needed airing. He
left it a trifle too long, and almost had to abandon
the trip at Adelaide.

“Daily advertisement columns promise fat for-
tunes to canvassers. Occasionally a really
taking, popular line spells success for the tout,,
and that without much trouble. Hereabouts-
a young widow does good business as traveller for
a summer corset, invented in Queensland, andi
warranted to ‘ wash and boil. ’ It’s made mostly
of washing elastic, and, in place of bones, there-
are pliable chain-wires that slip in and out on
washing-day without scissoring or needling.
“ A Melbourne bride-elect has been kept busy
telling her friends that she is not marrying
millions. ‘We’ll be cosy,’ she says with a bland
smile, ‘ but we won’t wallow in wealth. With so
much a year one cannot run motor-stables and a
swell stud. ’ Fancy having to stop the babble of
gossips by submitting the bank-book !

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The father of S.A. Assembly, William
Gilbert had his 75th birthday on Feb. 23.
The death has occurred at Ballarat (Vic.)
of Mrs. Sword, widow of the late Emanuel 3word,
and niece of novelist Walter Scott. She arrived
m Victoria in ’52, and was 74 years of age.
Of the three N.S. Wales barristers who
have just taken silk, Alexander Gordon is the son
of an old N.S. Wales Q.C., not long deceased.
Bruce Smith is the son of a sea captain, and Dr.
Sly the son of a Bridge-street financier.
Dr. Reville returns to Sandhurst instead
of resigning, as was expected, and the Cardinal’s
secretary thus misses what was taken as a good
chance to be appointed head of a wealthy diocese.
Dean Backhaus’ £120,000 is still locked up there.
George Throssell, who took up the Wes-
tralian Premiership in the Groper interest when
Bigjohn gave up the job in 1901, has decided to
pass out of politics. Forrest, Leake, Lee-Steere,
Throssell—soon there won’t be a trace of old
Groperdom left in the House.
Just died in Adelaide, aged 51, brewer
R. A. Stock. He was manager for E. T. Smith’s
brewery, and, on latter’s retirement, controlled
the whole business. He formed the S.A. Brewing
Co., of which he was the head, but was next door
to a Rechabite, and didn’t drink his own beer.
Premier James, of Westralia, is said to
contemplate reaching out for the Groper Chief-
Justiceship when it is vacant, which will be
shortly. The struggle for a “stiddy job,” as the
assistant blacksmith of “In Mizzoura ” used to
put it, is dreadfully keen among politicians.
The Bent Government in Victoria has
definitely refused to continue for Mrs. Graham
Berry the little pension which was granted by the
State to her dead husband. So far as the Bent
Government is concerned, the aged widow of
Victoria’s one-time grand old democrat can starve
or go to gaol.
Baronet Cooper, son of Sydney’s old
Dan’l, is credited with the ambition to be sent out
as next State Governor of N.S.W. The new
Cooper is an enthusiastic sport, and that is re-
garded as a valuable qualification. But how would
it look to have the State Governor prosecuting
tattered old men for stealing Christmaß bush on
his Sydney estates ?
“BIG JOHN ” FORREST, OF WESTRALIA.
A Sydney morning newspaper recently
described ex-Justice M. H. Stephen as the eldest
son of his father, which was incorrect. The three
eldest sons of Chief Justice Alfred Stephen were
Alfred Hewlett, in the church, born 1826 ; Mon-
tagu Consett, in the law, born 1827; and Matthew
Henry, in the law, born 1828, all seeing daylight
first at Hobart, Van Diemen’s Land.
Jacob Garrard (£7OO a year) has been
superseded as President of the Board of Water
Supply and Sewerage (Sydney). He will, how-
ever, be appointed as one of the Government
representatives on the Board at .£250 a year. As
Jake lives in his own house on the North Shore
line, is a teetotaller, and has a free life pass on the
railways he ma" be able to exist.
David Reid, the Albury cockie who bobbed
up the other day for his 83rd anniversary, conneets
the present with the days of Governor Brisbane.
He went to school to Dr. Lang, married into the
family of explorer Hamilton Hume, made two
speeches in Parliament, and started the first horse
race in Albury His branch of the Reids (an
English stock) has a habit of long living, two
sisters over 80 being still extant in Melbourne.
Dr. Todd, barrister, physician, lately
deputy-coroner, and the subject of official corres-
pondence between the N.S.W. Attorney-General
and Public Service Board, had a prototype in
Mudgee many years ago. Dr. Belinfante admini-
stered legal and medical advice, and “sat” on a
stiff ’un from time to time. He also went into
politics, which it is said Dr. Todd contemplates
doing. “ Rolf Boldrewood,” in his goldfields
warden days, grew several streaks of grey hair
fighting local battles with Dr. Belinfante. David
Buchanan wrestled politically with the many-
sided man. Belinfante was at last finally “ sat ”
upon by a rival coroner, a flooded creek getting
the better of him as he tried to cross with his
wife. The wife was saved.
“X.X.X.' Par in ** Woman’s Letter
(B. 4/2/’O4) about the death, at Balmain (Sydney) of
an old Countess who had lived for many years
incog., reminds me of other incog, title-bearers
eking out an existence in N.S.W. An Italian
Count sellsfruit in King-street; a Baron belongs to
an orchestra; a “My Lady” is a seamstress, amd
an heir to a baronetcy is a milkman. All genuine
cases, known to me personally but confidentially.
Have heard of others, but cannot vouch.
An interesting identity of Neutral Bay
(Sydney) is Mr. J. F. Mann, now midway between
80 and 90 years of age. Mr. Mann was one of a
party of explorers which went out over half-a-
century ago to search for Leichhardt, with whom
he was personally acquainted. Mr. Mann enter-
tained a very poor opinion of Ludwig Leichhardt
as an explorer. “He would lose himself in
George-street,” was the old gentleman’s terse
comment. Mr. Mann is related to the explorer
Thomas Livingstone Mitchell. Exploring runs
in his family.
Parson Kantopoulos, late ministerial head
of the Greek Church in Melbourne, who fell out
with his congregation over the custody of the
metroon (a book in which the births, marriages,
and deaths of the Levantine population of Melb-
is recorded), is a saintly-looking individual, with
thin, sallow face, great dark eyes, a full black
beard, and raven locks screwed away under a
skull-cap. Kantopoulos is short and slight, but
the style of dress he adopts—a long black gown —
attracts the attention which seems necessary to
the vanity of sky-pilots of whatever denomina-
tion.
The “taking of silk” by Gordon, Sly
and Bruce Smith, of New South Wales Bar,
will give long-looked-for vacancies to the plodding
juniors in the way of opportunities for the draft-
ing of pleadings and “second” briefs, which a
K.C. cannot accept. All the new recipients of
the badge were entitled to it years ago, but didn’t
like to renounce their incomes from pleadings.
The grumblings of the juniors probably hurried
the acceptance of silk. One thorn still remains
for the Equity juniors—they are aghast at the
tenacity of a leader who still refuses the purple
and drafts some hundreds of pleadings every year.
E. W. Hawker, ex-M.L.A., of South Aus-
tralia, has forsaken politics. He and his father,
G. C. Hawker, sat together in the Assembly,
just as old and young Parkes did in N.S.W., and
somewhat after the manner of Premier Charles
Cowper and his son, when the old man took
Charles Cowper, junior, into his Ministry. There
might have also been two Kingstons sitting
together in S.A. Assembly, but for the fact that
old George Kingston died four months before his
son entered politics.
Jack Conway (Vic.) is the only survivor of
the eighteen Victorian cricketers who played, on
the ground of the Melbourne Club, the first game
against the first All-England Eleven (H. H.
Stephenson’s). Stephenson’s team was here in
1862, beginning its tour on New Year’s Day. All
Stephenson’s men have passed over. There were
no “ashes” in those days—not even a cinder;
the Englanders won in a single innings, with 96
runs to spare. Then they beat 22 Cornstalks at
Sydney with 49 runs to spare.
Sacristan Patrick Langton, of St. Mary’s,
Sydney, was buried last week. Pat was a devoted
servant of Mother Church and a determined hater
of cricketer Coningham. When Dr. O’Haran, on
the day of the verdict in his favor in the notorious
divorce suit, approached St. Mary’s, leaning on
the left arm of Jack Want and on the right ditto
of Barrister Ralston, with Tom Slattery “doing
splendidly” in the wake, Pat Langton, from the
top of the main steps, sent up an Irish whoop
that could be heard in Sussex-street.
Sydney Evening News knighted Senator
Tom Playford, of S. Australia,- the other day;
yet the big fellow ought to stand out con-
spicuously as one of the precious few old S.
Australian politicians who have steadily re-
fused the daub. Not counting, of course
Sudden Solomon who was too Sudden to
have any chance, and the present Jenkins, who
isn’t ripe yet, Kingston and Playford are the only
two S.A. premiers for over 30 years who haven’t
taken a title. Playford resisted it even during
his lengthy spell as Agent-General—and that
billet is usually fatal. It is now nearly 30 years
since he first became a Minister and nearly 40
since he first entered the S.A. Parliament; and
he has kept his market garden going all the time.
The family of ex-Governor Loftus, now
deceased, were all-round as impecunious as
N.S.W.’s one time Excellency. The three houses
of the Elys, Rathfarnham Castle, Ely Castle and
Loftus Hall, went under the hammer to appease
creditors. Mr. Garbett is well remembered in
Sydney as a constant resident at Government
House, though only a few knew that he was a
bailiff sent out by creditors to seize a percentage
of the Governor’s salary as it was paid. Garbett
and Captain Loftus were close chums, the latter
making it appear they were blood relatives, but the
Governor never took to the “man in possession.”
Loftus did not sell vegetables, as is supposed (it
was Governor Belmore who made a bit that way),
but he sold fancy fowls regularly and got good
prices too.
THE LATE LORD LOFTUS (ex-Gov. N.S.W.)
Photo, by J. Hubert Newman, Sydney.
The next time Joseph Foster, of Mel-
bourne, transgresses the law of property he’ll
probably annex something sizeable, say a man-of-
war or a mountain. A while back he removed a
bike and the law gave him two years, but
suspended sentence so long as he kept to a straight
path. But he slipped again, and, going through
a casual stranger, took him down for all he
possessed—a whole penny. Now Joseph has to
do the former sentence as well as three month's for
the “ brown,” and it must dawn on him that the
game wasn’t worth the candle.
One of the sad old stories of Bully Bent’s
career as Speaker of the Vic. Assembly relates to
a certain wet night when the House had mostly
been enjoying itself. Bent reclined in the Chair
of Dignity with his hands folded on his paunch
and liis wig hanging negligently over his off-side
ear. Nobody thought of taking Tommy seriously
except a young teetotal member who, having been
sat upon by the awful potentate, bleated his mild
protest: “Mr. Speaker, I disagree with your
ruling on the point.” “ Oh, well, it’s good enough
for to-night, anyhow, ” said Bent.
Premier Seddon ear-marked “The Wearin’
o’ the Green.” years ago as his special song, and
even the driinkest politician knows better than to
jump the item when King Dick is there. Welling-
ton medico Cahill, who returned the other day
from a trip round the world, was glorified at a
banquet, and Seddon and Ward were present.
Either by accident or malice a miserable beast of
a band came and stood outside while the dinner
was going on, and struck up the grand old tune.
Every face paled, and a frantic individual flew
out and struck the band like a cannon-ball.
“Stop!” he cried. “Good God! Mr. Seddon’s
going to sing that directly.”
“ Spring ” on insurrectionists :
When I heard that John Haynes was going to break up
the Australian Commonwealth it set me thinking (for I
love rebels) of the world’s great insurgents. They seemed
to pass before me in procession. Danton, the terrible ;
Robespierre, the inflexible; Marat, the implacable.
They converse characteristically—Marat sneering at
Rohespiere’s weak habit of hanging his portrait on his
bedroom wall. “Marat’s is in the sewers,” replies the
curled and bedizened Robespierre. Danton denounces
Marat as a wood-louse, and shrugs his huge shoulders.
“Sometimes a shrug of the shoulders makes the head
fall, Danton,” snarls Marat, darkly. Robespierre is pale,
grave, with thin lips and powdered hair, and unwrinkled
light blue coat. Danton is an untidily dressed giant with
hair sticking stiffly up. His powerful pock-marked face
has large, blazingeyes. His mouth has kind corners and
thick lips which partly hide large teeth. Marat’s clothes
and hair are greasy, his mouth large and repulsive, his
forehead almost nil, his yellow face is blotched, and his
eyes are blood-injected. A world-dazzling trio. Others
go by like phantoms : Saint-Just, pale, profoundly sad,
with mvsterious eyes; Washington, wise-looking and
troubled ; Cromwell, with his noble wart planted on
his person where thrones saw and feared it;
Jack Cade of princely bearing (whatever that is);
Bogdan Khmelnicky, the great leader of the Cossack
revolt of 1651, who made the Polish oppressors tremble,
and taught the wild and manful Cossacks how to cut,
slash, stab, kill. Then there were others : Bern, Dembin-
ski, Kossuth, and the rest of the Hungarians of ’4B ;
Wolfe Tone, Bolivar, Miranda, San Martin, Iturbide,
Hidalgo, Cola di Rienzi; Kolokotrones, Canarie, Miaulis
and their fellow Greeks ; William the Silent; Garibaldi,
the Maccabees; Mehemet Ali, and the other Ali—he of
Janina ; Pekah, the son of Remaliah ; and Jeroboam,
the son of Nebat; Moses, the Devil, and innumerable
mighty rebels of old, whom I could not enumerate. With
their pictures fixed in ray eye I rushed madly forth to
get Jack Haynes to lead me to the field of carnage.
After two days’ search, saw him on a tram. I carefully
leaned my umbrella against the wall, put on my spectacles,
and scanned his person for traces of impending bloodshed.
Then I dropped the specs, with a snort. He lead us in
revolt! He ! HIM ! Hee—haw !
Bruce Smith, since he crawled into Par-
liament on all-fours under the whip of the P.D. A.,
whining that he never sent his children to a R.C.
School (which, by the way, he once did), seems to
have lost any political scruples he ever had. He
remarked in the House of Representatives the
other night that so little work had the High Court
to do that one of the Judges " had gone on a four
months’ tour of S’Africa.” Deakin interjected,
“you know that is not true,” and the Spruce
Myth fell lamely back on the plea that “ one of
the newspapers said so.” Then he started raving
about the demand of Victoria that the Australian
capital should be fixed in Melbourne, and being
indignantly told that no Victorian member had
advocated such a thing, the Myth thought “he
had seen it in some party’s platform.”
The late Leslie Stephen was, perhaps be-
cause of his Australian connections, deeply inte-
rested in Australian literature. Harold Stephen,
politician and journalist, a cousin of Leslie’s, kept
him in touch with the literary happenings of
Sydney and Melbourne, and Leslie’s neatly-written
little comments were mostly amusing. He wrote
on one occasion, “ If Australia ever lays a literary
egg your cackle will deafen Christendom.” On
another occasion, the crabbed microscopic charac-
ters conveyed: “You people should write for
children ; read another century before writing for
men.” And on another : “ Fairly good work, no
doubt; fairly good work; but I like it a little
better when it’s worse, for then you will not keep
on thinking it excellent.” And always : “Don’t
tell anyone in Australia to come to London to live
by his pen.”
“Pucha” makes some feeling remarks :
Maoriland newspapers are raising a mighty cloud cf
dustaboutone Plunket.orßunker, or Hunky, orsomething
of the sort, who has been appointed Governor in the place
of time-expired Ranfurly. One paper talks about Plunket
as representing not only the “ wit and national vivacity
of Ireland,” but also her “glorious past history.”
Another paper tells us in its list of virtues of the new
Governor that his father was the famous Archbishop
Plunket, and thus gives the show away. Prelate
Plunket, or Flunkey, or whatever it is, was chiefly
famous in his day for rabid Yellow Puppyism. It was he
to whom Henry George referred in “ Progress and
Poverty” when he wrote: “That right reverend Father
in God, Archbishop Lord Plunket, turned out some poor
tenants of his who refused to send their children to a
Protestant Sunday-school.” As for the new animated
Rubber Stamp himself there is nothing to be said ; up to
this probably he has not hurt a snail, but it is sickening
to wade through all the treacle that the M.L. Times and
other papers write about him and others of his class. It
is time a few daily papers began to think like democrats
living in a democratic community. Such men as
Plunket, or Bunkum, or Blanky, or whatever the right
name is, are anachronisms in our day, just as some of his
forebears were in theirs.

About late ex-Governor Loftus :
Augustus Loftus in his time must have been a cut above
the average well-connected person who becomes attached
to embassies and legations and such things. Apparently
he got a reputation for possessing considerable brains and
a lot of tact—and he kept his character. When the short
sharp war between Prussia and Austria was imminent
Loftus was appointed British ambassador at Berlin, and
when Russia, after the Franco-German war, began to
treat Britain as mud, Loftus was transferred to St.
Petersburg, where he remained what time Russia in-
vaded Turkey, and “ the Great Macdermott ” bawled his
historic Jingo song to the pot-valiants of the London
music-halls. It was Loftus, the diplomatist, who had the
job of constantly assuring the Czar that Britain’s em-
blematical lion did not want to fight but would be terrible
if roused. By the time that Russia and Turkey had
finished making widows and orphans and European com-
plications Loftus was a veteran of 62, and a very tired
veteran at that. So he came to N.S.W. as Governor and
drew his salary during six peaceful years. Then he
returned to England and he (or his creditors) retired on
a pension. With his usual tact and ability Loftus drew
the pension for 19 years.

Aboriginalities.
“ Curlew ” : Re “ breaking” young horses
(B. 12/11/’O3). Lived next door to a town
“ breaker ” once. The young’un was stuck in a
yard with dirt to eat and a scanty supply of water.
Three or four days of this made a pretty tame
moke; if he had strength enough left to lift a
leg, back again to the pen, for a few more days’
“taming.” Result: promising youngsters turned
into broken-hearted, dispirited cusses, too tired to
protest against anything except travelling fast.
Referred to in local mudslinger as “our very suc-
cessful horse-tamer.” He was all that ; but I
shifted camp. Couldn’t eat well with him next
door.

“T.L.”: Witnessed a fight between
several Dagos at a country pub. in Maoriland
recently. Said fight resembled a Rugby football
match with kicking in the scrums not barred.
One Dago was heeled out of the scrum, showing
unmistakeable signs of having been used in the
place of a ball, and exclaimed that he could not
fight that plurry language. Also, by way of
changing the subject, I saw the following advertise-
ment in a Maoriland paper recently : “Wanted, a
governess; must be a good milker.”

“ War E.” : The other day I inspected a
bank-owned property. The dwelling and out-
houses on it, spacious and well-built,
were falling to pieces from neglect,
yards and fences ditto ; a fine gar-
den had been destroyed by rabbits,
which were the only stock on a
heavily-grassed area. An elder-
ly married man was the “ man-
ager,’’and his salary had been
gradually reduced till it repre-
sented little more than board
and lodging. He and his
wife apologised for not ask
ing me to stay for dinner—
the meal was to consist of
bread and dripping—as the
bank had cut off the meat
supply when the price of
sheep went up! The “man-
ager ” informed me that
he had asked for permission
to put in some crop on
his own account. Peremp-
tory refusal. He asked for
a liorse and a few imple-
ments so that he could keep
fences in repairs. The bank
assented to the repairing
business, but regretted that
it could not provide the
horse or implements, and it
recommended its “manager”
to borrow them for the pur-
pose from his neighbors !
This is no isolated instance
of the policy by which
wealth - producing settlers
have been driven from their
homes by a foreign corpora-
tion, which leaves the places
mere wastes, fondly hoping
that the increase of popula-
tion and the consequent de-
mand for agricultural land
will secure it a profitable buyer.
“Curlew” :'Have often com-
mented on aboriginal’s persistent ,
dread of big camp-fires. Once heard
it put down to fear of bush taking
fire, inherent for generations, until it became a
national (?) characteristic. Fancy the tired feeling
has something to do with it. Notice same in
Assyrians, Arabs, <fcc., but their progenitors
weren’t afraid of setting the sand on fire or com-
busting the Euphrates. They simply required to
economise fuel. Another theory is that Jaeky-
Jacky brought the habit in his gripsack when he
toddled down from Asia, via Malaya, Sumatra,
&c. He’s got a fair grip of it now, at any rate.

“A.A.” : Can animals talk to one
another ? I have no doubt about it so far
as dogs are concerned. Had a collie slut
who acquired a decided taste for hen’s eggs.
Before anyone had a chance to gather them
in she had tracked the cackling hen and
scoffed the product. Not that she was at
all particular about the eggs being new-
laid ; I once located a deserted setting
through hearing the eggs “pop” as theslut
put her teeth into them. Finally, I kept her
a prisoner on the chain. Her kennel was
against the fowl-house as a protection from
native cats, and she used to peer in with a
disgusted expression, watching the fowls lay.
Then, soon after my small boy acquired a
terrier pup, the eggs commenced to dis-
appear again. The pup was supposed to be
above suspicion, but I determined to watch
him because he was always “ talking” to the
slut. At last I caught the pup coming out
of the fowl-house with his jaws almost dis-
located in the effort to carry an egg. He
carried the egg carefully up to the eager-
eyed slut, and placed it gently before her
with mutual complimentary wagging of
tails. He returned for another one —and
met me. There was one dog less that night.

“Ses”: A drover travelling from
the remote West to Charleville (Q.), re-
cently attempted to cross 1470 mixel cattle
over the flooded Georgina at Bedourie Cross-
ing. The mob started ringing in midstream,
and over 300 were drowned. A mistake was
evidently made in not taking a few over first
to the opposite bank. On a long swim cattle
will nearly always try to make back to their
fellows if there are none ahead of them ; or
they ring and smother one another. Have
seen men ride with swimming c itt’e,
strlightening them when they turned, aid
piloting them from bank to bank as they
would on land. Drawing-room drovers
generally bundle them into water in a packed
mob and let them rip. They often get over
without loss, and accordingly receive credi
for good management. Only cattle-men know
what unnecessary risks were taken, and how
the “ good management ” was fluked.

“ F.” : Explorer Thomas L. Mitchell, in
1835, discovered on the Darling an aborigines’
burial ground, called by the blacks Milmoridien.
Extensively laid out walks intersected the graves,
and mounds were planted with
beautiful shrubs or trees like
mourning feathers. “Civi-
lised man,” said Mitchell,
“ could not have displayed
more taste in beautifying
the resting-place of the
dead.” Can any of The
Bulletin’s way - back
readers identify the spot?
AN OBJECT OF TERROR.
GARDENER : “ Yes, mum, the birds are very bad
this season, they play up something awful
with this new garden.”
OLD LADY : “ Well, John, couldn't we rig up a
scarecrow?"
GARDENER : “ Lord bless ye, mum, I believe they'd
come jest th ’ same if you went and stood there
yourself.”
One can’t get a much closer call than
came the way of a White Cliffs (N.S.W.)
opal-gouger lately. They don’t timbei the
shallow rabbit-warrens in which the gem-
gouging is done, and up to now the diggers
have had extraordinary luck in mostly being
somewhere else when the workings started
to cave in. One of them, M‘Donald, was,
however, caught the other day as he was
tapping the roof of an old drive ; the stuff
buried him until only his finger-tips were
exposed. White Clifts crowded down to
dig him out; but it took a long while, for
the working space was small, and more
stuff kept falling ; and it was a solid hour
before the man’s face was exposed, and
another hour before his body was let loose.
And still he was alive ; the mullock had
fallen about him loosely, and hadn’t stopped
up his nostrils, and he got enough air to
just keep him going. There was a some-
what similar case a few years ago in a
Broken Hill mine. A creep took place,
killing three or four men; and another was
missing—he was pinned under a thick flake
of rock. They expected to take out a
mangled miner when they got this rock
away ; but, instead, he was alive and appa-
rently unhurt —his body had just fitted into
a slight cavity in the flake. The papers
said at the time that the man had had a
marvellous escape ; but perhaps it would
have been better if he hadn’t, for within a
few weeks he had become a complete phy-
sical and nervous wreck.
• * • *
“ A.P.R.” : The vilest crustacean
that wears scales is the Castlereagh cray-
fish. Soon as your fishing-line is cast he
seizes the bait and consumes it at his leisure,
after which he carefully hangs the hook
■on a snag, so that the line will be broken
in detaching it. The cray-fish presumably re-
gards you as a benevolent lunatic, who sits up
all night just to feed him; and this idea is
annoying, especially since he drives away all
other fish. Farmers detest cray-fish, because
they burrow underneath the embankments of the
dams, eventually destroying them. The cray-
fish is also a vile cannibal, who will chew at a
piece of his father (used as a bait), before the
parent is cold.
“ Owl-light ” on our natural resources
(written before the late rains):
Scene—Backblocks pub ; a few clouds about the sky
and a dust storm looming' up away in the west. Tuo
selectors sitting under the verandah deploring the
drought.
First Selector : “ My paddock is swept clean away and
every hoof is dead.”
Second ditto : “ My house is banked all around with
dust three feet deep.”
Publican (stepping out and looking at the skv
critically): “1 think we will have it before long; the
wind is in the right place and them clouds looks
suspicious.”
Colonial experience Johnnie (who arm o<l in the coach
that morning brand new from England) : *' Baye Jove 1
What evah is that ?” pointing to the big black du-t storm
now slowly rolling towards the pub from over the plain.
Selector : “ That’s a selection.”
Johnnie : “ A what? ”
Selector : “ A selection, o’ course.”
Johnnie : “ Baye Jove ! How tewibly black. Where is
ifc going ? ”
Selector: “ Struth ! Yer green. Didn’t yer never
hear of our great natural resources ?”
Johnnie : “ Well, ah, yes, I did ; I heaid Mr. Wise
before I left England speak of them. Is this one ?”
Selector : “ O’ course it is.”
Johnnie : “How stwange.”
And then the selection rolled over the town and all was
silently buried for two hours.

The forest of Robin Hood was a quite
insecure refuge compared to the forest of varie-
gated thistles that surrounds Moree (N.S.W.) at
present. The black-soil plains have sent forth a
dense growth that averages about 10ft.. in height,
and which in many places forms an impassable
barrier for miles. Horses won’t face it at all.
Provided the local law-breaker beats the pursuing
police to the wall of thistles he is then lafe ;
accordingly the thistles are acquiring quite a
population. Cave-dwelling having gone out of
fashion so long ago, it’s about time thistles got a
turn, and evolutionists should give their serious
attention to the possibility of the Australians
becoming a thistle-dwelling race in the near
future.

“ War E.” : The average sheep-farmer
seems to be about the most picturesque baron the
continent. For years he lied about his lamb-
marking percentage ; then the man who made up
the statistics began to inquire how the discrepancy
between “returns” and reports came about !
Then the sheep-man got to work on the “ average
weight” of his clip, but the financial institution
inquired where the rest of the wool had gone.
The sheep-liar wasn’t beaten yet— nd lie’s on safe
ground this time. The weight of fleeces cut from
his few “ extra special studs ” is going up rapidly.
The highest I’ve seen this season is a ewe 3U.lt.
and a ram 561 b. ! Just fancy a merino carrying
half-a-cwt. of wool round ! Wouldn’t mind bet-
ing it’ll be half-a-ton next year.

When the law first came in vogue in N.Q.
that blackboys had to sign on with their em-
ployers for a certain period, Tomkins was very
wroth that he, a white man, and a man of property
—he owned a small team of horses—should he
forced to enter into a contract of any kind with a
nigger. But the sergeant of police told him it
was inevitable. “ All right,” grumbled Tomkins,
“ but all the same it’s a crying shame that l should
have to bring meself down to the level of a dashed
aboriginal like that.” The papers were filled in
R. TURNER,
The boxer who burst the Blackmore bubble.
Photo, by Brand, Sydney.
and the pen handed to Mr. Tomkins. Mr. Tom-
kins cleared his throat violently and at last blush-
ingly admitted that his education had been ne-
glected and lie could only hold the pen and
make his cross, which he did. Then Binghi was
called on to hold the pen and make his mark.
“Oh, thank you,” said Binghi politely. “lean
read and write.” The sergeant poured water oyer
Tomkins, and when he came round he said:
“Eddication has ruined this dashed country.”
IN PREHISTORIC DAYS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the Dodo gladly gurgled out his prehistoric la3 r ,
And our early relatives were mostly beast;
When the earth lay dark and silent ’neath the first dawn
clouds of gray,
Long before the golden sun rose in the east;
Then the early tribal ladies gaily pranced upon a rock,
And their flowing manes of hair waved in the wind,
And the prehistoric baldhead who objected got a shock
When they hit him with a stone axe from behind.
They had very little comfort, had the people who came
first—
As a rule, they had to eat the entrees raw ;
They who couldn’t catch their dinner, prehistorically
cursed,
But each party waved the implement he wore ;
If some unsuspecting neighbor came wand’ring gently
by.
Dreaming happy dreams of his suburban cave,
Someone rolled a boulder on him, with a half-regretful
sigh
For the sudden end of one so young and brave.
They were vigorous and simple were our relatives of yore
And, of course, the early ladies did their best,
But if Pa got argumentative, and forgot himself and
swore,
Well—Ma tripped him up and sat upon his chest,
While the family would go on sadly gnawing in the dark,
Wondering how they could possess so little pride,
But would recollect with pleasure as they hacked at the
cold shark
That the weapons of the push were kept outside.
They knew naught of care and comfort, our relatione
long ago,
They had neither summer drinks nor thoughts nor cares.
And they flirted with each other sitting waist-high in the
snow,
And the only things that saw them were the bears.
There were neither plagues nor poets—only rending earth-
quake shocks,
No distractions, like mere work or problem plays.
All was giggle and flirtation in a world of sullen rocks
In the good old jolly prehistoric days.
VIC.

WHERE TO STAY IN SYDNEY
ADAMS’ HOTEL
PITT STREET.
ITEET HE AT THE HARBLE BAR.
' l Hugh Stone ” : I knew
a man in a wild mountainous
district of N.S.W. whose imi-
tation of the dingo’s howl was
so natural as to deceive the
animal itself. He used to go
up in the range with his rifle
and draw the dingoes to him
by imitating their melancholy
utterance. Then there would
be a dead dingo. Crossing the
range one clear moonlight night
he started howling. Imme-
diately he got a response miles
down the range. For an hour
he howled walking down the
mountain to meet his rival,
and the rival howled walking
up. In a clear patch he halted
and gave what he thought
ought to be the final howl, and
levelled his gun. A minute
later a neighbor who had ac-
quired the same accomplish-
ment stepped into the open,
levelled his gun, and howled.
It was nearly a tragedy.
* *
“ Werrimee ” : The bird
which “Vinco” (8., 25/2/’O4)
calls a mutton-bird is the pied
erOw-shrike, or black magpie
(Strepera graculina). It is
also called the fruit-eating
magpie, though it feeds largely
on carrion, and is just as de-
structive among com as crows
are. In some places it is called
the mutton-bird, but is more
widely known as the black
magpie. Very plentiful on
the northern rivers of N.S.W.
The common magpie (Gymnor-
hina tibiccn) is known as the
piping crow-shrike, or black-
backed crow - shrike, and is
principally confined to N.S.W.
Though the common magpie,
black magpie and butcher-bird
belong to the one family, and
are generically called shrikes
or butcher - birds, they are
specifically different and bear
very little resemblance to one
another. Australia has nearly
30 different shrikes. The mut-
ton-bird (Nectric brevicaudus)
belongs to the water-fowl, and
is very numerous among the
southern islands."
“Waler”: Be the in-
crease in shearing-rates: In
nearly all pastoral and farming
work rates have fallen steadily
within the last 20 years—fenc-
ing, clearing, harvesting, etc.
There was a time when clearing,
i.e., grubbing for the plough,
was paid for by the inch ■ — a
penny an inch for trees. For
instance, a tree 3ft. was
reckoned at 35., and so on.
This was merely for getting the
tree down ; the burning off
was a separate contract. Men
will now clear an acre for what
a few trees brought in those
days ! A clever dodge in grub-
bing then was, when you struck a tree with
tremendous roots to follow, to dig a hole alongside
and throw the earth on to the stump. The holes
had to be left open for inspection. The hole
alongside always passed—for a time.
* * * *
“Deeby”: An immense destruction of
small fish occurs when the Macquarie and other
western rivers are in flood. As the water recedes
the hollow ground at the feet of the big gums on
the flats or false banks is filled with dead fish of
various species. Was once travelling by the
Lachlan, when I noticed off the track what
appeared to be a newspaper flapping in the wind.
Investigation proved it to be a stranded 101 b.
cod fish lying in a pool of water nofcvmore than
2in. deep. It had been lying in that shallow pool
for at least 24 hours. Wonder how long it is
possible for a fish to live under these conditions,
allowing the crows keep away.

“ J.A.C.” : Be curious “shingles.” There

“ A.G.A.” : Re “ Elmore ” and “ A.K.C.”
on goanna. I have never seen a goanna swallow
anything, but I’ve cut goannas open many times
for their fat, and I’ve found on occasion a whole
’possum and a whole lamb inside. These goannas
did not butt against trees to ram their food down
—none of the surrounding timber bore any marks
of violence. I understand one goanna helps the
other. The goanna is remarkable for a very stiff
tail —in which he differs from the Australian
Eleven.
* * * *
“ K.N.T.” (a woman) writes from a far-
back W. A. town as to aborigines’ protection :
I had for some years an intelligent little aboriginal
child. She was motherless, and her father was a tracker.
After giving her to me, he left the district. When the
girl was seven years of age a white man, or rather devil,
ruined her, and for two years I tried to cure her of a
loathsome disease, but without success. I applied to the
aborigines protector, and he warned me against “ harbor-
ing an aboriginal.” When I told him I could not and
would not hunt the girl away, as she would surely die,
he wanted to know what that was to me. He said,
“ Whip her away into the bush and let her die.” That is
aborigine protection as practised in W.A.
* * * *
“G.M. ’ : Re par in Bulletin (21/l/’O4)
about two tourists meeting a Maori in the
Waikato district, between Waikaremoana and
Te Aroha. Waikaremoana is in the Wairoa dis-
trict, Hawke’s Bay. This reminds me that a
friend of mine was walking in the Gippsland dis-
trict, between Mildura and Hopetoun, when he
met a black carrying a message-stick. After two
hours’ close study my friend discovered that the
stick was a message from King Billy of Gippsland
to the chief of the Bourke-street tribe, saying that
Russia and Japan were going to war because
Irvine excluded the six hatters. It also had a
drawing of Reid on it in a kneeling attitude,
apparently wooing the Labor party. I know this
is true, because I met the black the following
morning just round the first corner after you pass
Hades, about six miles from Bourke and close to
Bill Jones’ public-house.
WASHING UP!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When you’re young and full of push,
And you’re living in the bush,
Far away in timbered country wh»re the neighbors are
not thick,
Oh ! the time that cools your heat,
And that knocks you off your feet,
Is that day of dire misfortune when you have the missus
sick !
’Tis the small ills, wise men say,
That drive happiness away—
’ Tis the irritating trifles that fill Sorrow’s bitter cup !
And you’ll own this doctrine true,
When, besides your work to do,
There's the housework for the missus, and the job of
washing up.
With a confidence sublime,
You resolve that every time
You have meals you’ll wash the dishes—keep the crock’ry
clean and bright.
You prefer things to look nice ;
So you do it—once or twice !
Then at morn and noon you leave it—you will wash the
lot at night!
And the stuff accumulates—
Oups and saucers, also plates—
But you promised, so you do it, ere you have a bite or
sup!
Though when one has held the plough,
It seems paltry, anyhow- -
Not a bit like manly grafting, is the job of washing up 1
So you clean up what will do—
Just enough to see you through ;
Then you put off washing any while there’s clean ones on
the shelf 1
And the missus, it is plain,
When she’s up and out agan,
Will discover you have used up all her little stock of delf 1
As you older, wiser grow,
All that’s needful you will know
Is a knife, fork, plate (one only), with a spoon, maybe,
and cup !
And I reckon him a man
Built on an heroic plan,
And deserving of a statue, who can patiently wash-up !
N.S.W.
ULOOLA.

“On the arrival of the steamer he was welcomed by
about 40 local adherents, many of whom were treated by
him for various ailments. He lightly touched the
affected parts.”
“ The affected part ” was very apparent in the
case of most of the adherents, and the wily Elijah
had no difficulty in “ touching the spot.” This
case was considered pretty hopeless.
“ Hugh Stone ” writes :
When I was a State teacher I was once appointed to a
mountainous district, the home of a sang of bush-
rangers who finished life’s short histor3' on the Darling-
hurst gallows. The old feeling which had caused the
brothers to make war upon society in the sixties was still
in the new generation when I arrived in the nineties.
They did not settle quarrels by a stand-up fight or ail
appeal to a police court, but they got even on each other
bv damaging stock, fences, and crops. I remember on
one occasion two female neighbors had the usual feminine
difference. No 1 vowed vengeance on No. 2, and one day,
when the latter came home from a visit, she found a
well-bred chook, with sundry well-bred chicks, all with
necks screwed, laid artistically along the doorstep as a
sign that it was to be war to the knife. I lived on a sort
of volcano, for I reckoned my time would come some
day, and it did. I had to report a grand-nephew of one
of the dead bushrangers, and the eruption began. A
valuable horse that never had the roving spirit in him,
suddenly disappeared, and though I had police and black
trackers after' him, I never saw him again. Almost
everything else disappeared by degrees. At last the
place grew so untenable that I also disappeared. I
believe this strange midnight method of hen-and-horse
warfare still survives in those parts.

The great and particular merit of Hunyadi Janos
is that it relieves without discomfort, in other
words, it effects reform without revolution. This
is because it is the best and safest natural
aperient.*
During the last twelve months there have
been twelve convictions for murder in Westralia,
and the convicts have been all Japs, Chinese,
Afghans, and other aliens. Yet White Australia
has to pay for the gallows, and the rope, and the
bear-grease on the rope, and the Nosey Bobs’
wages, and the wear and t >ar of gaol, and all the
other sundries.
Twelve Years of Indigestion.
Doctors Advised Operating.
A Remarkable Cure by Bile Beans.
“ About nineteen years ago,” says Mr. Henry
Speer, a blacksmith, of Lvnch-street, Young,
N.S.W., “I first felt the symptoms of indiges-
tion. The pains I experienced were terrible, and
would completely double me up. I consulted
several doctors. They all agreed it was an
aggravated form of indigestion, and one told me
that I would not live four hours unless I got ease
from the pain. Another doctor advised operating,
but this I would not agree to. All the doctors
could do for me was to administer morphia and
opium to deaden the pain. I was under the
doctors’ hand for many years, attending them
regularly, until ray patience was exhausted.
About seven years ago, hearing that Bile Beans
had cured many cases of indige-tion, I decided to
give them a fair trial. After having taken them
regular v for a month or six weeks, I noticed a
great improvement in my condition. Thus
encouraged, I persevered with the Beans, and was
effectively and thoroughly cured. It is now five
years since I had an attack of indigestion, which
I think can be taken as a guarantee that the cure
is permanent. Where life was once a burden, it
is now a pleasure, and altogether I feel a different
man. I have now been a resident of Young for
seven vears and am consequently well-known.”
Bile Beans are a permanent and speedy cure for
biliousness, headache, indigestion, constipation,
piles, debility, female weaknesses, nervousness,
bad blood, bad breath, anaemia, insomnia, loss of
appetite, and in fact, all ailments that owe their
origin to defective liver action. Bile Beans are
obtainable from all medicine vendors, price
Is. or 2s. 9d. large box.*
Business, Robbery, etc.
“ Chasselas ”: “ Eiddal ” (Bulletin 25/2/’O4) is
mistaken if he thinks I hold a brief for cheap Yankee
lines of machinery. What I want is a stiff import
duty to exclude Yank and German goods, so that the
money at present going to enrich foreign manufacturers
will be circulated in these States. All Yankee goods are
not the cheap and nasty sort “ Eiddal ” mentions.
Present writer has a Canadian binder which has done
good service for 15 years, and is still going strong. Yank
malleable castings, however bad, are superior to and
lighter than the heavy, crude, rotten castings manu-
factured locally. Fortunately, good Australian makers
use little cast, knowing they can’t rely on it. But why
not manufacture our own malleable castings, and make
them good enough to lighten the splendid machinery
turned out by local firms ?
* * * *
It was a German who first thought of making
firearms without screws, and the Mauser people turned'
out a repeating pistol which was composed of pieces
which dovetailed into each other and were finally held
together by a spring-pin. Once the spring-pin was
removed the whole weapon could be
taken to pieces with the forefinger
and thumb in ssec. and put together
in a very few seconds more. Now
the detachable bike has come.
The parts dovetail, and can be
taken apart and packed in a case,
which is carried on the top frame
bar, and which measures 29in. by
Sin. The idea of the inventor was
to save cyclists the special charges
levied on their bikes by railroad
and ferry companies, but the in-
vention has turned out such a
success that the military authori-
ties of five nations are testing the
thing severely with a view to
adopting it for infantry. The gain
in mobility would be enormous.
* * *
“W.S.T.”: Much nonsense
is printed from week to week about
the extent and richness of the
N.S.W, fishing grounds. As a
matter of fact, the supply of first
quality fish is small and variable.
Bar the alleged “ salmon ” and sea-
mullet (the former bad as a food*
fish, and the latter coarse and
tasteless except when in roe),
there are but few net-fish obtain-
able. A few whiting can still tie
caught, but the dainty flounder
and garfish seem to be things of
the past, and the river mullet is
getting ever scarcer Of line-fishes
we still have schnapper, bream of
various kinds, flathead, and jew-
fish, the last often figuring as
schnapper on the menu of the fish
shops. But with the exception of
“jewy,” it would not pay to fish
for these varieties oilier than from
a picnic point of view. We have no
banks to fish for cod ; the dainty
and swarming herring of Northern
seas is entirely absent; the floun-
der of Tasmaiiia-Maori waters is
poorly represented ; the trumpeter
and barracouta are too far away ;
and pilchards come and go only at
three or four year intervals.
Where, then, are our “magnificent
fish resources?”

Australia’s best-known col-
lection of stamps, owned by W. F.
Petterd, of Launceston, Tas., has
changed hands ; £2500 was paid
for it through a Sydney agent,
Walter Hull. Petterd has a mania for collecting
things ; he has or had fine collections also of minerals
and natural history specimens. There used to be a
story that one Australian mining expert, sent across
to report on the then newl.v-discovered Zeehan and
Dundas fields, some years ago, for a big fee, got no
further than Launceston, where he wrote a much-quoted
monograph on the strength of Petterd’s collection and a
yarn with Montgomery, the Government geologist.
* * * *
“Yes,” yarned the boss of the hosiery depart-
ment working overtime, “ when I joined this warehouse
we had a Distressed Employees’ Fund, and anyone who
joined and got hurt could draw on.it. I was a mean
little nipper in those days, and wouldn't join, and conse-
quently got a rough time, but 1 held out. At last I had
an inkling that the town entry-room clerks, were up to
some mischief, so I hurried to the sec. and paid my first
sub. of 3d. per week. Then I went down to the entry
room, and old , who was boss of that part then, con-
veniently turned his head, and the boys bustle 1 me into
the biggest wheeler (like they do the new chums now)
and bounced me horribly. The result was that my arm
was broken. I didn’t split, but told the manager I’d
fallen downstairs. I had to go to bed for some weeks,
but that didn’t hurt much, and as I drew full screw I
didn’t mind in the least When I went back I said to the
boys in the entry-room • * I’m going to draw ex’s, out of
the fund for that accident. ‘You’re not a member,
though,’ someone said. ‘Who said?’ I asked quietly.
You go and ask the secretary.’ And when they did
they told me I was too smart for the entry-room and that
I ought to be a traveller. It was 3d. well speculated.
What do you think? But the worst of it is that I’ve been
a member ever since, and haven’t had a blooming illness.”

The struggle to get the whole story into a sixpenny
notice.
“Bengalla,” from Gippsland : Down our
way harvest commences early—September gener-
ally. We gather it in with tomahawks, and
bind it in bundles with string.v-bark ties. Our
crop is the only one which this heavily-tim-
bered country will produce with facility
in the early stages of settlement, and con-
sists of the bark of the black wattle.
Notwithstanding its astringent qualities,
we live and thrive upon it ; and, when
prices go up to £7 per ton, talk freely of
taking up more land. The wattle, in-
deed, has been the main factor in settling
hundreds of men with large families and
small means upon thousands of acres in
this section, as it has afforded them the
means of living while engaged in subdu-
ing the dense forest. When Irvine came
into power, we selectors generally re-
garded his policy of economic reform
with favor, and said that revenue must
be raised even if the individual suffered ;
but when he reached for us by proclaim-
ing new and drastic provisions for wattle-
stripping on Crown lands —which, in
practice, meant handing over to the
Treasury about half its gross value per
ton—we" were unanimously of opinion
that the liberty of the subject was en-
dangered and his territorial rights
ignored. Naturally we regard the fluctu-
ations of the bark market with interest,
and our spirits rise or fall as the price
goes up or down. At the beginning of
one season prices ruled very low, and
a poor selector, holding over 2000 acres
heavily cropped with bark, and, to a less
extent by sheep, got the bulge on his neighbors by con-
tracting to supply a local tanner with a large quantity of
bark at a shade over current rate. Stripping and delivery
went on merrily until, suddenly, prices went up with a
bound. Then stripping went on, but delivery (to the
local tanner) ceased. The selector, as a Freetrader,
logically sold in the dearest market, notwithstanding
frequent applications from the tanner, and continued to
do so for two seasons. The tanner, getting tired of moral
suasion, had recourse at last to legal pressure ; and this
brought the selector to explain things. He endeavored
to arouse the tanner’s higher feelings (they were both
Wesleyans). Could he, knowingly, add to the weight of
the cross he (the selector) was then bearing ! The tanner
said he could, and added £9O costs to it, and got his
bark.
* * * *
“ Petramos ” writes of a new tooth-ache cure :
Jones and Brown, dentists, occupied adjoining shops.
Jones engaged a man to wear a uniform and stand
outside and direct suffering humanity his way, and
Brown copied the idea; wherefore there was trouble,
Jones threatening to sue Brown for infringement of the
Molar Extractors’ Copyright Act. But the commis-
sionaires were affable persons, and in a little while
got on all right together, and agreed to divide cus-
tomers, so that neither would lose his job for not being
as smart as the other. Things progressed favorably
until Smith, a third extractor, started business dose
by, and engaged a man to stand outside also. The
third man dressed like a dilapidated constable, and wore
a padded chest. One day a small woman, from Farback
apparently, paused, and gazed vacantly at the three pain-
less establishments, and the cheap imitation of the Law
endeavored to escort her to his boss ; hut the Jones man
took a hand, conveying in innuendoes that Smith was an
unregistered quack. The Smith man called the Jones
man fancy names, whilst the Brown man stood peaceably
in the background, content, for the time being, to remain
the Jones man’s sleeping partner. The old lady became
excited, for she was dragged first one way, then the other.
AN ADVENTURE WITH MUNGINDI
MOSQUITOES.
Suddenly the Smith man smote his rival on the stum-
mick, and the Jones man punched back, landing on the
padded chest. The lady politely stepped aside so as not
to hinder the fight. Five minutes later, whilst the Jones
mail was on his way to the local hospital; and the Smith
man (minus his chest) was gathering pieces of his lost
manhood off the hard, cruel pavement, Brown’s emissary
sailed smilingly up to the old lady and asked her to step
Brown’s way. But she uttered a sudden ejaculation of
delight: “ I’d forgot all about it. Watehin’ those fellows
it must’ave stopped.” Brown’s man was disappointed.
He was an Irishman, and considered he'd stayed out of a
fight without remuneration.

“S.T.S.”: I don’t know Mr. Mephan Ferguson
from a crow. Is the fact that he advertises in The
Bulletin any bar to my rejoicing over a par. just found
in a Birmingham paper? They seem to think a lot of
his steel pipes in the Black Country, chiefly because the
36 inch article can he made 28 feet long, requiring far
fewer joints than the local cast-iron pipe, which I
remember as running to about nine feet. To the
patriotic Australian, it is a refreshing change from the
usual order of things to read of “ headworks at Mel-
bourne, with a branch near Birmingham,” and that “ a
plant and several skilled men have been brought from
Australia.”

Two leading Broken Hill business houses have
made a new departure in the transport of goods from
Adelaide. Recently 130 tons of groceries and draperies
arrived in Broken Hill via Menindie, from Adelaide.
Other similar loading is now at Menindie awaiting camel-
carriage to B. H. The lan led goods cost £4 ss. a ton
from Adelaide The freight by rail would be £6 10s per
ton. On third-class goods a saving of £3 and even £4
can be made by using the river. Unfortunately, there is
a river only now and then.

“ Logo ” : Was told years ago by a Scotch
attorney who lived near Balmoral that the late
Queen Vic. and her Consort were not a happy
couple, and that Albert more than once threatened
to leave his royal wife and live permanently with
his brother in Germany. The limb of the law
longed to have charge of the prospective divorce
proceedings.
THE OLD MACHINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Tis a dingj' little office in a western country town,
Where I toil from morn till night to earn my keep ;
Where the dust drives through the doorway from the
highways bare and brown,
And piles up in the passage inches deep.
There I sit behind a screen,
Typing letters —always typing—on the creaking old
machine.
In the blazing heat of summer days the laden teams go
by,
Almost smothered in the clouds of dust that blow ;
And it’s “ Hi there, Satan ! Come up, Snow ! ” I hear the
drivers cry
To the bullocks, plodding heavily and slow.
Then I fold the faded screen,
But in fancy through my mem’ry runs the rhyme of the
machine.
I was wont to think the homestead ways were dull, and
bush-life plain ;
But ’twas freedom, and the mountain-streams were cool;
And I’m longing every day to see the old folks once
again,
But the time goes by, and I’m upon the stool
Forgotten and unseen,
So I send them Christmas greetings written out on the
machine.
Yet there’s one bright ray to cheer me through my weary
daily round ;
Just outside the office-window I can see
Standing tall a id straight, and casting forth its branches
far around
An exile like myself—an old gum tree.
An l it keeps my mem’ry green,
While I’m tapping for my daily bread upon the old
machine.
W.J.W.
Lack of Initiative.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ No,” said Tom, addressing a small but attentive
audience on the verandah of the Lake View ;
“there are some men who don’t know what
gratitood is.”
It was almost a judicial utterance, and coming
from Tom practically concluded the argument;
though Saul was heard to murmur discontentedly,
as if he thought the decision might still be upset
on appeal. He was not noticed, however, and
Tom continued :
“Now look at Harris! Will anyone here say
there could be any gratitood in Harris after the
way he treated me ? ”
“ When was that ? ” asked Saul.
“When was that?” said Tom in an injured
tone. “Why, don’t you remember, when Harris
stood for the Kangarookery ! Wasn’t I his secre-
tary ! Didn’t I get him the best of 800 votes !
Didn’t I work morning, noon and night for seven
weeks, and wasn’t I darned well sick of the whole
business ! And then for a fellow like that to say
I was lacking in inishative.”
“ How did he form that opinion?” said Saul
loftily.
“He never formed it ; he just jumped at it.
Anyhow it was years before I got rightly over
that election. I was kind of dazed afterwards.
Couldn’t collect my wits. For seven weeks I’d
gone the rounds witli Harris ; I knew the people ;
I knew the country. All the lads at the woolshed
said that they’d vote with me, and the chaps at
the sawmills were with me to a man. Then my
brother-in-law, J. J. Jenkins—you know, the
cove that married Kate—he owns all the houses
on the east side of the township, and I tipped him
the wink. And the baker—you know Hayes, the
fellow that married Sal —he owned most of ’em
on the west, and I tipped him the wink ; and my
brother-in-law Jenkins was in with the raisers of
fat stock up the river, and he tipped them the
wink ; and the wheat-growers on the flat told me
straight that they knew nothing about politics,
but if there was one man in the electorate they
respected more than Hayes, it was the man Hayes
would support for Kangarookery.”
“So you tipped them the wink,” said Saul in-
terrupting in a distinctly disrespectful manner.
“That’s not the case,” said Torn. “Hayes
tipped them the wink.
“ Of course Harris’s family and my family were
old friends, and when Harris asked me to stick to
him I promised I would stick to him ; betides,
Harris said he’d get me a Government billet, and
as I wasn’t exactly cut out for work I reckoned
that that would suit me very well.
“ Still, I soon found when I started cuivassin’
for Harris I had the most tremenjus job tint a
white man ever took on. It broke m ; up, it got on
to my nerves, it laid me out, in a manner of speak-
ing ; an’ yet he’s the fellow that will tell you now
that I’m ‘lacking in inishative.’
“It was awful,” said Tom. “Every man,
woman or child we met, whether he was above or
below the use of reason, parent or kiddy, old
or young, he always had the same greeting. He’d
open his mouth at him like a horse with the
lampas, and say, ‘H’ are yuh ? ’ He used to say
that he would insinuate himself into the good
graces of the people, and that all the electors of
Kangarookery were pleased with him inquiring
after their health like, hut it was awful for any
one that was with him always. He got that way
that wherever we went lie always wore that grin,
and I used to be afraid, when I moved a bit in the
trap or turned round sudden, that he would I reak
out with his ‘H’ are yuh?’ And the gibberin’
and the noddin’ got that awful that, cornin’ in of
a night after a hard day’s canvassin’, I used to be
afraid to look at bin:, and even when I wasn’t
lookin’ at him I fancied I could see his jaw
working. And the consequence, of course, was
my nerves got unstrung. It dragged me down,
and yet” (here Tom raised his hand with solemnity)
“that is the man that says I’m lacking in
inishative. Lacking in your grandmother, 1 say.
“The whole thing turned on Wattle Elat;
I could see that plain enough. I had every vote
counted, and I knew that if we could nab the
blokes in Wattle Flat we were right. We left
Wattle Flat till the day before the election, then
we drove aci’oss twenty miles through the timber.
Harris said that he was determined that Wattle
Flat should carry the impression of his virtues
fresh right up to the polling-booth. I know I’ll
carry his bloomin' impression all my life. I’ll
never forget that day in Wattle Flat. It was a
beautiful day The wattles were bloomin’, the
bees were hunimin’, the grass was green, and
them little yeller flowers were shinin’ all over the
ground along the road. But it was a terrible day
for me.
“When we got among the houses it ’ud be,
‘ H’are yuh ? ’ this one, and ‘ H’are yuh ? ’ that
one. Supposin’ we pulled up at a pub. it would
be ‘ H’are yuh ? ’ everyone. If it was only a
bloomin’ Chow diggin’ a potato patch, you’d think
Harris and he had been schoolmates. The grin on
him never used to wear off, and I got to feel like
he was snarlin’ at me, and I fancied every minute
he was going to bite me. It was a hard day’s
work, and I was glad when night came on and we
started for home. I reckoned we were just about
safe, perhaps a vote or two to spare. I was
soothed. Harris’ face settled a little as we got on
the lonely bush road. I was happier than I had
been for weeks, but if I had known what I had to
pass through, what 1 was to suffer for this very
man, I’d have—well, I’d have committed suicide
at any rate. It was a lonely road, and night had set
in when we left the Elat. We were about two miles
out, and we were going slow. Harris was tired,
I was tired, the horse was tired. We were just
about Harris’ Gully (that’s why it is called Harris’
Gully, on account of what happened there that
night). The track is narrow, and there s scrub
all round, and big trees. Then we came to a bit
of open ground. I was listless like at the time,
and I fancy Harris was dozin’ All of a sudden,
it happened.”
“ What happened ? ” asked Saul.
“ I’d rather you’d let me tell my story in my
own way,” said Tom, casting a withering glance
at his interrupter.
“ The timber cracked to the left of us, and two
old-men kangaroos broke through, crossed the
track, and made to get away over the clear
ground. I was startled, and I near fell out of the
trap, when I heard Harris shrieking through his
teeth, ‘H’ are yuh ? ’ At the same time he leaned
forward, pulled the reins out of my hands, cracked
the whip and started the neddy across the patch
in the ways the ’roos had gone.
“At that moment Harris leaned forward, the
wind blew his whiskers aside, the moon shone
through his teeth, and he gibbered at me and
fixed me with glittering eyes and sang out : ‘ You
fool, don’t you know that them gentlemen are
eligible for voters’ certificates?’ ”
“ I suppose you laughed a bit ? ” put in Saul.
“You s’pose wrong. I wasn’t that way in-
clined. My head swam. I saw the moon whirl-
ing in the air. I realised that I was driving
furiously across country with an insane candidate,
and I just fell out of the trap. My head struck
on the metal and the wheels went over me. I
came back to consciousness the day- after the
election.
“Harris? He was found two days'after the
election on a gum stump. He was quite nood,
and was wavin’ his hands to a native bear, and
saying something about being acquainted with
local conditions, and if lie was returned lie would
further the interests of the country while having
due regard to the industries of tli4 town. And
in between times he was saying ‘ H' are yuh ’ to a
locust.”
“But how did the election go?”
“We were beaten by one vote, and when T met
Harris after he recovered I told the stoopid idiot
that our two votes would have put us right if he’d
only stuck to the track and let them ’roos alone.
He just said it was easy to make excuses,
but everybody knew that the election was lost
owing to my lack of inishative in not approaching
them two settlers.”
