POLITICAL
POINTS
Australian Treasurer Turner, struggling
with the State Treasurers over the finance tangle,
might have inwardly quoted Tolstoy: “The
State is the evil.”
Announced by the Government represen-
tative in the Senate that the Deakin Cabinet will
claim a Federal capital territory of 1000 square
miles. It ought to claim 5000 square miles, but
even 1000 is something to go on with.
Holland, after a long course of Freetrade,
has now a Bill before its Parliament for the estab-
lishment of a Protective tariff in a small way.
There are very few worshippers of the Freetrade
Joss left now, and this threatens to make one less.
Wilks, N.S.W. M.H.R. for Londonderry
and the Yellow Pup and Dill Macky’s calves, in
the House : “ The man who raises sectarianism is
a diabolical scoundrel.” “A diabolical scoun-
drel ! ” Thanks to Mr. Wilks for the description.
Irvine and Shiels and Bent got on
famously last year; the rush to go somewhere
which didn’t enjoy the blessings of their rule was
overpowering. In the latter six months Victoria’s
losses by excess of sea-departures over arrivals
reached 7994.
Chief Justice Madden of Victoria has let
in one undesirable Chinaman for what looks like
a very weak reason. The customs officer started
to dictate to this Chow 50 words in English as a
test, but as the Chow couldn’t comprehend any
one of the first ten words he left off in disgust.
The Court held that he should have read the other
40 words, even though Asia was gazing at him like
a blank wall and plainly understood nothing, and
Ah Foo comes in on that technicality.
The “employers’ conference” must feel
pretty bad at not being able to blame the Labor
party for putting into the Federal Constitution
the power to legislate with regard to industrial
troubles. Except Trenwith, perhaps, there wasn’t
a single Labor man in the Convention ; and it was
not Trenwith who proposed this item—it was
J- H. Howe, a member of the Conservative party
of S- Aus., and a mining director. When one
reflects upon the constitution of the Convention,
one can’t help marvelling at the broad liberalism
of the thing which it produced.
FIRED OUT.
THE INTERJECTOR (who was a Methodist clergyman): “I shall only leave
this public meeting when I choose, or when expelled.”
MR. DOWIE: “Would you like to be carried out?"
THE INTERJECTOR: “If you do carry me out I shall take steps-”
Two ushers then seized the clergyman by the shoulders and started to
bustle him down the stone steps leading into Swanston-street.
Boston (U.S.A.)has rejected 80 Yorkshire
immigrants, as they might become a charge upon
the State. The’Murkans object to Britain dump-
ing its halt, lame and blind on their shores.
The Six Hatters case, which supplied George
Reid with so much platform pabulum, was not a
circumstance to the Bostonians.
“R.” writes :
It is incontestable that every Englishman and likewise
every Australian should abhor the Russians and maffick
for their defeat. Do they not live upon such degradirigly
filthy food as candles, train-oil and decaying mammoths
and are they not strangers to soap? Do they not main-
tain oppression and cruelty to the lower races ? And isn’t
there gold in their country, just as there was in Boer-
land ?
Harry Allan, brother of Frank Allan “ the
bowler of a century,” gave to a meeting of
Victorian public servants the following descrip-
tion of what the Public Service M.L.A. ought to
be:—“ I think we should select a man from out-
side the service. He should be a lawyer for
preference-one that will not behave himself too
well.”
N.S.W. Government resisted the tempta-
tion to make another political appointment to the
Sydney Water and Sewerage Board, or to continue
to sanction the scandalous job by which G. H.
Reid appointed the incompetent Jacob Garrard
to the post. An expert engineer of the Harbors
and Rivers Branch, T. W. Keele, has now been
put at the head of the board.
One of the most fearful results of the
Morgan Government’s retrenchment transpired at
Rockhampton (Q.) during the hearing of the Mac-
donald case. Judge Power, being in want of a pen,
had to send his Associate in quest of one. “ This
is what comes, ” remarked His Honor tragically,
“of having no tipstaff !” And the great heart of
Bananaland quivered with sympathetic indigna-
tion.
The Tory party in Federal politics is
striking some snags. The Melbourne election, at
which Malcolm M’Eacharn beat Dr. Maloney by
a very narrow majority, has been declared void,
and the matter is to be fought out afresh. Also
big landlord Blackwood seems in a fair way to lose
his Riverina seat to J. M. Chanter on the ground
that a large number of votes for Chanter were
wrongly declared informal.
Britain’s alien population is making itself
a nuisance in a new way. Recruiting officers
complain that they are pestered with applications
from indigent aliens for admission to the militia,
membership of which has a cash value. Extra
precautions are suggested as necessary to prevent
the militia being swamped by shambling Russian
Jews in the clo’ trade and all manner of miscel-
laneous aliens of dubious fighting capacity.
Another “Federal extravagance” lie
knocked on the head. Last December’s election
(including the N.S.W. State Referendum) only
cost £46,119 for 1,893,586 electors, as against
£56,331 which the thriftless States spent in
taking the opinions of 974,594 voters in ’Ol. The
economical Philp Government actually spent
£12,342 in ’Ol to do less work than the Common-
wealth Government accomplished for £6515.
Tax-dodging in Queensland isn’t nearly as
profitable as it used to be. The State Govern-
ment having indicated that its dealings with the
looal government bodies must be on a strictly
business basis, the latter are beginning to
give hints to the same effect to their own con-
stituents. Under district court warrants the
Toowong town council the other day auctioned
47 pieces of land on which rates hadn’t been paid
for years.
The Stinking Fish cry travels far. Thus
the Kansas City Journal :
Australia is rapidly losing population because of
business demoralisation caused by the Government
catering to organised labor. Australia seems to be
almost as badly union-ridden as Chicago.
Chicago, by the way, has lately increased in
population more quickly than any other city in
the world, and is the place least afflicted with
“business demoralisation.” So there’s hope for
Australia yet.
George Reid yes-noes in a pitiably clumsy
way nowadays. When he was raging in the
Representatives against the clause under which
the Six Hatters were not excluded, a query was
thrown at him as to why he supported the clause
when the Bill whs before Parliament. The Owner
of the Dog blandly replied that he only supported
it “as a matter of negotiation.” Which remark
seemed about as full of meaning as if he had sup-
ported the clause as a matter of Dutch cheese or
on account of the equator.
The history of the but-
ton industry in the United
States evidences the fallacy
of the Foreign-traders’ con-
tention that Protection in-
evitably means increased
cost to the consumer. Prior
to the M'Kinley tariff the
United States relied ex-
clusively upon imports for
its button supply. On the
imposition of the duty of
30 per cent, the price of
buttons immediately leaped
from 30 cents to 80 cents
per gross, and remained at
that for awhile—until local
factories were established.
Then the price rapidly
dropped until the buttons
that used to cost 30 cents
per gross under Freetrade
are sold at from 18 cents to
25 cents per gross. Result
of the duty—cheaper but-
tons than before the duty,
and employment for some
10,000 workmen in the fac-
tories alone. Australia has
already struck this same
experience with matches
and various other things.
All the members of the
Legislative Council of
Papua are Government offi-
, cials, and any ordinance
the Administration deems desirable is passed
without dissent. The Australian Government
should see that a reasonable number of non-official
members of the community are appointed to the
Council. It might result in better advice being
given to His Excellency than under the present
one-man arrangement.
Jawbone Neild is lecturing the Sydney
dailies for their persistent allusions to J. H.
Symon as “ Leader of the Opposition ” in the
Senate. Jawbone styles himself as “ Senior
Senator,” because he secured the largest number
of votes polled at the recent elections. He even
styles himself “senior among all the members of
Parliament in His Majesty’s dominions,” or some-
thing like that. The time will probably come
when Neild will rush out wjldly in his cocked hat
and style himself senior archangel.
Cabled that in Johannesburg, under free
and enlightened British rule, our colored fellow-
subject is to be put in gaol if he is found walking
on the footpath. As an inferior being, our
colored fellow-subject, who is supposed to have
been set free under the British flag, is to take a
subservient position in the gutter. Will Spruce
Myth, who is so angry because we don’t give the
nigger all the rights he is supposed to enjoy else-
where under the blessed Flag, please make a
note of our colored fellow-subject ?
AT THE COMMONWEALTH CIRCUS.
The feature of Mr. Keid’s speech was its strong de-
nunciation of the merchants of Sydney who met the
other day and denounced advanced legislation and the
Labor party. The point of interest to members was
what the deliverance was to be taken to mean. Mr.
Reid at the elections cut himself off from the Labor
party, and after the elections the Labor party, at a
caucus, declared that they could not accept a coalition
with him at the head. Mr. Reid, himself, now attacks
the merchants in Sydney, who denounce the Labor
party. Mr. Reid will probably await developments. This
seems at present to be his aim .—Daily paper.
Ist. Spectator: “My word, ain’t that Reid good
at them somersaults ? ”
2nd. Spectator : “ Yes, and can’t he keep it up.
That’s 298 I’ve counted, and ’e don’t seem
likely to stop.”
The attitude of the National Ass in respect
to the Adelaide trams is that, whereas it will agree
to the State buying them, it will not consent to
the State electrifying the system or working it
afterwards, as the Ass dreads “an increase in the
influence of the Trades Hall party.” As the Ass
absolutely dominates the Legislative Council in
S.A.. it will be seen that the prospects of complete
nationalisation are not rosy. The idea is to lease
the trams to a private company. Then instead of
being horse trams they will be Ass trams.
N.S.W. Public Works Department will
shortly, it is reported, be placed in charge of a
commission so far as construction works are con-
cerned. The commission will have the same inde-
pendence as the Railway Commission, and will be
instructed to put an end to all political influence
in the carrying out of State works. There is,
however, no proposal to do away with the political
influence which secures the initiation of unneces-
sary works to conciliate the member for Dog
Hollow ; and that is the greater evil.
Little while ago N.S.W. Electric Tele-
graph Department was presided o\er by a
superintendent (£BOO a year), an assistant-superin-
tendent (£7OO a year), and a general manager
(£OOO a year). To-day the same work is per-
formed by one plain manager, an old-time
operator, at about £5OO a year, and the general
public fails to note any difference in the working
of the department, except that things are going
slightly better. The Federation is certainly
strong on administrative economy.
W.A. Laborites are not resting. Labor
nominees will contest 36 at least of the 50 seats in
the State House. Even Bunbury, sacred to
Groperdom as the birthplace of Bigjohn, is in
danger of desecration, whilst Northam and
Albany, two old strongholds of the reactionaries,
are being assailed with fair prospects of success.
Under the new re-distribution there are 16 gold-
fields seats, and 14 of these are certainties for
Labor. The other two (Boulder and Menzies) are
held by Ministers, and a Minister’s position,
solidly fortified by loan money, is a pretty tough
kopje to rush.
“War E.” wants to wake up N.S.W.
Lands Office:—
A little more about that Ganmain Reserve (N.S.W.).
Something under half of it will be available for selection
within a few days, and already applications are pouring
in from all parts of this state, Victoria, and even South
Australia. It is ideal agricultural land, covered with a
luxuriant growth of grass, on which a few sheep are lost.
There is an ominous silence concerning the bigger por-
tion of this reserve, and if somebody does not soon give
the Lands Department a vigorous kick it will fall asleep
on its desk whilst a foreign financial co. sneaks away
with 20,000 acres of splendid country. Strange that the
Lands Department fails to see how much better for the
country it is to establish 30 families in place of one
boundary-rider! But dust blinds a lot of people some-
times.
.Nicholas Hawken, M.L.C., states what is
exactly the opposite to a fact in a letter on the
shearing labor question to Sydney Telegraph :
The A.W.U. is the grouping of the nomadic system of
shearing of times fast going by, and that of the M.S.U. is
the grouping of the resident system of shearing, which is
now stationary, and a true advance on the old nomadic
system, which was principally romantic in its lambing-
down incidents, and only defensible under uncivilised con-
ditions. The M.S.U. seems to be composed of resident
settlers and sons of settlers.
The truth is that the M.S.U. was directly orga-
nised by a shearing “contractor ” (under the con-
tract system a team travels round from shed to
shed taking off the clip at an agreed rate, the men
being paid weekly wages by the contractor),
whilst it is the A.W.U. which is mainly recruited
from the stationary worker who relies upon a
month or two’s shearing to help him through the
year.
“Marque Reel” : I think the W.A. Labor-
party is making a mistake in opposing Minea
Minister Gregory at the coming elections. Gregory
during his term of office has done more for the
worker in a practical way than any other M.P. in.
Australia. With his public batteries hundreds of
men have been enabled to make a good living out
of shows that they would have otherwise been
starved out of. He has also proved that State
enterprise can be carried on at a profit, even after
reducing charges to customers—in some cases more
than 50 per cent. I recently heard him say that
it was his ambition to see miners working then
own shows and keeping the money in the country
instead of sending the profits to London. I paid
40s. per ton for crushing ; then after the State:
battery was erected, had it done for 14s. Gregory
is a democrat with a tendency towards socialism,
and should not be opposed by the worker.
If the Age does not want to completely
lose prestige as a director-general of Victorian,
politics it should stop trusty henchman J. L.
Dow from standing for Parliament any more.
Dow goes out to brave the storm of every general;
election held in Victoria, and the other day
thought he had a “sure thing ” in the Donald and
Swan Hill seat vacated by Agent-General Taver-
ner. But Dow went down even worse than usual,
with bottom place among the three candidates,
and despite the usual good whoop-up by the Age.
When that paper finally alienated the working
man, and made a bid for the support of the Argus.
farmers, it made the mistake of its life, and if it
wants to cover up that mistake it had better start
by muzzling Dow and chaining him to the desk on
which he churns out his agricultural articles.
Dow’s cake is dough.
Perry, N.S.W. Minister for Education, is
trying to inculcate a spirit of self-reliance. The
Sydney Telegraph reports him thus :
Mr. Perry says that he always tries to instil into them
(school children) a spirit of independence and self-help.
As an instance of this, he mentions that applications have
frequently been received from teachers for appliances to
enable object lessons to be taught, but he has always in-
formed the applicants that the better plan was for the
children to obtain the funds by holding some sort of
entertainment.
This is very proper, as the rising generation will
need all its self-help to meet the obligations
incurred by Mr. Perry and his colleagues. But
couldn’t the Minister exercise his influence over
the rest of the Cabinet and get it to have a scrap
or two of self-reliance? If the teacher could show
his scholars the adjacent bridge which the
Government built without running to Bull for the
money, that would be something like an object
lesson.
Spruce Myth, at Sydney women’s branch
of the Empire League, the other day :
Australians must, if they desired the welfare of the
Empire, show respect for the rights and feelings of their
fellow-subjects. The late Queen had guaranteed to the
people of India, at the time of the peace treaty of
Lucknow, that her Indian subjects should henceforth
enjoy equal privileges with all the other subjects of the
Empire. The people of Australia had belied that
promise, and treated the Indian people as unfit to mix
with the Anglo-Saxon race.
Well, when Biitain itself carries out Queen
Victoria’s promise, and allows its Indian subjects
to “enjoy equal privileges” with other subjects,
of her Empire, it will be time enough for
Australia to think about it. At present, while
Britain declares that its Indian subject is such an
inferior person as to be unworthy even of a voice
in the management of his own country, Australia
cannot be blamed for thinking that he is an
inferior person, and refusing to be seen in his
company. In fact, Australia is loyal enough to
believe that Britain knows its Indian subject
much better than Spruce Myth does.

“ Marque Reel ” from Kalgoorlie (W.A.) :
The Kalgoorlie Miner has been giving Prime Minister
Deakin advice re the encouragement of immigration, and
its first cry is “ Down with Protection.” Now, this is
what puzzles me. I have been working in W.A. for some
years. This extra-protected State is the best of the
Australias for the worker, yet every year some of
my mates leave here for still more heavily-protected
Maoriland, and after getting there they advise their
former mates to come. These are not the ne’er-do-wells ;
they are the men who have put in a few years
stacking up some beans, and now want bo go to the best
place to make use of the vegetables in question. It
makes me think sometimes that there is something in
Protection, after all.

Premier See would have earned more than a
little approbation had he called the N.S.W.
Assembly together to rescind the resolutions under
which the Public Works Committee goes on
flouting public opinion and grabbing from the
Treasury. The seven members plead that they
must keep on investigating impossible schemes as
Parliament directed the investigations to take
place, and thus the simplest way out would be for
Parliament to meet again and quash what is
admittedly a scandalous imposition. On the
other hand, if the Committee sincerely wished to
end the costly humbug, enough members could
voluntarily retire to leave no quorum. As things
are going, the £69 a month drawn by each member
for compiling reports that will not be read, and
that can have no effect if read, stands as an in-
dictment both of the Ministry and the Committee.
Retrenchment of a few clerks lias no meaning
while the gang of seven, with £l7 a week each,
faces the public. The Assembly members of the
committee draw Parliamentary salaries as well.
A mild reaction having set in against the
brutal treatment to which Victorian public ser-
vants have been subjected by the Iceberg Govern-
ment, Premier Bent is beginning to hedge. He
has caused it to be put around in a muffled sort of
way that he is not averse to allowing the public
servants their increments this year. During the
late race week in Melbourne he sent out a
memorandum to the heads of departments asking
them to arrange a half-holiday for the staff. Also,
he is busy flying a kite in respect to the re-engage-
ment of some of the engine men who were dis-
missed from the railway service in consequence of
the strike. All this is happening just at the time
when the public servants are thinking hard over
the stamp of man to be sent to Parliament to
represent them. A meeting of the service decided
that whoever was elected should sign the Labor
pledge, and Thomas Beef would fain persuade the
service to think better of that.

wasting by evaporation, and contains no grit or other

The Maori system of land titles (as many
as 200 owners may be concerned in a quarter-acre
section) is the brown man’s best protection from
the importunities of the land-hungry white.
Much good land is still held by the Maoris in the
north island, between Hawke’s Bay and the Bay of
Plenty, that would have long ago been mopped up
but for the impossibility of finding half the owners
sober and of one mind, or even of finding out who
half the owners are.
According to a cablegram from London :
In reply to a deputation, Lord Milner said he would
stake his reputation that for every 10,000 colored laborers
imported to the Transvaal 10,000 whites would be added
to the population in three years.
It is a pity Milner hasn’t something more valu-
able than his reputation to stake. Let him look
at the adjacent Natal and see how things
worked out there. In the last year for which
figures are available, 1900-1901, Natal’s white
population diminished by 1130, while its Asiatic
population increased by 4016. The Asiatic isn’t
finding work for the white man in Natal; instead,
he has got to the point where he feels capable of
crowding the white man out.
Pumpkin Smith’s scoff that B. R. Wise
“couldn’t get an elective seat in the N.S.W.
Parliament,” is a clumsy lie. Wise represented
one representative for 11 years and gave it up to go
among the Fossils and get them to put through his
Arbitration Bill (which they did, and the Fossils
have been kicking themselves in a mild and pre-
historic manner ever since). The unreliable state-
ment also recalls a bygone election incident,
when Pumpkin urged a N.S.W. electorate to
return him instead of Barton, on the ground that
Barton was too great a statesman, and too much
interested in the welfare of all Australia, to keep
a constant and efficient eye on the local culvert.
On the other hand, Pumpkin assured the electors
that he (Pumpkin) was just the humble parish
insect whose eye would be glued on the local
culvert all the time. The Pumpkin Smiths of
Australia get many elective seats in that manner,
while the man with a big outlook fails, through
inability to get down to the Pumpkin level.
The Tory Arcus on the Labor members in the
Australian Parliament:—
The Homes bear the aspect of all that is buoyant and
hopeful and self-satisfied. How can a young man avoid
being pleased with himself who has been redeemed from
the slavery of seven-and-a-half hours’ manual work per
day, raised to a salary of £8 per week, and instructed
that he has nothing to do but wear his Sunday clothes
all the week and assist to make laws? Officially the
young Labor member holds that Australia is going to the
dogs, and that there can be nothing satisfactory about it
till everything is changed. But he cannot assume an
appropriate appearance of gloom.
Which is a cad and bounder’s way of throwing
political mud. Subtract from the £7 15s. a wee k
the Australian M.P. draws, election and travel -
ling expenses and cadging exactions, and there is
a very bare living wage left—a poorer wage on the
whole than the man would get in a mine or an
office. The Argus knows this as well as The
Bulletin does. But the Tory crowd in Australia
has no imagination, no education, and little sense
of decency ; it is always justifying Higinbotham’s
gibe—“ the wealthy lower orders.”
*
Some people are hard to please. Though his co.
got an award in its favor which has been most
loyally obeyed, the chairman of the Broken Hill
Proprietary Co. said, at the half-yearly share-
holders’ meeting in Melbourne, the other day,
that “it is a grave question whether legislative
interference between employer and employed,
such as the Arbitration Act, serves much good
purpose.” Yet it is a dead certainty that, but for
this Act (and the hope of it even before it came),
there would have been a strike at the Broken Hill
mines long ago. Before the Labor party got into
Parliament with strength enough to push through
this Act, the B.H. Prop, mine was laid up through
strikes three times within four years—once for
four months at a stretch. And the Government
had to “ interfere ” then just as much as it “ inter-
feres ” now ; in one case alone the “ interference”
cost over £20,000 for special police protection,
courts, etc. It always has been the case, curiously
enough, that the men who gibber most about
“ State interference between employer and em-
ployed ” are the first to whistle for the police when
a strike occurs.


A significant sentence from the speech of
Henry Butler in handing over Iceberg Irvine’s
reward for bis political labors on behalf of the
Tory element of Victoria : “You did what was
required.” Exactly, He did “what was re-
quired” by the Fatmen, and the Fatmen duly
paid him. Irvine saved his face to the last. He
took the illuminated address and his wife took the
cheque for £2050. It somehow recalls a far-back
episode in Australian history. A great, calm,
hairy “constitutional” statesman was once
offered a cash testimonial when departing for a
holiday, and refused it with a shocked expression.
His soul was outraged. But be allowed the purse
to be given to a female relative, and it was duly
presented on the ship as they were leaving. Ihe
statesman made some appropriate and digmhed
remarks about the Constitution. Then as the
vessel got away a slight scuffle was .observed
between the great calm statesman and his female
relative. The pillar of integrity was recovering
the money.

If the man on the land wishes to remain
there in peace, he should employ W. N. Willis,
Land Agent, 21 Bligh-street as his Agent. No

Most people in S. Australia seem to have
been born or arrived in 1850 or thereabouts. Ex-
Premiers Kingston, Cockburn, and Holder all
date from that year ; so does Gordon, who held
one or two Ministries up; Bonython, of the
Advertiser, is 18 months older ; Premier Jenkins
came along in 1851; and Thomas, of the Register,
the same year.
HEARING AND HOW TO KEEP IT.
An instructive article on “ How to preserve the
hearing, and how to cure Deafness ” appears in
the last number of the “Review of Ear, Nose and
Throat Diseases.” Anyone whose hearing is fail-
ing should send for a free copy of this Medical
Journal, a number of copies having been sent
out from London, England. All you require to
do in order to obtain a copy is to send your name
and address to : —“ Review ” c/o Box 241, G.P.0.,
Sydney. With the “Review” there is also a
report form by means of which anyone can obtain
free advice by post.*
The Inky Way.
It is hoped by the tired directors and shareholders in
the New Zealand Times that the appointment ot
Sydney Jenkinson, sub-editor of Melbourne Argus, as
manager and literary supervisor of Wellington’s big
morning daily, will send that most unsatisfactory paper
ahead. Mr. Jenkinson is to receive £7OO a year for three
years, and £lOOO thereafter. The Times has only paid
three dividends in goodness knows how many years. It
nas always been unfortunate, and as a literary production
it is the most wooden in the State. Abie men don’t get
sufficient inducement to stay on its staff, and even very
mediocre reporters look in for a month or so only to skip
elsewhere. Once upon a time it was thought that a cer-
tain journalist would stay because he was going to marry
the editor s daughter, but a few weeks after the wedding
he also flitted to another billet.

POETRY AND PROSE.
NEW ARRIVAL : "By Jove. What a magnificent sunset. What a glorious effect.”
OLD HAND: "My oath; it's BLANKY fine, isn’t it?”
Perth Morning Herald has a reporter who is let loose
on mail steamers now and again. He turns up in an hour
or two with a series of interviews, mostly with pursers
and stewards. Herald the other day solemnly printed a
column of the views of a cook on one of the French boats ;
cookey assured anxious Westralians that there wasn’t
going to be any trouble in China. Another time it had
an “ interesting interview ” with a German ship’s barber
on the fiscal question—only in the paper he was described
as “ one of the qfficers” of the G.M.S. Sauerkraut.
* * * *
“ 0.K.” : Wrongly stated that bright, seniceable
pressman Donnelly “ developed consumption when
roughing it in the service of the Argus.” He was cough-
ing badly before he came to Melbourne from Shepparton
(Vic.) and got a position on the Argus staff, after which
his health improved for a while under the influence, ap-
parently, of a quack medicine. Then the night work
began to tell on him, arid finally the sick man retired to
an Adelaide sanatorium to see what six months’ rest
would do for him.

Alleged that the reporter of a New York “yellow”
journal got this memo, of instructions one day from his
chief: —“ A prominent wealthy young fellow named
Stuart was up in the West Side police-court for drunken-
ness this morning. He pleaded to have his name kept
out of the newspapers for fear that his mother would hear
of it. She has heart trouble, and he says the shock would
kill her. Go over to her house at ——■, West Seventy-
second-street, and tell her about it.. See if you can’t kill
her. We need news.” Fortunately, so the story goes,
the lady turned out to be at Syracuse.
* * * *
The Tientsin (China) Courier is a model of caution.
Recently an enormous fish of some unknown species was
caught off the coast, and was cut up and sold. The
Courier stated that three hundred people who had eaten of
it died suddenly, and many others were seriously ill. Then
it remarked, with a commendable desire not to jump at
conclusions, that “ possibly the fish was poisonous.” The
S.M. Herald should hire that Tientsin Courier man. He
is just the grave, thinkful man that the Herald wants.

Several London papers have lately adopted the “hidden
fortune” game as a method of advertising (it has been,
in a way, imitated by the Sydney cycling people, who
offer to give away cheques in the street to persons
producing one of their advertisement). Paris Matin in-
troduced the method in 1903, hiding vouchers for £1000;
by reading a story in the paper you got hints as to the
whereabouts of the valuables. Since then several London
papers have followed suit. The proprietors buried the
money by night in a lonely part of England, and the
serial story about a hidden treasure which commenced
immediately after supplied the indications in an obscure
way, and people who read the indications wrongly dug up
several counties in quest of the loot till it looked like a
general revival of agriculture. The game is dead now in
the country of its origin ; the French police stopped it as
a lottery.

MURPHY: “ Y’ old scoundrelsh — y' fill ’em up's

(I “ Nym ” tells what may be truth or may be Sapphira :
“Thewai’s of the Australian author are not all beans.
Joe Furphy ('Tom Collins’), the Vic. literary iron-
moulder, tank-sinker and bush-whacker, has had his
little troubles since his book, ‘ Such is Life,’ swamped the
booksellers. Joe has been the recipient of letters from
the four quarters of the prlobe. Some writers praise,
others blame, but the multitude have written to tell the
author how they would have worked out the plot if they
had written the book. The worst trouble was in store
when the new year was born, for Joe became the recipient
of a proposal of marriage from an admirer, who availed
herself of the Leap Tear privilege to lay her wealth and
herself at the author’s feet. His ready pen lagged con-
siderably as he wrote declining the honor, owing to the
trifling fact that he had a good wife living and daughters
old enough, but too modest, to propose themselves.”

“Scribo ”: Andrew Bent’s Tasmanian newspaper,
published in 1817, was animated by these beautiful
aspirations. “We desire to encourage the cloudless
Eromise of rectified communion, rejecting each effusion,
owever splendid, of degenerated ouriositv and perverted
genius, of misanthropical acerbity and calumnious retro-
spection.” But it was an anti-climax to conclude ; “We
hope to publish with the aid of a man and a boy.”
• * * *
Donald, who is representing the Sydney D.T. at the
China war (he had a position on the China Mail, Hong-
aonfi', and offered to go forward at his own expense),
writes in his first war letter :—“ In order to be able to
move with the troops and view the engagements I set
out for Tokio to-day. Thence my movements will be
guided by the sword, and I will make with all speed for
where the war is thickest.” This high-flown but well-
meaning youth will probably find that, in that country of
tremendous distances, war is about as “thick” as two
blades of grass in a square-mile paddock, and he will get
there much more reliably if he is guided by the victual-
waggon instead of the sword.
You can differentiate between a mean and a generous
district by the pars, in the local newspaper. The district
where John Smith “left at this office some magnificent
specimens of the Bon Chretien pear ” is all right ; the
place where “we were shown yesterday by Mr. Tim
Jones a very large watermelon,” etc., is to be avoided.

Significant headings from an English paper—and a
friendly paper, too :
MR. CHAMBERLAIN
NOT GOING TO AUSTRALIA,
HIS WORK
LYING AT HOME.
Sub-editors should watch their headings ; half the news-
paper mistakes, and a good proportion of the newspaper
libels, occur in the headings.
* * * *
“ Shellback ”: Melbourne paper war maps are all
wrong. The editors should walk down the street when
they can find time and visit Donne’s, where a fine chart
of the scene of operations can be obtained for about a
dollar. Also they might give some hard-up sailor man
acquainted with the locality a job to keep them posted
up in details, and thus avoid making ludicrous errors.
* * * *
Sydney H. Jenkinson, who has been day sub. on the
Melb. Argus for over 20 years, goes over to Wellington
(Maoriland) to manage and edit the Times. Jenkinson
came to Victoria from the boiling springs country, where
he had been a schoolmaster. He was a valuable sub., and
built up a great reputation during the boom by throwing
out good copy to make room for big, highly remunerative
advertisements. The management used to say “ Jenkin-
son is the only man who seems to be able to make room
for all the advertisements offering.” And it was true ;
there was nothing ever written that. Jenkinson wasn’t
capable of rejecting to make space for a land sale notice.
A brother of his was appointed to the Maoriland Legis-
lative Council by Seddon,
“Alcinous”: The brightest gem in Australian news-
paperdom is undoubtedly the Brisbane Telegraph.
Editor Woolnough is an ex-parson, with a capacity for
metaphysics and a consequent knack of talking in
parables. He fights terrible battles with himself in his
leaders as to what is crime and why education is
demoralising. Sometimes he issues from the contest
victorious, with his victim (himself) underneath ; some-
times he is badly beaten by the victor (himself) on top,
but generally the struggle ends in a draw, both of him
being injured. The paper claims the largest circu-
lation in Queensland. Giving the devil his due, the
average issue would contain the latest news if it only
came out two days before its headed date, though as
regards its anti-Federation wail, it has not yet reached
Ist Jan., 1901, and its black-labor politics are awav back
in the dark ages. No special articles by local writers are
allowed to desecrate its pages, but it has about the best
pair of scissors in Australia, and its gum-pot is like the
widow’s cruse and faileth not.
THE PROTEST OF THE PIPE AND GLASS CLUB.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the low-roofed tavern-room, full of pleasant smoke
perfume,
We were drinking-, in the golden even glow ;
But, alas, the company was not what it used to be
When the Pipe and Glass Club met there years ago.
And our President arose and said : “ Brethren, I suppose
You know well what we are here assembled for?
We wish strongly to protest”—here he struck his grand
old chest—
'•’Gainst this cruel Japanese and Russian War.
“ When the nations go to fight with their bayonets
shining bright,
And their trumps and drums, and banners flaunting gay,
Bo they know, or do they care, for the woe they cause
elsewhere,
And the humble little industries they slay?
“I, myself, as you know all, earn a pittance sure, if
sinall,
Writing yarns re birds and reptiles, and so forth,
But no paper will from me purchase Natural History
When Unnatural History’s making in the North.
“ Our friend Tityrus looks pale as he sits and sips his
ale—
Well he may, for when the sword-blades rasp and ring,
And the armies fight and bleed (in the papers) who would
read
(Or would print) his ‘ Gentle Art of Gardening.’
“ There’s another friend I see—who will buy his Poetry
When the wild beast on the readers is let loose,
When the Press shall shout and shriek and shall like a
shambles reek
For his rhymes of love and dove who will find use ?
“ VV’hen the nations go to fight with their bayonets
shining bright,
To themselves it may conceivably be plaj'—
But, alas, my friends, I fear they care nothing for us here,
Nor for all the humble industries they slay.”
In the low-roofed tavern-room there was silence sad and
gloom,
Yet, somehow, I half expected blood would flow—
But, ah me, the company was not what it used to ba
When the Pipe and Glass Club met there years ago.

A BALLADE OF DEAD "PARS."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We who follow the Inky Way,
Seeking p„>nce that are all too few,
Hunting paragraphs night and day,
Chasing items till all is blue—
When we calculate what is due,
Coining money in Fancy's mints—
What of the “ stuff ” that wins not
through ?
Where are the pars, that no one prints ?
Gone for ever the gladsome lay—
Faith 1 we thought it a marvel, too 1—
Someone reckoned it common clay,
Passed it out—and we never knew !
Racy yarns—some were partly true—
Memory clothes them in tender tints ;
Someone said they were far too “ blue
Where are the pars, that no one prints ?
Fame’s a star of deceitful ray—
Will-o’-the-wisp that leaves no clue ;
Hope, the devil that points the way,
Fails at touch of the pencil blue.
Tough the fight for us—inky crew !
Rugged the pathway, sharp the flints ;
Hungry, the Basket gapes in view—
Where are the pars, that no one prints ?
Envoi :
Editors 1 ponder the things ye do ! / / /
Lend your ears to our woeful hints 1 /%
Tell us kindly, and tell us true— /,
Where are the pars, that no one /
prints !
BOREE.
THE POET MULLIGAN.
[For The Bulletin.]
Once on a time there lived a man
Who went by the name of Mulligan ;
This Mulligan had a poet’s brow,
And a poet’s soul—and he owned a cow.
Now Mulligan’s verse, it took the bun
(The cow he stole from a neighboring run);
It is true that he wasn’t a bard of fame,
But he poured forth verses just the same.
One day the cow to his humpy strode,
And chewed and swallowed his master ode—
The ode into which he had thrown his soul—
She chewed and swallowed that great work whole.
And then she died, and her soul arose
To the place where the soul of a good cow goes,
And there she knocked, and a Presence cried :
“ You can’t come here with a pome inside.
“ We don’t object to your bovine soul,
But we do to Mulligan’s—part or whole ;
You must go and get frizzled and grilled and fried
Till of Mulligan’s soul you’re purified.”
Then the soul of the cow went down below
To the red-hot spot where the coal-fires glow,
And the Ghoul who there at the portal sits
Said : “ Mulligan can’t come here in bits."
Then the soul of the cow with a logic keen
Said: “ What are you givin’ us ? What d’ye mean ?
The soul of Mulligan, whole, was throwed
Into that chewed-up master ode.”
But the Ghoul he laughed and said : “ If so,
He never had very much soul to throw ;
It won’t pan out a grain to the cow,
And to smelt it out wouldn’t pay nohow."
Then the soul of the cow began to moo,
And said : “ Pray, then, what am I to do 1"
And the Ghoul he said with a frizzly hiss :
“ Dunno 1 Dunno 1 But gerrout of this 1 "
Then the cow she turned disconsolate,
And wandered away from the blazing gate—
And that was a hundred years ago,
And what became of her I don’t know.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
“O.K.”: “Mud” cricket shows, firstly, the
individual resourcefulness or helpfulness of the batsmen,
and, secondly, it marks a distinction between the charac-
ter of the English and Australian fieldsman. The latter,
ic s-ems to me, is the more cool and alert when chances
are frequently happening in unexpected places. He is
not so easily flustered as the other man when the un-
looked-for chance comes straight towards him, but he is
not quite so quick (experienced?) in judging the flight of
a ball for which he has to turn and run. Six and a-half
innings in Test matches have been played at Melbourre
this season on a wet, treacherous wicket, Australia
getting the better scores because the visitors missed the
greater number of catches. As regards the howling
strength of the two teams, there was practically nothing
to choose. Sixty odd wickets were taken alto-
gether, and there were only seven individual scores of
more than 20 runs. Trumper and Tyldesley, and, in a
less degree, Foster showed a distinct superiority over the
other men. Tyldesley, the only batter who achieved
double figures at each attempt, made one score of 62
without getting any concessions from the field. Trumper
had the highest aggregate—74, 35, and a “duck”—but
he was twice let off before he had begun to knock up his
74. Noble (31), Duff (31), Foster (30) and Hopkins (25)
were each of them favored by exceptional luck, neverthe-
;less they seemed to deserve all they got in comparison
with several faint-hearted potterers who were trying to
■“ play the game ” — i.e., the fair weather game—the only
.game they know how to play. I would call Rnight the
most utterly hopeless of these careful batsmen on a bad
■wicket. He scored three “ ducks ” and a 2 in four tries.
’The veteran Trumble was almost as tame as Rnight.
Hirst seemed incapable of wise strokes, and of the others
who did at least try to do something, little Rhodes
'Showed the most discretion in his hitting. I heard it
[remarked, about 1000 times, that none of the batting on
.these wet pitches was real cricket. The play was too
full of incident. The runs and disasters came too rapidly.
Real cricket is a slow compiling of long scores by both
• sides. Give me unreal cricket every time.
* * * *
For some time on the Monday afternoon and
Tuesday morning of the fifth test match, the wicket was
in the proper state of deadness to suit any fast bowler
who adopts the first principle of his business, and tries
.to hit the stumps with a straight-pitched one. Cotter,
and then Hirst, beat some of the batsmen by the mere
pace at which they rocked ’em in. It was a treat to see
the wicket shattered, or one stump sent flying out of the
ground. Cotter, for a while, bowled dangerously, and
men were caught off him through hitting at balls which
might have been left to bounce high over the wicket,
with a chance of going for 4 byes. Later, he was able to
keep the leather low, and Foster and Rnight were suc-
cessively knocked out in the old-fashioned way. The
quickness of the ball deceived the eye. YY'hen the Aus-
tralians came in, Hirst, with his second delivery, banged
Cotter’s stump out of the ground, and his first ball next
morning spoilt the great Trumper’s wicket. “Scientific
defence” has discovered no sure way of dealing with a
fast ball which, being pitched in front of the batsman’s
reach, catapults straight for his wicket. How is he to
“ place ” it accurately when he can’t strike it at all ? And
if pace can beat the bat on a dull wicket, what sort of
chance would science have against a new variety of
round-arm ball that skimmed the ground at “ lightning ”
speed? To see a few stumps shattered by fast, straight
balls is to conceive the possibility of a round-arm, or even
an under-arm, bowler who could get 10 for 0 on the
hardest pitch—the harder the better, if the artist had
the trick of putting in bailers at an express rate.

* * * *
Sydney first-grade cricket grew a remarkable
crop of centuries on Saturday. There were five matches,
and four of them saw the hundred passed. Pite made
137 for Sydney against Paddington, and was still going
strong at stumps draw. Mackay slogged 149 for Burwood
against Redfern, before he was stumped by Baines off
Johnson. Farquhar had bashed 126 off University for
Central Cumberland when Mackay caught him off one
from Futter ; and Mackenzie had painted 109 on the
scoring-board for YVaverley against Leichhardt-Balmain,
when the end came through Carrick stumping him off
Sullivan. All this looks very well, and moves men to ask
why should Australian test teams have tails, but then
what would the scores have been had the batsmen faced
Hirst, Braund, Arnold and Bosanquet?

The tour of the Warner team in Australia will
probably end in a big money loss to the promoting
body, the Marylebone Cricket Club.
• • • •
The persistently aggressive bookie who stalks
on to private racecourses and bets, despite the club’s
prohibition, is making Maoriland beaks tired, and two of
him were lately gaoled for a month without the option,
at Christchurch. Other cases were listed to follow, when
counsel for defence suddenly rose and suggested that the
regulations against the fraternity were “ unreasonable,”
and it wouldn’t be a bad idea if the cases were adjourned,
in order that a decision might be obtained from the
Court of Appeal on the subject. This was eventually
agreed to, though the necessity for doing so wasn’t
apparent. The assertion that by-laws passed against
undesirable persons—illegal tote odds-layers, spielers,
and the riff-raff of the turf—are “unreasonable” is
amusing.

Sport Royal, after winning the Ballarat Cup
from a respectable field, with 9st. up, seems to have a
rough outside chance for Sydney Cup with Bst., including
the penalty. At the recent Y.R.C. Autumn meeting
visitors from YVestralia had a lot to say about Sport
Royal, and the horse was well liked bv the touts, although
he didn t appear to be quite himself. Hickenbotham has
evidently improved Sport Royal since he came back to his
native place from the West a few weeks ago. Given a
little more improvement, this stayer should pay expenses
at Randwick in one event or other. He may not be fast
enough for the Cup, but he’s a racehorse accustomed to
carrying big weights on the goldfields.

The latest horse-racing yarn cotnes from Colab
(Vic.). A local resident having dreamed Mairp for the
Newmarket, went to bed again with visionary intentions
and dreamed that he saw F.J.A. winning the All-Aged
Stakes and Emir winning the Champion. Backed the
three for himself, and advised his friends to follow his

a limited company and break the Ring on the strength of
his information.

remarked pleasantly—not to say sympathetically—of the
racing at the recent Warwick Farm meet: “ It is only to

running as a pardonable offence, it is only to be expected
that the Turf will get dirtier every year.
* « * *
The handy Mairp is a strong argument against
early racing for horses. As a 2-.vear-old in ’96 Mairp
never saw the post. He ran only three times in ’97 and
twice as a 4-year-old, since when he has run 80 races
winning the Newmarket in his tenth year.

A.J.O.’s coming Autumn meeting thinned the Doncaster

* * * *
Famous English amateur runner, Sid Thomas
(up to a few years back the best at his game the world
over, since YV. G. George), is coming Australiawards in
quest of matches at 3, 5 and 10 miles. Thomas fell from
prominence through becoming a professional and having
fewer opportunities for racing. Hard to think where he
can be accommodated unless Maorilander Huckstep,
Queensland Smith, or Victorian Lynas takes up the gage.
Thomas has covered 3m. in 14.24, 5 in 24.53 3-5, and 10 in
51.30, but that was over 10 years ago.
* * . •
Mr. R. F. Pickering writes: “ Mr. Eyre
Pickering has been shooting pretty straight of late.
YVithin the last fortnight he has won the * Double Rise'
at Botany with eight kills out of ten, and tied with Mr.
R. A. Hill for the Garrett testimonial, with 30 kills each,
without a break, both on the 27J yards mark. Altogether
during the last two weeks, he has accounted for 90 birds
out of 95.” There’s a pretty record of wanton butchery 1
This alleged “ sport ” is a cruel, wicked business without
one solitary redeeming feature or streak of manliness
about it. The man who kills pigeons at 27J yards will be
of no more use as a rifleman in time of warthan the ancient
kitchen-maid who wrings the neck of the Sunday-dinner
fowl in a dark, evil-smelling back yard. If Mr. Pickering
even ate the pigeons he murdered there might be some
justification for the wanton slaughter, but no man eats
90 pigeons in one week. Pigeon-shooting is a form of
amusement which should be suppressed by law.
♦ * * m
Ped. racing by night has “caught on” in
Queensland. Races, with prize money ranging from £l5
to £lOO, are now run regularly at Brisbane, Gympie,
Ipswich, Toowoomba, the Towers, and other places,
illuminants being electricity, “ Ritson light,” or gas.
Novice handicaps are included at some meetings, and the
local kid, with his pants rolled up, is now mostly engaged
in sprinting over the landscape, in order to get into
“ nick.”

Maoriland is threatening to send J. Arnst,
winner of last year’s Dunlop YVarrnambool road race, to
France to uphold the tui feathers in the great Paris-
Bordeatix road contest. If the money required (esti-
mated at £500) can be found he will go. Premier Seddon,
who is an enthusiastic sport and once threatened to send
away footballers at State expense, will probably justify
Government assistance Qn the ground of advertising the
State and its frozen meat, tinned tripe, and potted
tongues.
* * * •
If cyclist Taylor isn’t careful there’ll be a big
slump in his repute for holiness before he gets away from
Australia, and rabbit-fronted parsons will cease to arise
and preach sermons on him as a black and brilliant
reproach to the YVhite Australia policy. Major holds a
unique position ; he is the best cyclist here, he is
under a contract which practically prevents his disquali-
fication, and to biff him out of the programme would
practically ruin the big Sydney meeting. So the alleged
holy one can do pretty much as he darned well pleases.
All of which is a double-barrelled reason why he should
be extra circumspect. Yet his riding in the “Peter-
sham ” scratch five mile event was most reprehensible.
He looked round three times while passing the members’
stand, he wobbled dangerously for the other people, he
was fined £5 for interfering with MacFarland so that Mac.
had to retire from the race, and after the contest he was
seen on the green wrangling with the long white rider in
a manner which indicates that, although the Lord took
special care over the preparation of Major’s legs, the
making of his manners was badly slummed.
♦ * * *
The best race of Wednesday night, the first of
the Sydney Thousand meeting, was the “ Petersham ”
Five Mile scratch event. Eighteen started, and after
doing a lap were sent off to a flying start. A terrific
tussle for the lap prizes made the pace hot till the last
few laps were reached. It was a picturesque event. The
lamps shed a green glare. The riders, in as many colors
as a crazy quilt, went by with a swish that sounded
like the rain on a roof. Three-quarters of a lap
from home M’Donald made a tremendous bound which
put even Taylor’s meteoric leap in the shade, but the
dark rider bent down and glided up alongside. Then the
great long figure of MacFarland whizzed through the
green light, and 15,000 people stood_up and yelled as the
white American shot past the dark one. Then there was
a clang, MacFarland swayed, and rode off into the grass,
and the rest of the bunch came past the stands with a
swish. On the outside a man in a red guernsey was
coming up like a bird, but seemed to be unable to pass
the darkie, and Taylor, with the sweat lying in layers
along his black face, turned his head round and glared at
him, and then bent down to drive his pedals with all his
soul. Downing, the second man, was ten paces behind,
and close to him, only a few inches away, Don Walker
was third.
* * * •
The Mosman one mile handicap saw some good
racing. Floyd MacFarland won his heat from scratch in
2min. 0.1-ssec. In the next heat F. A. Murphy, of Vic.,
who was on the 90 mark, covered the remaining 1670yds.
in lmin. 51.1-ssee. Major Taylor, far away on scratch,
without pacing assistance, never got a look in,
and failed to qualify. In the final, Walker, Farley,
and M’Donald paced MacFarland splendidly, and
the front men did the same for one another just
as energetically, and the result was a savage tussle.
The front men took Plunkett of Maoriland along at a
tremendous pace, but the YValker crowd brought Mae-
Farland up to them half a lap from home and left him to
his own efforts. He swept round the bunch, and
Plunkett came away with a bound. Then, while women
waved umbrellas and screamed with excitement and men
yelled like an asylum full of deranged “ bottle-ohs,
MacFarland rushed past the struggling Maorilander and
finished three lengths ahead in lmin. 49sec., a new world s
competition record.
Farley’s win of the “Balmain” Five Mile
scratch race Saturday night was one of the brightest
things lately seen on the S.C.G. track. The main bunch
Were mooning along, with Major Taylor somewhere about
the tail end, all grimly determined not to do any pacing,
and saving themselves for an almighty rush at the finish.
In the fifth lap Burton, Chalmers, Farley, and Payne
rushed to the front and broke away like motors.
Chalmers fell back immediately and disappeared into the
main bunch, which reckoned the three in front were only
going for the lap prizes. The three paced one another
alternately till Payne dropped out ; the other two
went on until they had gained three-quarters of a lap on
the field, and kept leading alternately. A mile from
home the field began to spurt in a vain effort to overtake
the runaways. Taylor led for a lap, and then others
made momentary spurts till Filsell, of Victoria, went to
the front; but it was too late. Farley and Burton were
fighting out the finish between them, 60 yards ahead, and
all the back bunch could do was to compete for the honor
of third place, which Taylor took with his usual jump.
It was a good race, but the time (lOmin. 52 2-ssec.) was
slow.

The League of Wheelmen officials were active
at Sydney Cricket Ground on Saturday night. The
second heat of the YVoolloomooloo Handicap, an amateur
half-mile event, saw the whole 10 starters disqualified for
the rest of the meeting for collusion. This cheerful
thoroughness should have a salutary effect on riders.
“Platelayer” (Kalgoorlie): Paddy King, New-
castle’s Sunday-school pug., who neither drinks, smokes,
nor swears, is now host of the Central Hotel, Boulder.
If Paddy can run a pub. at Boulder City for one calendar
month without using cuss words he will deserve a halo.
Having disposed of Tom Dunn in seven rounds, the godly
one’s forthcoming scrap with Jessop promises a big gate.
* * * *
Mr. Arthur Smyth last Saturday reduced the
world’s amateur 6-mile record to 7min. 12sec. He also
reduced the 10-mile Australian record, formerly held
by W. Martin, from 17min. 69;)sec. to 16min. 63 4-ssec.
Mr. Smyth has been riding a machine specially con-
structed for pace-following, fitted with a pair of Wal-
lace Little Red tyres.*
B. RERAN, the Sydney boy who broke the
world’s half-mile swimming record.
Phc[?] by H. C. Wood, Sydney.
When lightweight champion pug Bob Turner
and crack Sydney welter Blackmore, scaling 9.12 and
10.6 respectively, shaped at the Sydney National S.C.,
last week, the hall held a record crowd (possibly 2000),
jammed into every corner available, and completely
blocking up all exits. Hundreds were refused admission,
and most of these, including the usual hundreds more
who take up the same positions always when a big fight
is on, stood in the street, on the pathway, or sat on the
kerbstone, and patiently waited and watched for bulletins
until the struggle ended. Blackmore had expressed con-
fidence in his ability to win inside three or four rounds,
and betting people laid 2 to 1 on him. Directly the gong
sounded, Turner, full of faith in himself, forced things,
and, before the third round ended, offers to bet even
money he would win were plentiful, and later, odds up to
8 to 1 were available. The struggle was fast, vigorous
and sustained. Blackmore’s hooks were as much in
evidence as ever, but his most dangerous one, that
which has gained every fight for him—the left
for the jaw—found its way barred by a bony
fore-arm fend, rigid and upright, so that it pro-
tected the jaw capitally, and, no matter how hot the
situation or what position his body was in, Turner, cool
as an iceberg always, rarely shifted his crude but very
effective and well thought-out guard. Blackmore hooked
the ribs hard with right and left, often and heavily
enough to settle most ordinary men, but the blows had
no effect on Turner’s fighting ; he still came, and always
with the same amount of ginger. By-and-bye the light-
weight’s left side, from just below the armpit to the
waist, showed red as raw meat and bled in parts ; yet he
kept going, and operated so effectively that people
marvelled at his vitality. Time after time Blackmore
was completely baffled, especially when Turner met his
unusual clinging tactics by punching away with both
hands. Four times, perhaps, Turner slipped through
getting the spilled water in his corner on his shoes, but
he bounced up instanter and into the thick of it as fresh
as at the start, and the close of the 20th round saw him
declared a victor. Blackmore never looked a winner
once.
* « * *
Lightweight champion bruiser, Bob Turner
(himself not yet 21 and a father), has five snowy-
headed younger brothers—all instinctive pugs. With
two small brothers of Australia’s champion welter, George
Johns, they give daily shows in a Pyrmont paddock with
the gloves that the late Otto Cribb' and Snowy Sturgeon
fought their second fight in, and charge the youth of.the
locality, who roll up in strong force, a ha’penny for
admission. The Turner family numbers eight boys
altogether—two older than Boh—and the whole lot, from
the smallest up, are fighters to the core.
* * * «
Fight between heavyweights, colored Tas-
manian Mallee Jackson and Victorian Joe Cullen, at Her
Majesty’s, Kalgoorlie, t’other day, was an earnest bridse.
Cullen, tallow-hued and flabby, showed poorly compared
with the big colored man. This bulldoggy scrap, w ch
only lasted three rounds, provided more fight than -0
rounds of most championship battles. The punishment
and force, and the frequent stirring situations, were
remindful of the Choynski-Joe Goddard four rounds
slaughter at the old Sydney Gymnastic Club 14 years ago.
They mixed furiously without thought of evasion. .In
the second round a right on the jaw dropped Cullen like
a log, and he was down twice again, and stretched his
opponent out once before the three minutes ended. The
last was a hair-raising round ; they rolled into eich other
like demons gone mad. The finish came through Cullen,
dazed and done but spunky as a bull ant, hurling
himself at the enemy and, as he bore in, crossing the
right and dropping his man with a heavy thud. The fall
shook the last shred of strength out of the black and he
remained prone, while the winner was so badly rattled
that his knees sagged and trembled and he had to' be
guided to the dressing-room. It was the most vimful
fight ever seen in the West.
b » * * *
Rapidly-advancing Fort-street (Sydney) repre-
sentative, L. Murray, easily won Australasian schoolboy
swimming supremacy in i. 3 2-ssec. This is faster than
anything same lad had done before, and it is interesting
that in each of his last three 100yds. races he has im-
proved l-ssec. in a week. No schoolboy ever before
travelled so quickly a sprint trip.
“Nat.”: Display of diving at big swimming
carnival Saturday was remarkable—especially Wickham’s.
From a 30ft. high platform one of his headers was as
regular and well controlled as the flight of a huge bird.
Springing out he seemed to poise momentarily and, then
turning with legs rigid and the whole body well set,
struck the water at a beautiful angle with no more noise
or splash than the slash of a sword would occasion. It
was magnificent and could not possibly be excelled.'
Wickham, with such a performance, would easily win the
high diving championship of the world, but can he
repeat it ?
* * * *
In sunlight and sea green they battled for the
half-mile championship of Australasia at Farmer’s Baths,
Rushcutter’s Bay, Sydney. Around the sides stood the
expectant crowd, and the glitter of bright costumes and
garish hats lit up the dark recesses. B. Reran, O. N.
Smith, R. Cavill struggled for N.S.W., W. L. Harris alone
upheld Victoria, while Queensland was represented by
D. Gailey and F. W. Springfield. They struck the water
in a line, and then the three N.S. Walers shot to the front
and swam abreast till Reran forged in front by yards and
behind him trailed Cavill, with Smith following. At
170.vds. the solitary Victorian dropped out, and the
Queenslanders were far in the rear. Cavill made spas-
modic spurts, but Reran cut through the water with a
clean, graceful motion. Cavill floundered, he seemed to
try to tear the water apart and kick himself into the
cavity, while the other went through it like a needle
through silk. His limbs worked with rhythmical
regularity, and at 600yds. he had left the champion
six yards to the bad. Cavill spurted, he struggled,
he tried his “ crawl ” stroke, but he lacked condition,
and ever the other streaked on, increasing the distance
between them till he touched the ropes ten yards in front
of the beaten champion. They shook hands in the water
and then climbed out, and Reran learned that he had
beaten Cavill’s world record by 20 3-ssec. having done the
distance in llmin. 29 4-ssec. Cavill lives in the water,
trains on peanuts and hop beer and only comes out of
the harbor to sleep. Reran, who works at the carpenter’s
bench on the Sobraon, swims twice per day and does the
half mile twice a week and has no peanuts or hop beer.
Judging by the result hop beer and peanuts have a
demoralising effect on champions. Sydney' was lucky on
Saturday ; it saw two world records beaten. The other
was the mile competition bicycle record which was
wrecked by MacFarland at Sydney Cricket Ground in
the evening.
* * * *
“Plunge”: Have gone through Keran’s lap
(50yds.) time in his world record - breaking swim of
Saturday, and find that at 400yds. his 5.3 broke the
previous best (Englishman Jarvis’ 6.13 1-5), that at
500 yards his 6.23 3-5 beat Englishman Nuttall’s 6.24 1-5.
Reran did 600 yards in 7.45 (better than Jarvis’ 8.3), and
occupied 10.28 in covering 800 yards, while the prior
fastest was Jarvis’ 10.58 2-5.
* * * * i
Second Australasian Swimming Championship
carnival, at Newcastle, Monday' night, saw Cavill turn
the tables completely on Reran, who, though he displaced
“ Dick” at 60yds. and led by a yard at 90, lost his posi-
tion 60yds. further on, and, from a yard to the good
then, Cavill had an advantage of 4yds. (and more in
him) at the end. Reran was second, only a yard
in front of his “coach” (Bob Craig), and got out of the
water badly tired. Perhaps Reran is going stale, or did
Cavill’s high-tension pace in the early part break him up?
Time (2.31 1-5) is 1 2-ssec. faster than the previous Aus-
tralasian best (Cavill’s) accomplished last year, but
2 3-ssec. slower than the world’s record (Lane’s) made in
England two years ago.

After the last police raid upon gambling
schools at Broken Hill, and when heading ’em was
unhealthy for a time, the double-headed penny became
of no further special use, and gradually got into
circulation. Most coppers in Broken Hill sooner or later
pass through the local newspaper office, whose publisher
made a collection of the sinful coins. Within five months
he had gathered out of his cash 11 with either two heads
or two tails. It was good proof that the police hadn’t
got a move on any too soon.
• * • •
Geelong was the rendezvous of the Yic. fire-
men’s holiday-making this year. Here, as usual, the
laurel was not always for the swift, nor the pewter trophy
for the strong. Notwithstanding stringent rules, the
brigade with brains and luck gets home in the various
gambols on the track. In the teams’ events, &c., wet
hose are used ; less time is lost through the Geelong
water —which, like the inhabitants of the Pivot, is on the
lingering side —percolating slowly through the hose.
But the brigade that has “pointers” with patience
enough to run a few bucketsfull of soft soap, or a few
bags of shot well-lubricated with graphite through the
hose a few times before starting afield, will gain more
seconds in the run down and the overflow than the com-
bined enthusiasm of all the firemen on the ground would
gain if not accompanied by these aids. Luck and
wrinkles bring joy, and shatter hopes all along the pro-
gramme. Disqualifications for such lapses as running
with a short hose (the cussedness of the average hose,
which shrinks feet at a time with no apparent cause, has
passed into a bve-word in red-coat circles), leaving the
couplings loose, &c., &c., are mere details in the lottery.
It is a lottery, and owing to the malignity of inanimate
objects, the first to the fireplugs are generally last, and
the last are first. Soon—next year, probably —the pic-
turesque red-coats will march ’neath the glare of the
torches no more, for the Fire Brigades Board has decided
the firemen’s dress uniform will henceforth be of blue,
and the crimson jackets are to be reserved for use at
fires.

THOSE ASHES.
[For The Bulletin.]
Warner, when you take “ the ashes”
Down to where Bull’s store of cash is*
Tell him that you’ve got a treasure)
Skite about it without measure ;
Tell him it’s beyond all buying—
For it all the world is dying ;
Tell him how Australia prized it,
How its dearest blood baptized it;
Then the Silken Bonds draw tighter—
Tell him how our hopes are brighter ;
Mention how our crops are growing,
How our barns are overflowing ;
Twos and threes our flocks are bearing
(Be of calves and lambs unsparing)—
Proof all this, please say, of course is
Of our natural resources—
“ Marvellous recuperation ”
“Certainly the coming nation”
(Rub it in, but rub it gently,
Don’t attack him violently);
“Land’s just flowing milk and honey,
But—it’s rather short of money."
(Broach it so, and deftly snare him—
We have done enough to scare him).
Tap him where his store of cash is,
Raise a trifle on the ashes ;
Help us, Warner, Ring of Cricket—
Ours the money, yours the ticket,
Do Australia this good turn :
Pawn the ashes and the urn.
S.H.P.

N.S.W. Government has acted on one
recommendation of the Birth-rate Commission
very quickly, and by a proclamation declared
forfeit at the Customs all articles known to be used
solely for prevention. This is a power which a
State Government has, though the Customs Depart-
ment is administered by the Federal authority.
During the hearing in Rockhampton (Q.)
of the terrible M‘Donald case (in which M‘Donald
and wife were found guilty of the wilful murder,
by deliberate ill-usage and neglect, of M ‘Donald’s
daughter) it came to light that, previous to his
second marriage, the father had never in any way
maltreated or neglected his child. The step-
mother, while her step-daughter was dying, un-
cared-for, by inches, noticed one of her own
children unwell, and sent her off immediately to
Brisbane for medical treatment. No doctor came
near the other, although she was a mass of sores,
suffering from a broken wrist and fractured ribs,
and other injuries. The father announced his
child’s demise to the police thusly : “Sergeant,
God’s will be done ! My dear daughter is dead ! ”
Isn t there a Danish proverb that says: “The
child who gets a step-mother gets a step-father as
well.”

Extract from the speech of the Mayor of
Ballarat city, one Richard Pearse, at the feed
given by the Chinese Bo Reong Society to coun-
cillors and jaypees of the City of Statues: —
When the Chinese became Christianised and inter-
married with Britishers, the fear of war throughout
the world would be groundless, as they would be able to
defy the other nations. God made the Emperor just as
much as he made him (the mayor), and he asked them to
drink his health.
Our Imperial destiny— -a bastard Chow-British
race!
Mr. Deakin, when seeking a new com-
bination, should have turned his thoughts to that
great supporter of enterprising _ vigor, D.C.L.
Whisky, the incorporation of which beverage, in
properly regulated instalments, would impart more
strength and backbone to Ministerialists than any
other ally. As wisdom is the best guide to success,
so is D.C.L. the surest aid to wisdom. D.C.L.,
The Whusky of the Brainy.*
M'Millan’s place as deputy-leader of the
Dry Dog party in the Reps, will be filled by either
Dugald Thomson, of N.S.W., or Glynn, of South
Australia. Glynn was reckoned a Radical at one
time; nearly everybody who is something of a
sportsman (Glynn is a dashing rider after hounds)
can be reckoned a Radical. Certainly, he had
good land-tax views, too ; the name of him —
“Paddy”—probably had something to do with
that. It was partly the Paddy and partly the
land-tax views that turned the Assembly district
of Light against him two or three times. Glynn,
besides speaking with a most beautiful brogue,
rattles along at the rate of 200 words to the
minute, which made him absolutely unintelligible
to the little German farmers who swarm in that
district. All that they could make out was that
he was going to do something with their land,
and his enemies spread the idea that he was going
to take it from them, and down Glynn went every
time. But he’s P. M'Mahon Glynn, B. A., LL.B.,
now, and nobody suspects him of Radicalism in
these later days.
Premier Seddon got into a very perturbed
state of mind recently through a paper publishing
the fact that he and others had gone on a fishing
excursion in a Government steamer on the Saw-
bath. The Wellington Ministers’ Association
gathered up its shiny coat-tails and passed a
resolution a quarter of a column long condemning
the big man in its choicest style for scandalising
the community, and for using the people’s boat to
accomplish his vile design. Seddon defended
himself violently, and cast fish about in all direc-
tions, but there is no doubt that he has trodden
on a pretty large public corn in this matter.
Alec Marks, formerly Japanese Consul in
Melbourne, is now in America engineering a big
deal in brown meat for Bolivia, where the J ap coolie
isata premium. A. Marks is oneof the wealthiest
men in Australia. He and two brothers left
Melbourne about 45 years ago, just after the gold-
fever had subsided. They arrived in Japan with
a small capital and became the first wealthy
foreign firm in the Chrysanthemum Kingdom.
Their first great rise was made in buying up much
Japanese silver coin and shipping it to America.
The Japs, who were then in a barbarous condition,
knew nothing about using alloy in their coinage
and in their primitive way used to put more silver
in their yen than it was nominally worth. The
three brothers soon became merchant bankers,
each brother carrying on business in different
centres. They had their own vessels for trading
between the islands, and on one occasion, whilst
Alexander’s two brothers were on a voyage, a
squall struck their ship and they were drowned.
Alexander inherited all the property. After
making a fortune Mr. Marks returned to Mel-
bourne and married a daughter of Edward Cohen,
who was Commissioner of Customs in J. G.
Francis’ Ministry. His wife died and left him
two children, a daughter, who is married to a
Cohenor at Tam worth,, and a son who married a
daughter of the Chairman of Directors of the
Commercial Banking Company—another Cohenor.

S.A. Supreme Court had a delicate point
to decide recently A lady booked from Port
Adelaide to Fremantle by the Kanowna was
observed, when she got on board on December 19,
to be in a very “interesting” condition, and the
captain refused to take the risk of conveying her.
So she had to lose her passage and stay where she
was till her child was born (which, after all, was
not till February 5th) and then she claimed £99
damages from the A.U.S.N. Co. Verdict against
her, confirmed on appeal ; so that the term
“dangerous illness” stands as a definition of
pregnancy ; and the birth-rate may consider itself
at a discount.
/A backblocks’wife recently celebrated her
golden wedding in circumstances which showed a
genuine Mark Tapley spirit of cheerfulness under
difficulties. The husband was not present, but
the old lady took the head of the table and served
out the refreshments. A neighboring cocky pro-
posed the health of the couple, and the wife
responded, telling the company that the old man,
in his last letter, said he was well and happy, and
wished to be remembered to them all. The old
man was serving a sentence in Goulburn Gaol for
horse-stealing.

“F.C.” : The pestiferous small boy who
can never go a straight message is always causing
mischief. T’other afternoon a vision of loveli-
ness, in a green underskirt and lace frills, was
standing on the lowest step of Melbourne G.P.O.
awaiting someone. She tarried a while, and as
the minutes passed and the appointee didn’t pass,
the biting of her lip and the tapping of her foot
augured a gusty time for him when he did tran-
spire. A youngster happened along swinging
a billy. He belonged to. a neighboring
restaurant, and was going for the cook’s beer. In
returning, instead of using the level street, he
must needs go up and down the Post Office steps
to save the corner. As he descended, another
imp, hastening from the stamp-window, collided
with him, and the pair slithered down right
against the ankles of the waiting damsel. The
stamp boy picked himself up and “got” with-
out stopping to argue points ; but the beer
boy tried to rescue his billy, and in doing
so got under the lady, who sat on him,
weightily. The billy—well, there wasn’t any
billy; it was just a flat piece of tin ; and as much
of the beer as didn’t go down the boy’s neck ran
into the damsel’s shoes and soaked into her nice
green underskirt. The ribald onlookers shrieked,
whereupon the enraged lady took her parasol and,
still sitting on the youngster, lambasted him
enthusiastically for the space of two minutes.
Then he squeezed himself from under the mass of
angry woman and cleared with the ruins of his
billy, while his chastiser stepped on a tram and
rode home, dripping “booze” along the street.
It was a regrettable episode all round, especially
the loss of half a gallon of good liquor, and some
good young man —the one who should have
arrived but didn’t —will have a hard time over it.
