Re Australian Cup. Of 42 races 9 only have
gone to N.S.W. reps. Latest winner (Marmont) is the
aged gelding which gained such distinction over the
jumps last autumn, when he appropriated five events
right off and subsequently annexed V.R.C. G.N. Hurdles,
afterwards the Doutta Galla Hurdles, and, later still,
Y.A.T.O. G.N. Hurdles. Present season saw Marmont
sporting silk a lot in flat races without doing much till
V.A.T.O. meet, few weeks back, when he squelched a
good field in Bond Cup (l£m.), but failed to draw weight,
and Proceedor got the coin. Marmont is by Wellington,
son of Panic, from Lady Gay, by Neckersgat from Fille
de Joie, by Gang Forward from Levity, the imported dam
of Don Juan, whose Melbourne Cup 31 years ago was a
sensational win by reason of the big pile of shekels the
nag’s owners (including whilom King of the Australian
ring, Joe Thompson) landed. Latter stages of this year’s
race were particularly fast. Final mile occupied only
1.42 i, and last 6fur. I.lo}.
• • • •
The more the racing authorities multiply by-
laws and regulations the greater are the facilities offered
for objecting to winners. The last M.L. Racing Confer-
ence ordained that only holders of jockeys’ or apprentices’
licenses should be allowed to ride in races. Whether this
rule was to supersede a previous one relating to ap-
prentices wasn’t stated, and this murkiness is making
trouble. The old rule reads that apprentices mustn’t
ride until they have been apprenticed one year, and then
only with the consent of their employers ; further, that
they can ride in any race, “ except as hereinbefore speci-
fied.” Whether the new rule includes the year limit and
the stipulation about employers and the “ hereinbefore
specified” and is merely an addition to the old one
neither man nor horse can tell. Amid this confusion the
owner of Convoy threw a bombshell at the racing com-
munity sometime after the Wellington Cup by objecting
to Gladsome, the winner of that race and also of the R.O.
Handicap, on the ground that jockey Jones was un-
licensed. As the mare has won quite a number of big
races this season with Jones in the saddle the point
almost amounts to an earthquake. If the owner of
Convoy proves his case the arrears of chaos will be
tremendous.
• • • •
The one time inconsequential Maoriland racer,
Bulawayo, who was tried at pony racing at the end of
December last, and within a week proved himself a Car-
bine among the miniature steeds, was saddled for
Dunedin Cup (sUosovs.), t’other day, and beat a field of
seven with ridiculous ease, putting up 2m. 36 2-ssec. for
the mile and a half, a record for the race. The N.Z. Cup
winner, Canteen, and the flying Gladsome (9st. 81b.)
figured among the starters. Bulawayo, who is a well-
bred nugget, had 6.13 up.
• * * *
This season has been remarkable for the Autumn
failures of small and early three-year-olds that were
“cracks” in the Spring. F.J.A., the little fellow who
ran brilliantly, and appeared to be fully up to w.f.a.
requirements, in the Toorak Handicap and V.R.C. Derby,
is shaping like a second-rater of late. Sweet Nell is now
hardly recognisable “on paper” as the filly who got away
with Caulfield Guineas and Cup. Lord Cardigan, the
Melbourne Cup winner, is a somewhat diminished hero.
Two big colts, Scottish King and Emir, have come on
whilst the others have gone back or stood still, but thus
far they don’t look likely to distinguish themselves in the
Sydney Cup under first-class test weights.
* * * *
Four horses remained in after final acceptances
of V.R.C. St. Leger. And the Age picked two as pro-
bable winners. When a sporting organ has to select half
the field to win a race, it is time it abandoned the effort
to tip winners and tried to tip a dray.
• * * •
Recent V.R.C. meeting was remarkable for the
success of Wallace blood. Seven races went to that
sire’s credit, and through a doubt as to whether he or
Bill of Portland is responsible for the existence of Mar-
tinique, the credit of that filly’s triumph is divided.
Grafton is a good second with 4 winners, then came
Malvolio and Wellington, 2 each. Results place Emir
top of the season’s 3-three-olds. Messrs. J. Wilson, jnr.,
and A. Wynne lead winning wners with two firsts each,
and the most fortunate of the riders was R. Lewis (3),
and W. H. Smith, J. Mason, and Barden (2 wins).
• • • *
Melbourne’s lately deceased sporting solicitor,
Mr. O. D. Halloran, was a mighty punter. Considering
that he was a V.R.C. committeeman for 22 years he ought
to have known better ; but General Report says that
during the last few years he lost to the bookmakers not
less than £40,000. Of course, in these matters the General
is known to be a thoroughly unreliable person with a
tendency to exaggeration. Under the name of C. Denis
the dead sportsman owned such pretty horseflesh as
Bohemian, Gladstone, Dowry, Eugene, Britomarte, and
others.
• • • •
Australia is threatened with an invasion of
Rugby footballers from British Columbia. Judging by
our past showing against Maoriland, we won’t make a
decent breakfast for the Columbians on this side ; but a
match between them and the Maorilanders ought to be a
sight for the gods. The Columbians play amid the snow,
and there have been cases where the ball has cracked
and had hunks kicked out of it through being frozen
hard while the game was in progress. The average
British Columbian is about 6ft. 3in. high, broad in
proportion, and fitted with a kicking apparatus which
puts a mule to the blush. One of the most terrific
contests imaginable would be a match between the
Columbians and the Bailey family of Taranaki district
(Maoriland). The Baileys can put a fifteen of their own
in the field, and the baby of the crowd is sft. lljin. in
his socks, and has been known to pot a goal against the
wind from the half-way mark. To relate the heights and
doings of the rest of the family would be to bring the
veracity of The Bulletin into question.
* * » *
S. A. Football Association, which last season
played on the Adelaide Oval “ for the last time,” has
just made good its threat by arranging all its programme
for the coming winter on outside grounds. “ Gate ” is,
of course, the trouble. Up to about 15 years ago, foot-
ball was not allowed on Adelaide Oval ground, as it was
supposed to damage the turf, and' inter state matches
were played on the now-neglected Kensington Oval (in
S.A. every recreation ground is an “oval,” in both name
and shape). The spread of the electorate system has
created good playing enclosures at Norwood, Port Ade-
aide and (Jnley ; the Exhibition Oval, though central,
is somewhat small for either cricket or football. Figures
of the dispute are as follows : Cricket Association gets,
as landlord, 20 per cent of the gross takings, and for the
new season asks 25 per cent. Footballers will give only
20 per cent, of the net receipts.
• • * $
“ Australis” : The Melbourne street urchin has
evolved a new gamble, or rather a variation of an old one.
It is played enthusiastically and universally. Was
puzzled by the enormous demand of the small boy for
cigarette-cards (the card with a picture of a glaring
female on it enclosed in some packets of cigarettes) until
I saw 10 or 15 kiddies gambling with them in King-street.
The game is simplicity itself; all that is needed is a wall
and a supply of the cards. These are flipped with finger
and thumb up against the wall, the player kneeling on
the kerbstone while pitching. The one whose card falls
nearest to the wall gathers all cards and tosses them up
preferably in the teeth of the wind. All landing picture-
up he gets, whilst the others toss m order of proximity
for the remainder. The rules of pitch-and-toss govern
the game even to the “ barring ” of a bad “ spin.” It is
a sight to make the angels weep to see these boys, some-
times in groups of 20, gambling with feverish earnestness
while the language used surrounds them with a purple
haze. The game is gradually spreading to the State
schools and is rapidly placing a fictitious value on the
daubs which form the gambling medium. The day of the
wax-matchbox top, with which the writer gambled in
boyhood, seems gone for ever, its place being taken by
the more awkward and less easily procurable “ cigarette-
card.”
* * * *
"Mots”: Have recently observed the genus
goffer” in his haunts. Many of him may be seen on
Sunday morning in Albert Park, Melbourne. They run
in all shapes and dimensions, but are mostly either of the
George Reid robustiousness of proportion or of the lath-
like flexibility of Lord Dick Nevill. The short, fat indi-
vidual generally wears check-patterned knickers, which
pan out about one check to the square foot; the tall and
narrow one wears stripes. The fat player stoops down,
obscuring a large portion of the horizon and nearly
bringing on a fit of apoplexy, places a small hall on a
heap of earth, makes several feints at it with a club, and
then hits one mighty stroke, and you hear the square
pattern on his knickers breaking up in all directions. If
the hit is a successful one the ball travels fully five to ten
yards, and the lath-like person (who usually wears eye-
glasses) drops down on his hands and knees, and with
nose close to earth inspects the spot to which it sped. If
the hit is unsuccessful the cows browsing close at hand
start off at a gallop, shocked by the awful language of the
man in the big checks. The players then solemnly pick
up their clubs, coats, camp-stools, sideboard, cruet-stand
and other furniture, and proceed to follow the ball with
the light of a tremendous purpose in their eyes, and the
cow with its tail standing up disappears over the horizon.
• * • *
“Scrip”: Strayed into a sixpenny bar t’other
Saturday afternoon to analyse the contents of a bottle
with a new label, and discovered some working men
arguing about Cornishmen and fight. (The Maoriland
wurkin’ mahn always monopolises the toff bar on pay
days, and spends the money he has kept from his old
woman like a prince.) “ Cornishmen are no d good,”
said an excitable little man with a face like a paralysed
jam jar.” “ What ’bout Bob Fitzsimmons, the fighter ? ”
asked a chap in a big collar which resembled a white boa-
constrictor coiled round a hospital. “He’s the excep-
tion," said the little cove as he planked his flagon on the
bar. “ I know him ; I taught him how to fight, and how
to find the heart in three tries.” The crowd grew
interested. “ I showed him new punches, so I did ;
but, as I was saying, I know St. Stephen’s Church in
Glasgow, I’ve been in Warsaw' and Trinidad, and I hit an
American once for sneezing on the British flag, but
Cornishmen are no d good.” Someone here expostu-
lated, but was urged to silence. “ But I know a fightei
who is going to ring the bell on the world. He is neither
English, Irish, nor Scotch (Cornishmen are no d good),
and he can hit a 3501 b. punch, so he can. I’m teaching
him. He’s a buck nigger, with a heart as big as a sheep
station, and he’ll plane chips off any man living, including
Cornishman Bob Fitzsimmons, but, as I said before,
Cornishmen are no d .” He said no more, as a plunk
on the nose sent him down, and he had to be
carried out. “Did that chap really teach Fitzsimmons
here?” I asked one of the party. “Never saw him in his
life ” was the reply.
* * * *
“ Glonoin ” : Why doesn’t one of the Sydney
boxing clubs get up a heavyweight novice tourney for
coal-heavers and such ? If the big men were encouraged
we might possibly produce a world’s champion again.
More than likely that, within next day or two,
turbanned champion ’rastler of Australia, Buttan Singh,
will be matched, with M.L. top-rung performer, R. J.
Scott, for £lOO aside. Scott wants the struggle at Grey-
mouth (M.L.), St. Patrick’s Day, but it’s hardly likely the
Hindu will agree ; there’s not enough time to get ready.
Seott will find the alien a tough customer—he is strong
as a Pagel and lissom almost as a contortionist.
* * * •
Is two-up fascinating? It attracts Sydney
people so much that they play it all night. There’s a
coffee-stall, with some spare space at the back, and a hole
in the wall for known customers, with right of ingress at
all hours. And in Melbourne, on the Yarra, just below
the end of Rensington-road, a narrow-necked peninsula
gives standing-room to a crowd of 40 to 60 men each
Sunday. They are guarded by four sentinels—one on the
neck of the land and three river-wards.
“D-Population.”
“Filius Crucis, ” who wrote recently on the
subject of “ Depopulation,” writes this time under
a new heading:—
. Which, if not much of an improvement on “ Depopula-
tion,” the title to which you demurred, seems however to
be the idea of most of the countries that have abjured
Rome, for they nearly all rank below their rivals in
birth-rates and proliiicness. The great exception,
Germany, is as regards two-thirds of it, Catholic, and it
is in its latter description of Empire that we find the
most notable increases. I ruefully enough admit that
vou make a strong point when you furnish figures to show
that in death-rates the scapular, etc.-believing-innations
are as inferior as in the two items animadverted on they
are superior, but I think you hardly show your usual
justice when, to swell this hygienic particular, you put
in your list of Protestant and Catholic peoples
Maoriland and Australia, for. with their mere
suggestions of populations, and large areas, they
must necessarily show better as foes to the gentle-
man with the scythe than places like Belgium,
Portugal, and Italy. These latter and their congeners
also have large poverty-stricken populations, so that
mortality must work fearful havoc with them They,
however, all show a marked improvement in life condi-
tions, so, in a comparatively short time, their slaughters
of innocents will be less noticeable, while advents of
fresh cherubs will as surely increase, too. Fervently
Catholic Ireland, it may be noted, has nearly 700 cen-
tenarians in it; England, with nine times its population,
but about 300! In your list of 17 countries the Pro-
testant total seems to be unfairly increased b.v Germany
appearing twice (as Protestant); once it is as Prussia
(births per 100,000, 3612); then it is Germany (births per
100,000, 3596). A duplication of this Empire into Pro-
testant and Catholic segments would give the real thing
all right. For a final word I positively must say I think
your contention that Greek Church countries ought not
to be counted as Catholic ones is a weak one ; the respec-
tive cults are as similar really as Salvarmism is to Con-
venticlism, a doctor to a surgeon, or a paper contributor
to an ass. ' '
To which The Bulletin - replies briefly that
“ Filius Crucis ” is mixing things up. In
showing the birth-rate of Protestant and R.C.
countries, The Bulletin put down Germany
(apart from Prussia) as mostly Protestant, and
with 11 Protestants to six Catholics it is so.
“Filius Crucis” would do much better if he
complained of Switzerland, which has a very bad
birth-rate, being classed with Protestant States,
seeing that it lias a much larger R.C. per-
centage than Germany. As for the attempt
to prove that Catholic countries have a
better birth-rate than Protestant ones by
including Russia, and claiming that the
Greek Church is really R.C., this is rather thin.
The Popes have hurled the brick of excom-
munication too violently at the Greek
Church for that theory to pass muster. At all
events, if the Russians are Roman Catholics, it
would be safer to break the news to them by tele-
phone or anonymous post-card. Russia isn’t
in a good temper just now.
4
“Straggler”: Re Maoriland Health Depart-
ment (“A Woman’s Letter,” B. 11/2/'o4)—if
Ward s address to the Science Congress claimed
any superiority for M.L. methods of food inspec-
tion, he was a bit audacious. M.L. has no syste-
tematic supervision of food supply at all. It
probably has the statutes and the machinery, but, if
so, they work only in spurts and patches. Meat
and butter are in some degree under inspection ;
for two or three of the larger cities exercise fair
control over the slaughtering and sale of meat,
and butter factories are liable to odd visits from
Government inspectors. But the great bulk of
both those products passes from producer to con-
sumer without any supervision at all. The same
may be said of fish, fruit, and bread. The open
shop-front of the butcher, the greengrocer, and the
fishmonger, still offer hospitality to the fugitive
microbe, and uncovered dust-carts help it on its
way. Provisions of various kinds are still exposed
on the sidewalks. The dairyman of Maoriland is
probably somewhat more sanitary than he used to
be, but the tuberculous cow may still contaminate
the whole output of a factory, and the farmer’s
consumptive family may still handle and distribute
milk and butter.
4
“ John Gilpin” discourseth about the declining
birth-rate:
Tiie 15u;,1k tin’s idea of instilling into the average
Australian his duty to the nation as regards population,
by waking in his soul a patriotic or national sentiment,
etc., reads real funny to one. of the common herd. I lent
your ariicle on the above to a woman of ordinary in-
telligence to read, and when I asked her what she
thaught about it I only got for reply a very tired smile.
To make a man a patriot, you must first make him love
his country, and to make a man love his country you
must make the said country lovable. Now Australia at
present is not at all lovable, from my point of view. I
suppose I may consider myself as belonging to the
“common people” who are supposed to trot out the
future generations. Hence my views.
To beget children it is almost necessary to be married
—one ought to be at any rate ; but the present
state of Australia morally forbids such a luxury to
the average toiler, for the simple reason that his earnings
are insufficient to comfortably maintain a wife. (Note
the word comfortably. The average Australian likes to
be comfortable ; indeed comfort and patriotism mostly
go together.) Hence the declining marriage and birth-
rate. Every man would like to be a father, and every
woman a mother, but in the present state of affairs I am
“ not taking any.” Why should I? Am I not pushed
enough as it is to eke out an existence? I consider it
would be criminal for a man in my position (and there
are thousands in the same boat) to attempt to rear
children, and it is a stubborn fact that there are thou-
sands of men and women, married and unmarried, who
look at the question in this light. It is a practicable view
and a sensible one also. As long as Australia is governed
in the interests of the money-lender and Rebecca Bull
and Co., the Australian birth-rate won’t go up worth a
cent, but will decrease from purely natural and other
causes, which will be a sad thing for Australia. This
country may yet want all the Australians it can get.
Tb- re is only one remedy. Australia must wake up
and commence to be Australian. It must find employ-
ment for its people, not by cutting prickly-pear or clea'r-
ing seruh lands, but by a thumping protective tariff,
thereby promoting industries such as iron and steel
works, cotton mills, and others too numerous to mention.
The great drawback in this country is the difficulty in
obtaining constant employment. The man out of work
finds it hard to get in, while the man who is in finds it
mighty hard to stop in. Overcome this state of affairs
and Australia will become full of national sentiment of
the right sort; and the birth-rate will go up in such a
way as to “ stagger humanity.”
Give us employment, constant employment ; and I for
one will marry, become a patriot, and attempt to remedy
the failing birth-rate. Also, Australia must not lose sight
of the facts that there is not a food-stuff used on the face
of the earth that can’t be grown here, and that almost
every metal is found in some part or other of this terri-
tory. The raw material is here ; the labor is here.
Utilise these, and there will then be no need to appoint
commissions whose report, in nine cases out of ten, does
not go within coo-ee of the root of the evil.
4
The natives of some of the Pacific Islands
are very superstitious in connection with their
dead, and resemble the Maoris in this respect.
When a small schooner, named after a defunct
chief, was wrecked at Atiu a while back, the
natives got the cargo ashore and then demolished
what remained of the vessel by fire. Even the
anchor, chains, etc., weren’t retained, but were
taken out and sunk in deep water. Idea was that
as the chief was dead his boat was dead also, and
if they divided' the salvage the spirit of the
departed would curse them for ever, and they’d
be struck by lightning, or all their teeth would
drop out, or something.

It is said that Bewick, Moreing and Co. are to
retire from the management of the Lake View Consols
and Great Boulder Main Beef (W.A.), in the belief that
these mines prospects are practically nil. It was recently
stated in London that the Main Beef’s reserves were nearly
depleted, and that what ore remained was unpayable.
This was promptly denied, but the fact remains that, in
1902, the co. worked at a loss of 2s. a ton, and it is a cer-
tainty that last year’s results were still worse. The Lake
View must be looking very hopeless if 8., M. and Co.
voluntarily resign from the management. The weakness
in the scrip may be ascribed to their secession.
* * * *
It is current report that most of the bosses of the
Great Boulder Perseverance (W.A.) are to be fired out.
Inis is said to be on account of the management having
handed over to the Lake View Consols their tailings for
filling in the stopes, whereupon the Consols people re-
them, and, it is alleged, found they were worth
lzdwt. per ton. If the rumor is true it would be hard on
u y -V n iu ene . r £ e tic shift boss, who kept the mine going
while the nominal boss was seeing the world—supposing
rL nn were included. Anyway, it would be absurd to sack
an underground. boss, who merely superintends the
mining, for a mistake possibly made in another depart-
ment. Bumor has it that Fred. Morgan, one of the
numerous brothers who help to run the Golden Mile, is
to run the show. It is also said that Frank Gardner has
lost control of the mine.
* * • *
B° u^er (W.A.) main shaft is now down
to 1100 ft., and exploring from this level will shortly be
started. The crosscuts at 900 ft. are still proceeding, but
nothing of any value has so far been encountered.
Except as a gambling counter, the scrip is valueless, as
here often stated. The lode worked in the early days
underlaid west into the Associated, and no lode of any
value has been discovered on the eastern side of the
property. The Brown Hill, Brown Hill Extended, and
the Oroya East have spent thousands trying to locate
an eastern lode, but all, so far, without any result. The
country is, apparently, outside the gold belt, and is
never likely to be any good. The co. was last recon-
structed in June, ’O2, with a 2s. 6d. assessment, and there
is still an outstanding liability on the scrip, which may
be called up at any time. There are 85,000 reserve
shares, but they are about worthless, as they can hardly
discount Whi ' e the 80rip is c l uoted afc 80 per cent.

which came from the levy on tributors. The co.’s new

The reason of all the rumors regarding changes
of management in the Associated, Associated Northern,
and Northern Blocks Syndicate (Kaigoorlie) are now
apparent. The offices of all three concerns have been
transferred to 20 Copthall Avenue, the abode of Bewick,
loreing, and Co. As B.M. and Co. rarely, if ever, retain
old officials it is pretty certain that both Gleisberg and
Bobei ts will be relieved before long, and new importa-
tions take their place. The next big grab may be the
Perseverance including probably the Boulder Deep Levels
ana South Kaigurli, which are at present under the same
management.
* * * *
Iyanh° e Junction (W.A.) is putting through a
310-ton ciushing. As is not uncommon with Adelaide
companies, the announcement is immediately followed
by a call of 6d. per share. This, in itself, is enough to
make one sniff at the show. The call comes as a surprise,
as the last call was made only about a month ago. Aus-
tralian shareholders appear to be even more long-suffering l
tha,n their brethren in the Great Smoke when they allow
calls to he made on the eve of what they allege is to be a
payable crushing. Manager Johnson may be a good
miner but results, not reports, are what' shareholders
should insist on ; and, so far, the results have been nil,
and it won t be at all surprising if the present return is
very poor. This Ivanhoe Junction has been running
for years, and shareholders will get a pleasant surprise if
the show now turns up trumps.
Victory United, Cuddingwara (W.A.), has an-
nounced its second crushing as 45 tons for 41oz. 9dwt.
Ihe Murchison press is very sarcastic over the result-
quotes all the optimistic reports issued on the property
and suggests that a meeting of local shareholders be held
to investigate. Anyone who knows the wavs of Adelaide
management will not, or should not, be surprised at the
result. Nearly every Adelaide-controlled company has
issued scores of dubious reports.
* * * »
Great Fin gall, Day Dawn (W.A.), appears to
have got into a poor zone at No. 10 level. Both drives
north and south, have been extended about 200 ft., and
the grade of ore is much below the average. The
north averages lldwt. except for the last 20ft., where
1* , has declined to sdwt. The south drive averages
f ° r the first 100 ft., and only 6Jdwt. for the second
100 ft. So far, the pay chute at this level is thus
less than 300 ft. long, and the grade of ore much
under the usual 25dwt. average so far treated,
ihe first extra 20-head of stamps, which were
expected to start by the middle of February, only
commence work about the middle of March. The further
exploitation of No. 10 level will be watched with interest
if not with anxiety.
* * * *
Hainaujt, Kaigoorlie (W.A.), has succeeded in
placing 10,750 of its reserve shares to Bull shareholders.
profit of ,he Co ‘ for the P ast three months has been
£B5O a month; this is exclusive of London charges
With the 10 head which is to be added to the mill it will
take the co. all its time to earn 10 per cent.
* * * %
During February, Ravenswood (N.Q.) treated
2323 tons for 40050 z., the best since last August The
largest contributor was the Sunset, the boss mina’of the
New Bavenswood, Limited, about the only English co
that has won a div. on the field. An additional 4441
shares have been issued to the shareholders at £5
bringing the capital up to £40,000. It is proposed now
to split these into 200,000 shares of 4s. each. Last year
the company paid 15s. in divs. (75 per cent, on the £1
share) ; but as sales are supposed to have been made at
£lO, those that bought at that price realised only 71 per
cent., which isn’t the same thing. The multiplication of
scrip is obviously to help the market. Although not
registered on Q. ’Changes, shares were quoted once.
Secretary Kift, from London, and general manager
Wilson offered first £lO and then £lO 2s. od. ; but no one
in the room had any to sell—although a lot wished they
had at the price. The property embraces the Sunset,
General Grant, Black Jack, Shelmalier and Manalieur
reefs ; but the backbone is the Sunset, which averages
with concentrates, about 35dvvt., and the sands “are
valued at lOdwt. They raise about 1300 tons a month
The Grant crushes about 30dwt.; but it seems from the
returns that they only have annual crushings of about
250 tons. The whole field is suffering from a big boom
and a bad burst. Grant and Sunset Extended, which
was expected to “ bottom on the Sunset footwall lode in
500 ft. ” {vide prospectus), stopped at 920 ft., and is now
opening out on a reef cut at 505 ft. When it was cut it
was merely a leader “showing the proximity of the main
lode,” but as nothing better was got below, its import-
ance has been enhanced considerably by selling brokers.
The Bavenswood reefs are of high average value, but
their existence at a depth and the persistency of the
chutes have yet to be proved.
* * * *
Tributors at the once famous Burra copper
mine (S.A.), which the co. deserted 30 years ago, have
got into an ore branch from which good returns are
coining, What became of the Burra ore deposit has
always been a mystery. Officially the co. went on the
assumption that it was a big surface deposit, while old
hands hold that it was fed by fissure lodes which ought
to be found. The melancholy mine and town have been
repeatedly threatened with revivals, so S. Australia has
long since left off taking much interest in these announce-
ments ; but the fact that ore is actually being produced
marks this “revival ” as something out of the ordinary,
* * * *
The unlucky Anglo-Australian Exploration Co.,
formed in 1894 and reconstructed in 1897, since when it
had paid no div. up to the end of 1903, broke the mono-
tony then with one of Is. 6d , which left it with only £3OB
to carry forward. And it might have been better if it
had not been able to pay that div., seeing that it was
mostly profit made out of the sale of 11,670 shares in Mt.
Boppy, which is now on the up-grade, wherefore the
A.A.E. Co. is probably sorry it sold. Still, according to
London reports, the Anglo-Australian did not go out of
Mt. Boppy to the extent that was supposed, and still
holds 38,174 shares. The cash (apart from profit) got out
of the sale has been put into an Indian venture.

The Atlas feat of supporting Yulcana on one upraised
hand is curiously recalled by the London Daily Tele-
graph of Jan. 20, which says that Mr. Chamberlain was
accompanied at a big demonstration by the hon. secre-
taries, “ waving small Union Jacks and several ladies.”
A silly little Melbourne publication is responsible for
the statement that Florence Young has developed “con-
sumption of the throat. ” Meanwhile Florence’s beautiful
mezzo voice is ringing out vigorously as usual.
The passing round of the hat to allow some song-bird
to keep its beak supplied with seed has become a mono-
tonous ceremony in Australia lately. A refreshing break
away has been made by Miss Maggie Farrell, of Stawell
(Vic.). She has a voice, and her friends took up a collec-
tion. Now the money is to be divided among charities,
as she has decided not to pursue her studies—she gets
married instead. <
There’s a movement on foot to send another singer
away to Europe. Mr. Kinahan (he’ll have to change that
name into choice Italian or something before he can
expect an engagement) is reported to possess a tenor
that will compare with any in the world, only it’s in the
raw.

“Cockaigne”: From the London Athenaeum I glean
that Charles Wyndham has signed articles for an Aus-
tralian engagement and will be here this year. He is
nearly, or quite, as old as Charles Mathews when that
tombstone came, but Wyndham is much better preserved.
Of course, “ Little Mary” will bloom first. Then come
David Garrick,” “ Rosemary,” the “Squire of Dames,"
Case of Rebellious Susan,” and a whole catalogue of
merry things.
“ P-C-” : The intention of Pope Pius X. to reform the
music sung by R.C. choirs is not a new idea at all. As
far back as 1564 another Pope Pius—the Fourth—formed
a sort of Commission of Cardinals to inquire into a
similar trouble and stamp out the abuses that had crept
into the musical services of the Church. Some musical
cranks of those early days composed Church services of
canons, rounds, and the like, which were simply Chinese
puzzles m a musical way ; others would take a popular
air and use it as the foundation for a whole Mass, and
there is a Mass of that time still extant in which the
s^ou * s “Alleluia, Alleluia,” and nothing but
Al'elllia” from the first bar to the last. The result of
the labors of Pius was a Mass composed by Palestrina
which is considered a model of its kind even at the pre-
sent day. r
Theodore Kremer, who, according to. London Daily
Mail, has now won fame and fortune as a dramatist, was
for some time, he states, a Domain dosser in Sydney,
then a "super” at various Sydney theatres, afterwards a
detective clerk at ’Frisco.
_ r !‘? jeon Brodsky ” : A letter just received from Mr.
William Archer, to whom I wrote for advice concerning
an Australian National Theatre, suggests that the best
way to start is with municipal theatres in Melbourne and
Sydney. He thinks the cash difficulty the least of all
A manager, actors, repertory, and traditions will all be
very hard to obtain.” The repertory will certainly offer
its problems for solution, but it and the traditions can
stand over. The absence of a past in Australia certainly
militates somewhat against the creation of an artistic at-
mosphere (who was it said that there was nothing 1 as unin-
terestmg as the woman and the country without a past?)
but then we shall have the chance of avoiding much
error which is encrusted in a lot of European tradition.
Of the four difficulties mentioned by Mr. Archer, the one
that seems the greatest to me is that of the actors. I
doubt if at the present moment we were to get a theatre
for purely art purposes, whether we could get together a
good enough company. At present the plays produced
m Australia mean nothing, and consequently an actor’s
success is conditioned by his personality, his looks or
something else that does not count as art. The sooner
therefore, we can get a new school of young men and
women, with a broad general knowledge and some real
enthusiasm for art, to train for the stage, and to train
only in good plays, the better. Valuable work can be
done m this connection by amateur clubs;
More than half the country members of
N.S.W. Assembly more or less learned their fate
on Tuesday morning. The new electorate boun-
danes are not absolutely fixed, objectors being
offered 14 days in which to convince the Commis-
sioners that some other plan of distribution
would be better; but serious changes are not
likely. The Newcastle and Maitland group will
lose three members. Some of the old electorates
are carved up among three new ones. Thus
on?, ,P art of Waratah is tacked on to
Wickham, another part on to portions of
East Maitland and Wallsend, and a third
on to Kahibah. Newcastle West is cut
U P the same W; W- Wickham, Kahibah,
and Newcastle East remain intact, with frag-
ments added to them. If the man whose old
electorate is left in this way has any “pull ” this
arrangement will be good for Fegan (Wickham),
Edden (Kahibah), and Dick (Newcastle E.). Either
“Pa” Brunker (East Maitland) or Gillies (West
Maitland) will, it seems, have to be crowded out.
Outside that district Grafton (Premier JSee’s dis-
trict), Armidale, Glen Innes, Tenterfield, and
Ballina are left whole; while Lismore, Tweed
Richmond, Raleigh, Inverell and Uralla Walcha
are sliced up. In the Far West there is slaughter.
Broken Hill loses one member ; Cann’s district is
left; but Ferguson and Williams will have to
fight for a combined Alma and Sturt. Wilcannia,
Condoblin, Cobar, Barwon and Bourke are thrown
mostly into two electorates, with Bourke and
Cobar as centres ; and it takes most of Went-
worth, Hay and The Lachlan to make up another.
These Far West districts now return Cann
Ferguson, Williams, Scobie, Clara, Willis, Davisi
Macdonnel, Byrne, Carroll and Sleath—ll of
them, and six of them must die. In this case, if
the man whose old district is left intact has the
“pull,” the advantage lies with Cann, Scobie,
Macdonnel and Davis.

“ Hist ” sends along a legend
Re references to the effect of Vic. Black Wednesday on
Governor Bowen. He was the most astounded man in
Australia when he discovered the tremendous shock to
the public mind on the publication of the Gazette sack-
ing the civil service. His Ex. had been easily got round
by a promise of an additional vote for furniture, and was
so delighted with the windfall of tables and chairs that
he never gave his signature to the fateful proclamation a
second thought. A year afterwards, when feeling had
cooled down a bit, he threw his arms round the neck of
Gowen Evans, one of the Argus bosses, and begged to be
forgiven. Gowen smole a sickly smile, but said nuffin.
On leaving for England, Bowen made the tearful remark
that he had forfeited the good opinion of the only people
in the State whose respect he valued.
Seems to have been a very weepy Excellency, if
this yarn is based on fact.

“Vulcan”: In noticing Sir Montagu
Gerard’s book, “Diaries of a Soldier and Sports-
man,” London Times gives two extracts, which
show why Britain may look for the loyal support
of “our brown brother” when time of need
comes:
A subaltern gets into a railway carriage, where, to his
disgust, he finds a couple of Hindu gentlemen. He
quietly waits till the train is in motion, and then, as he
expresses it, “ fires them out of the door."
On another occasion, a petty rajah, going on a state
visit to Agra, takes his seat in a first-class compartment,
and is given a great “ send-off” by his subjects. On his
return the said subjects make for the first-class carriage
for him, but find him timidly crawling from a third-class
compartment, when he explains to the waiting crowd
that, on his way down, he was boxed up with a couple of
sahibs, all muddy from snipe-shooting, who made him
shampoo them the whole journey.
The hook is full of similar instances of how the
gentle sahib is encouraging our beloved fellow-
subject to stand by the Empire.

A muffled curse from Bunbury (W.A.) :—
John Forrest is looked upon with reverence by a great
many old Westralian settlers, particularly by those of his
native town, Bunbury, which, some time hereafter, will
no doubt appear in the school geography books as chiefly
noted for being Bigjohn’s birthplace. Some t’othersiders
were visiting Bunbury on one occasion, and soon became
tired of hearing about nothing but the deeds and misdeeds
of the burly knight. “That’s a nice allotment over
there,’’quoth one visitor. “Nice allotment! I should
think it is a nice allotment,” came the reply of the Bun-
burvite. “Why, I’ve played cricket with John Forrest
on the very spot. See the old fence there. I mind
chasing John Forrest—Jack Forrest, we used to call him
then—over that fence. I suppose he lepped four feet if
he lepped an inch.” Said one of the visitors, with bitter
sarcasm : “ I heard it said that once, when John Forrest
was a boy, his hat blew off in this town. I should like to
see the spot.” The old identity considered the matter
seriously : “ I disremember that, but I daresay it was the
time when I chased him. At any rate, 1 know he bust
his pants.”
“A.L.M.H.”: Can anyone tell me the
origin of the expression “ Miserable as a bandi-
coot ? ” I can follow the line of reasoning which
dubs a spiteful female a cat, and can also dimly
comprehend the qualities involved when one
gentleman calls another a cow, but the reason of
the bandicoot being a synonym for misery still
escapes me. He is a more than ordinarily light-
hearted little beast, and prances about at night
with a cheerfulness which at some seasons approxi-
mates almost to giddiness. He enjoys his existence
as much as most of us, and even if his habitation
is a hollow log, or, at most, a shallow burrow, he
is far from being depressed about it.
Dear Bulletin. —Strolling down the prin-
cipal street in , the other night, I happened
upon a ludicrous affair. A stout dwarf of a woman,
whose loose, fat face was the color of a tomato,
was severely belaboring a Chow. She had him
strung by his pigtail around a lamp post, and was
bashing him with the remains of a jew-fish ! After
she had belted him until you couldn’t discern
which was Chow and which was fish, the Wrathy
Wraith released him with a “Come on now, yer
old Chinkie ! ” Tne Chow smiled vaguely and
departed arm-in-arm with her. Then I noticed
that she had a wooden leg. A by-stander told me
thislittle romance: One night she came to the Chow’s
shop and demanded a long beer. He shoved her out,
but she refused to go. At last he weighed out 3d.
worth of pea-nuts and offered them. She was
grateful and came to live with him. But the
yarn spread, and every gutter-snipe jeered at
John and told him to go and get legally married.
John couldn’t stand the popular contumely, and
did so. On this particular night they had done the
Block, and someone had asked him how was
“ his illicit spouse with the wooden leg getting
on.” John turned indignantly. “Memallied—
plopperly mally—Plespertelian Churchee. Blully
P lallakin ! ” Then his wife told him to shut
up and come along, but he disobeyed and turned
and said “Blully lallakin” again. Whereupon
she hit him with the fish.—“X.”

“Flabby Paul”: Our assistant-surveyor
is an advocate of White Australia, and when the
boss told him to go and survey a Chinaman’s farm
he was annoyed I and another were in his
party, and so we proceeded to the farm. Arriving
there, about half-a dozen hungry-looking dogs
rushed at us. Wo scared them off, and the a.-s.
gave one a kick that landed the dog five
yards away. This seemed to relieve his feelings
a little. A Chow came out when he heard the
row and said, “Hulloa, you messer?” The a.-s.
glared, and words failed him for 36i seconds.
Then his 25 years of experience in the Australian
language came into play. “You slant-eyed
yeliow son of a Boxer—you call me that again and
I’ll break your jaw ! ” “ Whaffor you swear like
that—you messer ? ” The a.-s. nearly took a fit.
Placing his theodolite on the ground, he spat on
his hands. The Chow retreated. “ Whaffor, me
no savee—you messer ?” The a.-s. rushed, and
the Chow did “even time” to his hut. We
followed to see the fun, and found the Chow at
bay, with an axe in his hand, the a.-s. dancing
round him. “Come out of that, you useless,
leprous streak of misery. Call me bad names,
would you ! I’ll drag yo.’.r miserable hide-bound
carcase round the hemisphere !” “Whaffor you
fightee me—me no talk longa you—me asken you
—you messer ; messer land for me.” Then the
a.-s. saw his mistake, and calmed down. He was
so much depressed thac he even spoke kindly to
the dog.

A tired fragment of irregular mental
habits—one Charles Anderson—lately set the
Victorian law a considerable problem in social
economics. 0. Anderson certainly didn’t intend
to do anything so harsh ; he was just a shiftless,
weary Port Melbourne loafer, whose children had
been cared for by the State because he was too
fatigued to maintain them. He owed the depart-
ment some £70 on account of the youngsters, and,
being summoned therefor, explained that he
hadn’t the money, wasn’t likely to have it, and
didn’t care whether he ever had it. He further
assureu the court that he had passed many happy
hours in yaol; that he there found restful seclusion
and freedom from worry as to his daily tucker,
and that the court would confer a favor on him by
“retiring” him some more. Being assured by
the police that this was strictly accurate,
and that C. Anderson’s constitution seemed
to require periodical spells of rest, the
court did its best to disoblige him by turning him
loose and cancelling the arrears. It was all it
could do, so C. Anderson, solely by reason of his
weariness, is above the law. An entrenchment of
exhaustion secures him from attack, and he retires
to his fastnesses of sleep. The Victorian law
only allows imprisonment in such cases, and C.
Anderson likes imprisonment, as a sheltered and
horizontal condition in which his food is
brought to him regularly, so he can scoff at the
statutes. If a gaol was also a huge factory where
profitable work was carried on, and C. Anderson,
during his incarceration, had to toil on the full
jump for nine or ten hours a day to earn money
to support his children, he would probably decide
that it was easier to work eight hours a day
outside. Then the law would distinctly “get the
bulge ’’ on C. Anderson. But the simple idea of
making our steel rails in gaol hasn’t reached
Victoria yet.

Lighthouse inspection as practised in
N.S.W. On a recent “tour of inspection” by
the Navigation Department along the N.S.W.
coast the “ picnickers ” arrived off the Heads of a
Northern River at midnight on a Saturday, and
were straightway brought ashore by the local tug
from the Victoria, which remained outside. At
two o’clock Sunday morning they commenced in-
specting the lighthouse and adjacent cottages
with the aid of a small lantern. Aftera “thorough
inspection,” lasting about ten minutes, the party
returned to the yacht.
“ Prooshan ” : Knew an Irish lady who
left her mahn and lived with a sawyer. Four
years later the malm became joined on to another
female whose rightful husband was living with
Leary’s wife. When the Irish lady heard of this
she sent a flying messenger into the township 14
miles away to “bring the sargint out tuwanst.”
When he arrived she said : “You remember me
mahn Tim, Sargint ? Well, he’s livin’ wid another
woman and I want ye to give me a divorce quick-
like —he might be lavin’ her.”
“Burglar Bill”: Bulletin writers on
weird spots never mentioned a haunted cell; per-
haps they never had one on their beat. A N.S.W.
country gaol had a haunted dark cell, and a dark
cell inhabited by a ghost is about the spookiest
place on record. You are locked in without a
light, and the ghost can rush through the keyhole
and biff you on the solar-plexus and dance away
again, while you lash out and hit thick atmosphere
that hasn’t been outside the apartment since the
gaol was built. A sleeping prisoner on two or
three different occasions was dragged off his
mat by the heels and pulled round the cell, and
No. 28, doing dark cells for abusing a warder,
came out with a black eye. One Good Friday
the gaol tough was in serving seven days, when
he started to yell like a regiment of bottle
collectors. When dragged out he informed the
warders and the prison doctor that the Saviour
carrying the cross had walked through the cell
and disappeared. The doctor cancelled the
remainder of the dark cell sentence, and the
tough was placed in the yards. He had twelve
months to do, but during that time he never came
before the visiting magistrate, so anxious was he
not to meet the Redeemer again. The man with
the black eye would never tell what a fight with a
ghost is like.

THE fIEANING OF THE TERM—
“ GOLD=FILLED.”
FEW people correctly understand the meaning of the term “Gold-filled” as
applied to our watch cases. Some seem to regard “ Gold-filled ” as a
form of electro plating—from which it is entirely different. Gold-filled
Watch Cases are intended as a substitute for solid gold, not only in
appearance, but in wear. A Solid Gold Watch Case is valued for its beauty—
for the gold look about it. The beauty and the gold-look are entirely on the
surface. The gold hidden away in the thickness of the case adds nothing
to its beauty, but does add greatly to its cost. To make a gold-filled
watch case an ingot of metal is placed between two ingots of solid 14-carat
gold, and the three are then rolled flat, forming a solid sheet with a thick out-
side of solid gold and a centre of metal. This centre adds strength and lessens
cost, but its presence cannot be detected unless the gold is cut away. It will
be seen that gold-filled has nothing in common with gilding or plating; the
former implying a substantial layer of solid gold, the latter meaning gold
deposited on the base metal article after it is made. Men who cannot afford to
buy a solid gold watch, and men who can afford to, but do not care to run the
risk of losing such a valuable article, are recommended to consider the advan-
tages attached to buying one of these Gold-filled Watches.

THE NURSERY SOAP.
MAGIC.
In ancient days the remarkable curative properties of a medicine which possessed the power to cure
complaints so, seemingly, different in their origin as
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Backache
Sciatica
Indigestion
Anaemia
Blood Disorders
Biliousness
Jaundice
Gravel
Stone
Bladder Troubles
General Debility
Sick Headache
would have been regarded as magical, but Magic is dead and Science reigns supreme. No effect is
now allowed to be attributed to a supernatural cause. We demand in each case a scientific explana-
tion, definite, clear, and comprehensive. In order that it may be fully understood why
Warner’s Safe Cure
sures, as it does, all of the above-mentioned disorders completely and permanently, it must be realised
that each of them is but the manifestation of a diseased or inactive condition of the
KIDNEYS AND LIVER
If these vital organs are working freely and perfectly, none of the disorders in the list given can
afflict us. 1 Ins important fact will be fully realised by reading the following description of the won*
derful functions performed by the kidneys and liver.
The Kidneys filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine every day. In this
quantity of urine are dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains in weight of uric acid
wrL ether i_ W * t k other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce!
When the kidneys are in health, all this solid matter is in solution and is invisible. Directly the
kidneys, through either weakness or disease, become unfit to do their duty properly, a proportion of
the solid matter remains in the blood, becomes actively poisonous and causes us to suffer from uric
disorders such as Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Gravel
Stone, Bladder Troubles, and Bright’s Disease. A simple test to make as to the condition
of the kidneys is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and
let it stand until next morning. If it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust like sediment, or if
particles float about in it, or it is of an unnatural color, the kidneys are not healthy, and no time must
be lost in adopting remedial measures, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some less serious but more
painful illness will result.
The Liver.— ln the liver various substances are actually made from the blood. Two or three
pounds of bile are thus made from the blood every day. The liver takes sugar from the blood
converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood’
gradually, as the latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble into
urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with blood corpuscles which have lived’their
life and are useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from Indigestion
Biliousness, Anaemia, Sick Headache and Blood Disorders. B
health of the liver and of the kidneys is so closely connected that it is almost impossible for
Che kidneys to be affected, and the liver to remain healthy, or vice versa.
nearly thirfcy ye f B since scientific research, directed specially to diseases of the Kidneys
and Liver, was rewarded by the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as
Warners Safe Cure. It was realised, at the outset of the investigation, that it was necessary
to find a curative agent which would act equally upon the kidneys and upon the liver these
organs being so immediately associated in the work of dealing with the body’s waste material and
after many disappointments, the medicine which possessed the required action in the fullest

When Our Spine’s Had Time to Grow!
[For The Bulletin.]
We shall have a different country when our
Spine’s had time to grow,
We shall put a price on manhood when we run a
manly show;
We shall teach our little children that within our
bounding sea
There is room enough and work enough for such a
folk as We ;
We shall realise—l’m hoping—that our life must
be our own,
Not a feeble, poor possession of the land that
lends a loan ;
We shall realise —I’m hoping—we’ve a decent
plot to hoe
If we only buckle to it—when our Spine’s had
time to grow!
We shall have a sturdy nation —when our Spine’s
had time to grow,
Not a race of beery babblers all devoid of grit and
go;
We shall shake the throne of Tyranny and grip
the throat of Wrong,
We shall breathe a manly spirit in a poor and
sapless throng;
We shall run no more contented at our mother’s
apron string,
But, with Freedom’s aspiration, we shall rise on
Freedom’s wing ;
We shall laugh, in proudest scorning, at each
craven mean and low,
In the gallant time a-coming—when our Spine’s
had time to grow !
We shall feel this poor abasement—when our
Spine’s had time to grow;
How we duck at others’ bidding—how we dodge
a real foe.
We shall learn the manly lesson, with a new and
glad surprise,
That there’s little good in limping thro’ a rotten
lane of lies;
That it’s better for a nation and it’s better for a
man
To go marching to the forefront with an honest
rataplan,
Than to skulk behind another when the winds of
battle blow;
We shall truly, truly feel it—when our Spine’s
had time to grow !
We shall have no weary whining—when our
Spine’s had time to grow,
We 11 have done with poor repining—when our
Spine’s had time to grow ;
We shall rise up self-sufficient for the fronting of
the fray,
And the days of poor dependence shall be gone
for good and aye,
And the nations who neglect us as a feeble folk
and pale.
In their circle will receive us with a new and joy-
ful “Hail!”
And the paltry fears that fettered us to bondage
poor and low
.They shall part—and part for ever—when our
SPINE’S had time to grow !
Q.
P.L,
Seddon's Brass.
Arthur Griffith to The Bulletin :— Australia
is being 1 compelled to witness the sickening
spectacle of raging Jingoes like Seddon and his
crew, now that the slump in Jingoism has set in,
howling their protests against the importation of
Chinamen to work the Transvaal mines, when
every sane man knew that it was just for this one
purpose that the war was engineered by the mine
owners and their “ kept” press. As Seddon and
Co. are now pleading that they didn’t know it
was loaded, it may be just as well to show that
they did know, or, at any rate, should have known
all about it. The following are a few of many
public utterances of men in the know, published
before ever Milner forced his war on the Trans-
vaal:—
In his examination before the Industrial Commission,
before the outbreak of the war, Mr. Albu gave the fol-
lnte(estlng evidence upon the wage question
• ,■. native at the present time receives a wage which
is far in excess of the exigencies of his existence. The
native earns between 60s. and 60s. per month ”
the wages' of* KaffirsT’* ““ “ inin * indUStry to r< * ulate
“. a K. reat extent it is, provided that the Government
assists us in bringing labor to this market."
bo much for Kaffir wages, now for white :
Are you of opinion that the wages paid to (white)
miners at the present moment are abnormal ? “ Yes.”
In an article in the Mining World, also prior to
the commencement of the war, appeared the
tallowing pregnant passage :
White wages have not been reduced in the past,
because the Outlanders desired to work together for
salvation, and any attack upon the white
laborers pay would have caused a split in the ranks.
MUST COMEDOWN! COnditions P revai >. WHITE WAGES
While the war was still in progress, and Seddon
organising and despatching contingents,
the Hon. P. Lets, M.L.C., had an article in the
.Nineteenth Century suggesting that
As there is now no Boer Government to prevent it, the
5""“° e .. r ° f Mines should import a few thousand China-
men to take the place of white miners.
About the same time Earl Grey, at a meeting of
the Chartered Company, speaking of South Africa
generally, said :
• T1 ?ey must dismiss from their minds the idea of develop-
ing their mines with white labor. P
J. A. Hobson in his book, “The War in South
Africa Its Causes and Effects,” published during
the nrst year of the war, says :
Put in a concise form, it may be said that this war is
being waged m order to secure for the mines a cheap
adequate supply of labor. This need has been a pressing
and a growing one. The last report of the Chamber of
rUt?nn SS** h l6 f natter thus : “ Under the permanent con-
dition of shortage of supply (of black labor) which has
prevailed, it is clear that under stress of competition the
tendency would have been to gradually increase wages ”

a large, cheap submissive supply of black and whit”

It is no extravagance to argue that the blood and the
money of the people of Great Britain are being spent for
’ that f P reBe "t no other definite tangible
Af fl - ICt Can be shown - The nien who, own-
ing the South African press and political organisations.
engineered the agitation which has issued in this war are
the same men whose pockets will swell with this increase.
Now all of these were widely-published utterances
of admittedly representative men—they were
made and published either before the war broke
out or during its earlier stages, while we were
still despatching oontingeuts—so that the men
who sent these contingents must have known the
object and the obvious result of the war. That
these men should now claim popularity by pro-
testing against the fulfilment of the object for
which the war was waged is only a proof that
they think the electors are fools—and a pretty
fair proportion of them are. Personally I prefer
the attitude of our avowed Colored Labor party*
and I am almost inclined to admire the consistency
of Cakkutheks and his crowd, who gave a solid
party vote a few months ago against my motion
asking the New South Wales Assembly to protest
against the importation of Chinese miners to the
Transvaal. There is some merit in being con-
sistent, even if it be in a cheap labor policy.
The Brummagem Business.
When the Commonwealth Parliament has a spare
evening it might do worse than deal with the
frauds of the shoddy jewellery trade. At pre-
sent an Australian, buying Australian-made
metal ware, can have no official guarantee what he
is paying for; and, as a rule, about nothing else
that he buys does he know less. Not even the
most experienced buyer can tell whether what he
is paying for as 15 carat gold is really that or only
12 carats ; yet the difference in each ounce would
mean about 9s. The less experienced—which in-
cludes most people—cannot even tell whether
what they are buying is solid gold, more or less
alloyed, or only “rolled” gold—copper, most
likely, with a thin coating of gold over it. They
have to wait for 18 months or a couple of years to
find out the difference, unless they happen to
carry the article to a pawnshop meantime. And
naturally the jewellery business, being a traffic in
which fraud is thus so easily possible, is a traffic
to which swindlers pay particular attention. If
for no other reason than that, the public ought to
be protected—by the setting up of a Common-
wealth establishment at which articles of jewellery
might be tested and officially stamped. But there
is another reason. English-made jewellery can
be hall-marked; in fact, plenty of jewellery
made in Europe outside Great Britain, and
intended for sale in England or Australia,
is taken to England to be hall-marked. So
long, therefore, as foreign-made stuff can be
sold with this official guarantee, while no
such guarantee can be given with Australian-
made goods, the sale of the latter is prejudiced ;
and so the Australian Protective tariff fails to do
the work it ought to do. If Australia would give
its own honest manufacturers a chance to get the
same guarantee that the foreigner can get, the
last remnant of excuse for purchasing gold and
silver goods from countries which don’t themselves
produce either gold or silver, while we produce
both, would disappear. That would be one way
of doing the work, only it wouldn’t be doing it
all—it would simply provide manufacturers a
means to issue a State guarantee with their goods,
if and when they wanted it. The wise buyer
would, of course, demand the guarantee with
the goods; but buyers of jewellery are
not always wise, and the jewellery trade
shark would be pretty certain to invent reasons
why the hall-mark wasn’t there. The other way
would be to stamp all gold and silver ware,
giving the manufacturer or the importer no
option in the matter. The more or less sanguine
persons who deck themselves with tawdry
brummagem might feel hurt if their bangles
and brooches and other fixings were branded
h carat, and if they felt sufficiently hurt to leave
the rubbish in the darkest drawer in their homes
the landscape would not suffer in the least;
after a while even the persons themselves might
feel all the better for having left off their silly
little lies and frauds.
A woman on the subject of Australia’s empty
cradle :
Will you give a woman who has thought much about
the empty cradle, and whose own is designedly empty at
present, space to make a few suggestions. Whatever
may be said, it rests with us whether the cradle remains
empty. With those women who can afford every help
and yet refuse to take their share of the burden for their
country’s sake, I have nothing to do. But there are a
great number who cannot afford any outside help, only
bare necessaries, and those amongst them who think,
feel, as I do, perfectly justified in limiting their
families. The burden of several children, and keeping
them and a little home decent, is too great for one.
Why not, as.it has been made a State question, give the
woman a little help ? A band of State-paid mothers-
helps—what good they could do ! It is not only a nurse
just for the month that is needed, but help before, when
a woman is unfit for toil and suffers, and help after, that
she may sometimes have a few hours free from the dear
children who always are so exacting with their mother.
Such would benefit the children most of all. Much
has been written of the blessedness of maternity ; little of
how heavily “the curse of Evb ” falls on those whose
means are limited and uncertain, or surely men would
know why thinking women deliberately refuse to bear
a burden which they feel is beyond their power.
Which, after all, only bears out The Bulletin’s
contention when it published the article headed
“ Depopulation ” (25/2/’O4). It isn’t the question
of bringing children into the world, but of rearing
them up afterwards, which makes the difficulty.
The country with an immense death-rate has also
an immense birth-rate—there is no scruple about
bringing child No. 8 into the world when children
Nos. 1 to 7 are mostly dead and have ceased to
cost anything. After all, Australia has even now
the largest net birth-rate save one—the largest
surplus, in proportion to population, of births
over deaths—in the world, so things might be
worse. When there is more work for the people
—when Australia gets over the Pumpkin Smith
disease and finds work for its citizens in making
the things that it now imports—things will pro-
bably improve. It is impossible to have a large
birth-rate and the Pumpkin Smith leprosy at the
same time.

“Disloyal” England.
* INQO Bill,” a correspondent to the British
Australasian, maintains that disloyalty to “the
Empire ’’ i 3 a charge to be brought against Britain
rather than Australia. After instancing the
British toleration of the annexation of a part of
Papua by Germany, and of French convict settle-
ment in New Caledonia, “ Dingo Bill ” shrewdly
remarks:
The fact of the matter is that, out in Australia, they
are again feeling, what every Australian who visits
London must feel everyday of the week, that the Empire,
which the colonials have believed to exist, must remain a
thing of the future—or of the past—so long as Great
Britain persists in her open disloyalty. Australia is sur-
rounded by mementoes of the past disloyalty of this un-
reliable old country. A few years ago, however, it really
seemed that such blunders might be spoken of in the past
tense.
A good many of us certainly believed so. Those who
did not are now able to point to the swarm of Chinese
just about to descend upon the Transvaal, and say, “ You
fought for this.” They can run a forefinger along the
Alaska Arbitration Award, and say, “You fought for
this." They can point to the newest ally of the Empire—
a colored savage race, with a thin veneer of Western
civilisation—able to grapple with the strongest military
Power in the world ; they can demonstrate the possible
complications arising for the Empire out of such a situa-
tion, and say, “You also fought for this.” The precise
retort that would leave the colonial loyalist with a verbal
advantage does not ocour to me.
Other matters complained of are English press
slanders of Australia, and British contempt for
everything Australian bub its cricket, and this
home-thrust concludes:
If Great Britain were loyal to the Empire the fact
would be apparent in smaller things. The magistrate
who released a dangerous criminal on condition that his
friends despatched him forthwith to ** one of our
colonies;” would be promptly sacked. ... I hope that
when these islands assume their proper position of
insignificance in the scale of Empire, the dominant
colonial races will remember what is due to themselves,
and to the little spot from which they sprung. I feel sure
that no proposition will emanate from South Africa for
planting a Chinese colony in Cornwall, to work the
neglected tin-mines there ; nor are the Australians ever
hkely to seriously advocate the swapping of Ireland to
Germany for that part of New Guinea which was so
disloyally fooled away by their incompetent dominant
partner.

By Rail and Semaphore.
MANAGER GARTH’S REPUTATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Y 7. H. Garth, the new District Traffic Manager
of Romahapa Section, was making a clean and
far-reaching sweep. He had shunted half-a-dozen
drunken station-masters from the Block Signal
zone to paltry country stations, where by no
means could they interfere with train-running.
Some of the non-paying country stations he had
closed up, removing the officers in charge to
sidings, which he converted into stations. If
there was anything in Garth’s “improvements,”
then the Old Man, who had just gone up a step,
ought really to have been dropped ignominiously
out of the service.
During the few weeks Garth had been on the
section he had established a reputation for dis-
covering things. The staff grew scared ; when-
ever it struck anything unusual it immediately
divined that it was a dark trick of Garth’s to find
out something. So the staff went warily, and
when it found sixpence over in its cash it
promptly made fourteen entries relating thereto
and reported the matter at great length and with
considerable promptitude.
Manager Garth was a stark, ruthless man. He
towered up head and shoulders over even Fluter,
the Traffic Clerk, who was in appearance reminis-
cent of a telegraph-pole. But with his great
height he combined girth, and when Garth
stalked down the platform or across the shunting
yard, Wagpoleof the Goods, who was classically
inclined, said he reminded him of Satan in “Para-
dise Lost.”
He started to shuffle the staff round as though
he were playing cards, and Knutsford suddenly
found himself torn out of his warm nest in favor
of Goods Agent Laurence, and sent to work hard
in the cool shade of Traffic Cierk Fluter.
* * * *
Mongataranui was a lonely little place just out-
side Puketapu Junction, and the only houses in
sight were distant homesteads. Why there had
.ever been a station-master there no one could
understand. Garth closed it the first week of his
reign.
Knutsford swung himself off No. 16, and
drank in the scented gale, for all round the station
were giant hawthorn trees and great sycamores.
1 lie beautiful hawthorns were all in blossom, red
and white alternately, and they ran away along
the main road till the double line dwindled into
one and was lost round the shoulder of the hill.
KnuisfonL had come out to remove the ticket-
cabinets, which had by some oversight been left
behind when the station was closed up. He un-
locked the door and entered the pokey little
office. It had a deserted, chilly look. TheS.M.
fiad only been away a week, and already every
corner was thick with cobwebs. With true British
instinct Knutsford first examined the till, to see if
any half crowns had been overlooked in the
abandonment; but naked emptiness stared him
m the face.
He unlocked the ticket-cabinets, and, in open-
ing them, dislocated a whole system of spider
webs. One huge, fatherly old' insect dropped
down within an inch of Knutsford’s nose to inves-
tigate, and, seeing it was a detested man, began
to ascend again with all speed.
ff° to Hades!” said Knutsford, hitting out
wi dly with his list of tickets, and knocking the
spider into the cabinet, where it disappeared into
a crack. Knutsford whittled down his penholder
and jammed it into the crack, and probed every-
,toP bnin£ fell out of the cabinet with a
1 tie cloud of dust. The spider had disappeared,
ut a little ptircel of dirty paper was jammed into
ttie space. As the sheets fluttered awav one by

Knutsford snorted. So Garth was testing his
honesty, was he ? Well, he’d fall in. He made a
little parcel of the notes and put them in his
pocket, ihen he tied up the two cabinets, had a
fina! look round the office, and locked-up just as

mores, till presently Bradley, went through the
carriages to collect Terawhiti tickets. Knutsford
cautiously lifted the trap-door of the right-hand
clog-box. An ominous growl made him let it down
with a bump. The left hand one was, however,
empty He put a mail-bag under his knees, and
carefully felt the sides and top for a cranny in
which to stow the notes. There was none, and,
as the guard might return at any moment, Knuts-
the package in the corner on the floor
of the dog-box and went back to the window seat,
intending to take it out of the box sometime in
the afternoon before the van again left Romahapa.
it there was any stern inquiry for the money in
the interval Mr. Knutsford intended to indig-
nantly demand an instant search of his person.
it was six p.m. before Knutsford got a chance
to go to the van, and by that time it was at
the platform on No. 31 South, and being rapidly
tilled up with parcels and baggage. The S M
the most perfect gentleman on the Maoriland
railways, was on duty, and somehow the van
never seemed to be empty for a moment.
Knutsford stepped inside. Guard Millar was
sorting his parcel invoices into the respective
station pigeon-holes. Knutsford got into the
window seat nearest the dog-box.
‘‘Where you off to?” asked the Guard.
1 m going out as far a'' Ballantyre 1 ” replied
the treasure seeker.
Well, get into one of the carriages—the S M
won t let anyone leave here in the van. You can
come in again at Ranelagh, if you like.”
~ y° u arc, ’’ .said Knutsford, getting on to
the platform just in time to meet two porters
navigating a savage bull-dog with window hooks
in his collar.
“Where’s he going to, Meredith?” he asked
the porter.
