Mil ar before lie gets there,” said the porter.
• it '«. n I they put that savage brute into the box
m which the money lay, a „d Knutsford decided
not to go to Ballantyre that afternoon. Popa-
tunoa was the terminus, 60 miles away, and it was
obviously necessary to wait till that dog had left,
even if the Crown of Great Britain and Ireland
was in the box.
* » * * -
When the train arrived at Popatunoa at 9.15,
Jimmy the Rake pompously informed Guard
Millar that he’d take any cur out of a dog-box,
and he wasn’t going to keep the van waiting all
night till the owner of the flaming dog finished
drinking. Then he lifted the inside trap door,
grabbed the chain, and started to haul the dog
into the van. The brute came out with a rush,
and Guard Millar took a flying loip from the van
just as the dog bit Jimmy the Hike for the third
time.
After this little excitement the lad porter
arrived to sweep out the van. He removed the
traces of Jimmy’s acquaintance with the dog, and
propped up the trap-door while he swept out the
box. The second dive of the broom brought out
the paper parcel. «
After the lad porter had opened that parcel and
found it to contain 22 Colonial Bank of Maoriland
.£1 notes, he sat on top of the dog-box and thought
hard for 20 minutes, and finally decided that it
was a trick of Manager Garth’s to test his honesty.
Having arrived at that decision he carefully tied
them up again, and placed them in the most in-
accessible corner of the dog-box, with a mental
reservation that if they came back again from
Romahapa they’d stay in Popatunoa.
Next morning Guard Millar, of No. 4 North,
Popatunoa to Romahapa, was rather astonished
to be boarded at a small siding, 20 miles from
Romahapa, by Mr. Knutsford and a bicycle.
After discussing the weather Guard Millar dis-
coursed fluently on the tremendous traffic in dogs.
He ascribed it to the rush of shepherds to the
North Island, because the squatters were all ex-
tending their runs. “I’ve got two blessed dogs
in each box this very morning, all going to Roma-
hapa.”
Mr. Knutsford cursed and then apologised, and
said it was sudden cramp in the right calf.
Millar went off to issue tickets to some passengers
from the last siding.
* * • •
When he got back Knutsford had disappeared,
although his bike was still in the van. Millar was
scared ; he rushed out on to the tail-board and
strained his eyes down the track to see if the
young officer had fallen out.
As he stepped back into the van an angry
inquiry—“ Are you there, Millar?”—made the
guard jump. He gazed stupidly round the van,
for the voice sounded muffled and far off, and
wondered if he was dreaming.
A volley of oaths and another frantic inquiry :
“Are you there, Millar ? Damn you ! why don’t
you answer ? ”
“Where the blazes are you, anyhow?” said
Millar as soon as he could speak.
“In the blanky dog-box ! Lemme out.”
“Well, I’m blowed!” said the guard : “Which
dog-box ? ”
This one, you fool!—the right-hand one.”
“ Well, if my van isn’t good enough to travel
in I’d try a first-class carriage, and not the dog-
box,” said Millar as lie cautiously opened the
trap-door.
Two collies shot out, and then Knutsford
dragged himself into view, and the collies were
recaptured and shoved in by main force.
“ What's the game, anyhow?” asked Millar.
Knutsford told him, and Millar promptly
demanded half the loot.
“ But I haven’t got it—it’s gone.”
“You were a fool not to tell me last night, and
we’d have had it all safe by this time; now Jimmy
the Rake or his lad porter’s got it.”
* * * *
As the train drew up at Romahapa platform the
S.M. stepped on board, and was horrified at the
dirty state of the van. He also complained that
the presence of dogs was distinctly in evidence.
Ten minutes later Sweeper Macguire received
orders to clean out F 203, and protested to the
S.M. about having to sweep out Popatunoa vans.
The S.M. was decisive.
“You go and obey orders—and pay particular
attention to those dog-boxes—they are very malo-
dorous.”
Sweeper Macguire worked on the dog-boxes so
viciously that he knocked the head off his broom
in one of them, and, much to his disgust, had to
get three parts of his body inside to recover it.
He found the broom-head against the wall of the
box, where a little brown parcel was concealed
under traces of dog. He pulled it out, and, when
he got into the sweeter atmosphere of the van,
cautiously opened one end of it.
He gasped; then a cunning smile overspread
his features. He understood now why the S. M.
laid stress on the dog-box. That officer meant to
bowl him out in an act of flagrant d'shonesty.
He fastened up the money and started for the
S.M.’s office. On the way doubt fell upon him,
and he slipped back to the van and again dis-
appeared, all but his legs from the knees down-
ward. As he emerged he made a careful note of
the number of the van. This time the notes were
in a crack in the dog-box roof.
* * • *
It was Guard Millar’s day off, and Jimmy the
Rake sat in the window seat of F 203. M'Kenzie,
the fisherman, had got in at Mertondale with 12
trout, and with many hiccoughs presented Jimmy
with a pot-bellied one. The other one to which
Jimmy had already helped himself reposed in
the right-hand dog-box. M'Kenzie was, he
reckoned, too drunk to notice one trout missing in
eleven.
They stopped at the platform with a jerk ;
M'Kenzie came staggering down to the van.
“ Whar’s ma fush, Jimmy ? ”
“Here you are, Mr. M'Kenzie,” said Jimmy
with all the affability he could muster.
“Thank ye, Jimmy.”
Then he counted them—one, two, three, and so
on to ten. He counted them twice, and then
staggered up to the van door and called out :
“Jimmy, you swindlin’ thief; bring oot ma
other fush. D’ye think A’m a fule ; bring oot ma
troot. A had 12 troots, an’ a gave ye a pot-
bellied one, an’ that’s one for ye and 11 for me ;
an’ a hae but 10 troots here the noo; ye son o’ a
border cattle lifter.”
Jimmy’s luck was right out. The S.M., who
was passing, turned, and, worse than all, the giant
figure of Manager Garth stalked down the plat-
form, the personification of dignity; it s wonder-
ful what a lot of dignity attaches to a corporation
if it is carried properly.
The S.M. turned to inquire the cause of the
trouble; and M'Kenzie, >ow thoroughly angrv,
told the story. The S.M. turned on t e guard
and asked what he meant by taking gifts from
passengers. The Manager came up and listened
attentively. Jimmy was sent to the guards’ room
to aurait the result of a search. They looked in
the racks, among the parcels, in the drawers and
the pigeon-holes ; but could find no trace of the
missing and the Manager decided that
M'Kenzie -must have made a mistake. That
sportsman was sitting on the top of the dog-box
blinking at them as they searched. When they
told him he had made a mistake he asked them
why they didn’t look into the box he was sitting
on. &
The manager smelt the box; then poked his
heaa out of the van and hailed a lad porter passing
wRh a broom and bucket. _
I want you to see if there’s a trout in either of
these dog-boxes.”
The youth got down on all-fours and searched one
box and declared it empty. Then he dived into
the other and immediately started to back out.
There was a sound of a sudden tearing of clothes
as the back of his coat caught in something, and
then he exclaimed, “This feels like it! ”
As he backed out into the light the Manager
grabbed at a parcel that rested between his
shoulders, and the S.M. took the paper parcel
frorn his hand and displayed the trout.
The Manager opened the parcel he had grabbed,
and whistled softly.
_** * *
Ten minutes later Manager Garth was back in
his office. Fluter, the Traffic Clerk, was out.
Mr. Knutsford answered the bell.
“ Knutsford, I’ve found out something ! ”
“That’s nothing new, sir !” said Knutsford.
Manager Garth smiled; he liked the reputation
he had acquired.
“But this, Mr. Knutsford, is unique. Fifteen
years ago I was clerk at Mongataranui—there
was a big trade there in those days; just as much
as I could keep pace with. One day the bank
cash, £22, all in Colonial Bank notes, mysteri-
ously disappeared. The S.M. said I must have
taken it. I hadn’t, and I promptly accused him.
He kept me in the office and telegraphed here. A
Relieving Officer came out, and my luggage was
searched, and I was sent in here next train. The
train after me brought the S.M., the Relieving
Officer taking charge. As the cash was in my
care I had to pay it. That S.M. was run over in
Romahapa the day he came in—which was
another reason why they made me pay it. To-
day I had occasion to search Guard Poulter’s van,
and I found a trout he had stolen from a pas-
senger; and, strangest of all, 22 Colonial Bank
one-pound notes, very old, very faded, and corres-
ponding in date to those lost at Mongataranui.
Will you please take steps to have Guard Poulter
arrested for stealing one trout and 22 Colonial
Bank notes. ”
Mr. Knutsford hesitated, and then he explained
briefly and to the point.
Manager Garth leaned back and looked inter-
ested. “These are my notes all right, and I’m
going to annex them—in fact, they are annexed,”
he said as ho stuffed them into his pocket. “ Per-
haps we’d better only disrate Jimmy the Rake,
after all. Now I’m going to find out the details
from the time you put ’em in the dog-box till they
came out on that porter’s back.”
And he did, with great tact and diplomacy ;
and his mana was great from Whangarei to the
Bluff.

The Pessimist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I met him at the Anchor Inn,
The hero of my tale ;
While ’midst the smoke and vulgar din
Flowed fast the dubious ale.
There with a motley crowd, I saw
Him seated, well at ease,
A short clay pipe was in his jaw,
A pint pot on his knees.
His face was seared with many a line,
And o’er his features free
The rude trade-marks of wind and brine
Were stamped indelibly.
I longed to learn by heart what lay
Beneath that visage grim,
And in an unassuming way
Some questions put to him.
He took between his finger-tips
His pipe as black as coke ;
He drew one hand across his lips,
Then waved aside the smoke.
“ Yes, mate,” he said, “ I’m sailin’ still
In them old sailin’ craft ;
Of wind-bags I ’ave ’ad my fill—
Square-rigged an’ fore-’n’-aft!
“ Tried steam ? Not me ! I sticks to these
Old battlers. They’ave bore
Me over ’Orstralasian seas
This forty year or more.
“ Yer steamboats —bah ! I tell yer they
’As been this country’s curse ;
For who’ll deny since they’ve ’eld sway
That things is ten times worse ?
“ In those old days, when we was free
Good wages to demand,
They wanted us—they wanted me !
By men their ships were manned !
“ Sailors ! I tell yer they’re not fit
To pole an old mud-barge !
Yet everywhere they flourish its
An A.B.’s good discharge !
“ And every blarsted country lout
Must be a sailor bold.
They ship ’em, too, and chuck us out—
Real sailors, in the cold !
“ I mind me well the days gone by
(Glad days—shipmates o’ mine !)
While men was scarce an’ wages ’igh
For work we did not pine !
“ I’ve left an old Black Di’mond ship—
(They’ve gone now, one by one)
And on a Yankee took a trip,
For fifty pound the run !
“ Yes ! they ’ave gone behind the scenes,
And o’er Time’s edge have rolled—
And in their place are damned machines
With fair winds in the hold ! ”
I ventui-ed then the meek remark,
“What ship did last you know ? ”
He said : “ A little Hobart barque
I left, three weeks ago.
“They built their own ships there, about
The time when things did hum ;
And on the Derwent turned ’em out,
Good stringy-bark an’ gum !
“ And e’en to-day the Tassies feel
The pangs of Used-to-be —
They’ve crushed—these giant craft of steel —
Their one lone industry.
“ Ah ! mate, this bloomin’ country’s done ! ”
(A round, colossal tear
A-down his wizened cheek did run
And dropped into his beer.)
And then with providential tact
As he did pipe his eye,
I drew attention to the fact
His pot was nearly dry.
“Yes, mate ! I don’t mind if I do;
I’ll ’ave the same—ah, well !
This blessed country through an’ through
Is straightway bound for ’ell ! ”
He took the pint-pot in his fist
And wished me lots of cheer ;
And so I left my pessimist
Communing with his beer.
TAS.
WALTER J. SNADDEN.
The Great Writer
[FOR THE BULLETIN.] .
It was Simpson of the Reflector, who sits in
his chair all day reading those silly proofs of
theirs, smiling absurdly to himself when he comes
across what he thinks is a good thing, and who
tells you that you can't read one of Carruthers’
speeches without recognising a great statesman—
you know whom I mean. Well, he first told me
the great Bollingsby tradition.
Discovered quite by accident, it seems. Only
an ordinary, respectable man at first, out Auburn
way somewhere. He happened to write a letter
of remonstrance to the paper re drains or some-
thing. Just about four paragraphs or so, but the
Ruskinesque English of it so struck the editor that
he wrote back off-hand begging B. to accept a
responsible position on the staff.
And from that day—-well, lie’s Bollingsby. That’s
enough ! To think such a man as that stood near
to being wasted on a bank-managership or some-
thing of that kind !
Why, journalism is the life’s breath of him.
Look at his memory ! Gives you day and year for
everything—all out of Iris head !
Has his own vast indexed collection of statistics
—ali put together by himself—half a lifetime’s
work, filling the shelves of a large room, and any-
thing under the sun can be looked up in a minute
or so.
Some said he kept half-a-dozen separate tables in
his study, each with its little library and each de-
voted exclusively to a particular subject, and that
classification and method were the trumps of
Bollingsby’s hand.
Others, again, scorned the idea that he was a
man of the sort that could be made or helped by
the everyday devices of the ordinary hack.
A University scholar, they‘said^ aca pa b] e linguist
and of vast readiner, he d little or no u&e ior tne
cyclopaedia, the dictionary, the thesaurus, and
such things—he carried all his data in his head by
virtue of his superb memory, and was a walking
chronological table of all ‘things-the literary
machine par excellence. You just wound him up,
set him to any theme, and forthwith, with no
trouble on his part, there arose a pile of neatly
written MSS. of first quality, at the rate of about
2000 words an hour.
Some said he did most of his work (like Hall
Came) on sheets of white paper, sitting up in bed
i?!rc«ri eei i 4 in the morning, throwing le
MSS. about the floor as he wrote-leaving it to
the servants to come and arrange the mental pro-
duct in bales in the morning
Others said he invariably did it all on 6 ne co s-
cap with a quill pen, m the train, with an ink-

the moral beauty of the home life was how
gossip put it. It, above all things, made your
mouth water to,know the man.
Bollingsby, in the beautiful villa that they said
he lived in at Malvern, surrounded by his wife
and family—the latter understood to be all sizable
daughters, and handsome—Bollingsby unbending
himself jn this charming home circle, singing in a
rich baritone in the good old English glees around
the drawing-room piano-what a picture !-some-
thing to east in the teeth of those who are always
on hand with a sneer at the respectability of
journalism.

SMILAX.
The Victorian temperance people, having
made a bargain with the liquor party some years
ago that hotels should be closed by local option
with compensation, now want to go back on their
deal. They came to Premier Bent the other day,
and asked that in future hotels should be closed
without compensation. One reverend member of
the deputation got very angry when Bent re-
minded him of the bargain. He declared hotly
that the temperance party had been misled. A
lot of decayed matter was also got off about the
barmaid, and a request was made that she should
be abolished, say by instalments spread over five
years. Nothing was said about what the abolished
one was to do for her crust.

“ T.D.”: The most painful of all stage spectacles is
that of thte understudy who has played a leading 1 part for
a week or two during the star’s iilness, but has had to
shift back to the position of walking lady or gentleman.
This happens more frequently in comic opera than any-
where else. After her meteoric burst, the chorus damsel
is assertive for a night or two in order to let the people
in front know that she is ready to take over the prin-
cipal’s job as a permanency, in preference to the high-
salaried noodle now in charge, if only the audience will
rise up and demand that she be promoted to her rightful
..nosh,ion ; but she probably gets reprimanded by the
stage manager for getting out of her position among the
rank and file, or for “overdressing” her part, or some-
thing. Then she crowds herself away into a corner and
sulks, and that is the end o i the meteor, for thq
management ceases to take any notice of her, and as a
rule she quits the show within a month or two, and plays
principal boy in a country-touring company which has a
mournful habit of forgetting “ the ghost.” The male
understudy who has achieved temporary greatness just
stands and glowers at his “study,” saving his opinions
for open expression in his favorite bar later on. -
The most dreaded day of the year for the super-aesthetic'
manager of a Melbourne musical conservatorium is the
one on which competitions are held for the annual
scholarships. This event came off a week ago, and the
Proi is still walking about with the crushed and confused
air of one who has seen hell in a nightmare. The aspirants
for singing honors were many, and their voices were
various, but the worst thing about them was that the
majority came along with music-hall ditties about
Doreen ’ and "similar nice girls, whilst a couple thought
they had ascended to classical regions when they pro-
duced “Under the Deodars,” from the “Country Girl.”
But the piano students were even worse. One of them
thumped out the “ Hiawatha "■melody, and another, in
short frock and a clean white pinny, played “The
Maiden’s Prayer,” beloved of our piano-learning grand-
mothers. Also, her ma was present, and insisted on
pointing out the beauties of that delectable composition
to an anguished professor.
“Nym.”: A train-ride in Melbourne is a feast of flesh
nowadays. When men are wearing l overcoats and cussing
the .cold sou’-wester, dozens of women board the tram
dummies wearing blouses cut as low as their low-cut cor-
sets, the rest of their exhibition forms being covered with
an airy cobweb of lace—back and front. But Annette
Kellerman’s diving antics at the aquarium are the nearest,
and most modest, display of the nude Melbourne will ever
get without Mrs. Grundy's gamp being raised in vehement
protest. Annette is a shapely lass, girlish to fascination,
plump and nimble—but not the best swimmer I have
seen. For outline, gymnastic ability, and attraction
generally she is unapproachable; and the innocent
manner in which she threads her way through the crowd,
hanging by their eyebrows to the corky scenery round
her splashing pond, is refreshing—seeing the, scarcity of
her apparel. Puzzled my head, during Annette's exhi-
bition of “ form,” how she gets in and out of her skin-
tight, dark blue garb.
Frank Lincoln, the monologist who can mix a “cock-
tail” with his mouth, is just now serving as a curtain-
raiser at a London theatre. That is to'say; he gives a
half-hour entertainment before the comedy commences.
The golden age is only just beginning for variety artists.
They,getdigger salaries than they earned at the’alls a
few years ago, when prices were deemed extravagantly
high, and now the theatres are competing with the ’alls
for the services of the brightest artists.
A curious libel case was settled in England t’other day.
Plaintiff was a beauty actress who had "had her portrait
taken showing her beautiful teeth prominently. An
enterprising dentist saw the picture in a magazine or on
a po-t card, and, not knowing that it represented any one
in particular, used it as an advertisement. Some of the
front teeth were “ blacked out,” and the picture in that
state showed a fright of a face, Then with the teeth
restored it displayed the srpiling triumph of dentistry.
Original sued for libel, claiming that the pictures alleged
that her teeth were false, which they were not. Defen-
dant didn’t go into court but paid damages and destroyed
the advertisements, so the plaintiff didn’t have her mouth
inspected by twelve strange men on the jury.
When ‘ the much-travelled Smythe was lately in Tas-
mania, on his way to Maoriland to pilot Presbyterian
Parson Ewen on his Scotch lecture tour through those
islands, he struck Hobart just as Sister Mary Paul (Madame
Christian), whom he “discovered” 35 years ago, was
there for her health, broken down by much teaching of
young Melbas, Castleses and Crossleys. And talking of
Ewen : He was minister at Burwood (Sydney), some ten
years ago, when the town clerk killed the mayor and
himself, and the bodies had to be removed from the Town
Hall by the inspector of nuisances. Ewen preached a
dramatic sermon on the subject. That old sermon might
come in handy now as a popular lecture, “ Tragedies I
Have Known.”
Bland Holt, who ha* recently acquired a dog with a
large jaw, is following in the track of other mumniers.
Yarmouth used to do Broadway with a bull-dog
—though Yarmouth’s claim to be a mummer rested
mainly on the dog. Henry Lee’s Dane, which died last
year and was buried with an advertising tombstone above
it, paid its meat-bill by stage appearances. And Johnny
Sheridan used to drag a pup about with him a good deal
—Galled it the Shaughraun. Holt’s new animal requires
a man on a bicycle to give it exercise when it’s not wanted
at the photographer’s, or in the pony-cart with Bland
and Mrs. Bland.
“A. 8." : Geelong (Vic.) municipal theatre is not
proving the success anticipated; in fact, it is a frost.
It has not been used by a dramatic co. since the opening
night last October. The Geelongese are in a state of
panic at the result of their enterprise. They imagined
that the possession of a theatre called “His Majesty’s”
would make Sleepy Hollow a live town. Instead of
relieving the rates, the spec, does not pay interest on the
borrowed £SOOO. His Majesty’s is shunned by the few
visiting companies which -have the courage to go that
way. It is too expensive for them.
“L. 8.”: It is said that Henry Arthur Jo- es’s latest
play is the best he has done. “ Joseph . .dangled ”
seems to be a finer bit of work than “The Liars,” to
which it has some slight resemblance, or relation. In
“The Liars” the people are actually lying, and having
an uncomfortable time at the pumps keeping the water
under. In “Joseph Entangled” they are not lying, but
telling the precise truth, whioh no one will believe. The
members of the family are absent from their town house,
but the wife has to come down to town on urgent busi-
ness. Only the butler and housekeeper are on the
premises. The housekeeper attends to her mistress, who
goes to bed. Then enters a friend of the family, who at
one time had an attachment with the wife. He has lost
his luggage, and cannot very well put up at an hotel. The
butler, who was once his own servant, reckons the best
thing for him is to stay the night at the house. The
friend of family does so, and next morning he and the
wife, much to their surprise, mee't at breakfast. There'
are explanations, and even some love-making. They
agree to depart to their respective destinations at once.
But luck is against them, and people inopportunely turn
up. The wife and the friend explain the peculiar posi-
tion, and, of course, are politely disbelieved. Something
happens in the end to prove that the prospective respon-
dent and co-respondent were as innocent as new-born
babies or freshly-spawned alligators ; but there is a long
and complicated struggle before truth prevails, and it
prevails with great difficulty.
